Introduction to FinTech

Posted on March 6, 2020March 15, 2020

This course is from edX, scroll down & click “Read More” for more informations.

About this course

Over the past decade emerging technologies, paired with massive changes in regulations, have driven an unprecedented transformation of finance around the world. This process is happening more rapidly in China and Asia than anywhere else. This course is designed to explore FinTech fundamentals and help make sense of this wave of change as it happens.

New players such as start-ups and technology firms are challenging traditional players in finance, bringing democratization, inclusion and disruption. Companies engaged in social media, e-commerce, and telecommunications, as well as, companies and start-ups with large customer data pools, creative energies, and technical capacities, have brought competition to the existing financial infrastructure and are remaking the industry.

These transformations have not only created challenges but also unprecedented opportunities, building synergies with new business and regulatory models, particularly in emerging markets and developing countries. To meet these changes, 21st-century professionals and students must be equipped with up-to-date knowledge of the industry and its incredible evolution. This course – designed by HKU with the support of SuperCharger and the Centre for Finance, Technology and Education – is designed to enable learners with the necessary tools to understand the complex interaction of finance, technology and regulation.

In this course, through a series of video lectures, case studies, and assessments you will explore the major areas of FinTech including, beginning with What is FinTech before turning to Money, Payment and Emerging Technologies, Digital Finance and Alternative Finance, FinTech Regulation and RegTech, Data and Security, and the Future of Data Driven Finance, as well as, the core technologies driving FinTech including Blockchain, AI and Big Data. These will set the stage for understanding the FinTech landscape and ecosystem and grappling with the potential direction of future change.

What you’ll learn

Skip What you’ll learn

  • The major areas in FinTech, includingMoney and Payment,Digital Finance and Alternative Finance
  • Major technological trends, including cryptocurrencies, Blockchain, AI and Big Data
  • FinTech Regulation and RegTech
  • The fundamental role of Data and Security in data-driven finance
  • Businessand regulatory implications of technology for the financial industry
  • How regulations and RegTech are applied
  • Ways to analyse and evaluate what is driving technology innovation in Finance
  • Hownew technology impacts economies, markets, companies,and individuals

Welcome and Course Administration

Welcome to Introduction of FinTech

  1. FinTech involves the use of technology,
  2. particularly information technology
  3. to transform the way that finance is being done
  4. in global markets, developing countries,
  5. and across start-ups and tech firms.
  6. 20 years ago, IBM spent $100 million
  7. to build Deep Blue, the super computer
  8. that beat Garry Kasparov.
  9. This smartphone is more powerful than Deep Blue.
  10. If you want to be in finance today,
  11. you need to understand technology.
  12. RegTech, short for regulatory technology
  13. may be a game changer
  14. as it will allow financial institutions
  15. to deal with compliance and regulatory burdens,
  16. not only more effectively,
  17. but also more efficiently.
  18. FinTech flourishes where the need is greatest.
  19. China leads the world in many regards.
  20. YueBao is a money market fund enabled by FinTech in China.
  21. In 9 months, it became the fourth largest in the world.
  22. Now 3 years later, it is the world’s largest.
  23. FinTech has evolved over 3 eras,
  24. from infrastructure to banks to start-ups,
  25. entrepreneurs today are building the B2B solution
  26. that will be powering the financial system tomorrow.
  27. This course will be an illustration
  28. of how this is happening today.
  29. In the future, finance will be
  30. about an experience, not a product.
  31. That’s why it’s important for students today
  32. to understand how the industry is changing
  33. and that’s what this course will be about.

Course Outline and Syllabus

Introduction to FinTech Course  –  Course Outline

Introduction to FinTech is a six-week, six-module course. Each weekly module compiles 8-12 lesson units (or subsections). In addition to the main units of the lesson, there are also Industry Showcases highlighting examples and experiences. These include segments from the traditional financial services industry, startups, technology firms, and more.

The major learning activities within each lesson unit include: video discussions of major aspects, as well as continuous assessments in the form of Quick Check questions, Polling, and Word Cloud activities. In addition, there are a range of additional resources, including reports, studies, and useful links. There is a Conclusion Quiz at the end of each module to draw out the main messages.

Module 1 Learning Objectives

Module 1 introduces the evolution, context, opportunities, and challenges of FinTech. It highlights to students that FinTech should be defined by the user of technology as opposed to simply the fact of using technology (the ‘who’, not the ‘what’) so as to better understand why this industry is growing ever more rapidly and what opportunities and risks it creates.

Key Learning Objectives:

  • Understand how finance and technology have evolved and are transforming finance around the world.
  • Discuss key interactions between finance and technology over time to better understand the developments which are taking place today and are likely to take place in the future.
  • Consider the broad spectrum of the financial sector and the way that technology is changing it faster than ever before, particularly with the explosion of new entrants, including startups, tech firms and emerging markets.
  • Consider both the opportunities as well as the potential risks of FinTech and the challenges it poses for policymakers.

1.2 FinTech Transformation

  1. Hi there, very excited to be here.
  2. My name is Henri Aslanian
  3. and my passion and focus is the future
  4. of the financial services industry.
  5. And I’m very lucky to be able to
  6. do this in my academic life
  7. as an Adjunct Associate Professor
  8. here at the University of Hong Kong,
  9. where I teach the first
  10. FinTech university course in Asia.
  11. Also very lucky to be
  12. doing this in my professional life
  13. as the FinTech and RegTech leader
  14. here for Hong Kong for PwC.
  15. Very excited to be sharing about
  16. the latest developments
  17. going on in the broader FinTech space
  18. throughout this course.
  19. One question often pops up is that
  20. how come FinTech became such a big reality?
  21. What really caused the rise of FinTech?
  22. Well it’s a very interesting question.
  23. Traditionally… actually, as technology evolved
  24. banks were pretty good at also
  25. always keeping up with technology
  26. and in many cases,
  27. being some of the early adopters.
  28. But all of this really changed
  29. during the global financial crisis in 2008.
  30. During that time,
  31. banks and financial institutions
  32. were busy dealing with regulations, compliance,
  33. and other many regulatory enforcement situations
  34. that were taking place.
  35. Innovation became a very, very distant priority.
  36. But at the same time,
  37. some of the biggest game changing innovations
  38. that took place
  39. that have changed our lives took place.
  40. Think about Uber, WhatsApp,
  41. WeChat, or Airbnb,
  42. and many others that really changed the way
  43. customers ahead experience the services
  44. they were receiving in many industries,
  45. except financial services,
  46. and this gap was created
  47. between what financial institutions
  48. were offering to their customers
  49. and what customers came to expect.
  50. And this gap is
  51. what the FinTech industry wanted to tackle.
  52. And the FinTech industry wanted to tackle
  53. very many of the use cases;
  54. that matched the pain points
  55. that were in this industry.
  56. However, it was not only the startups.
  57. And actually what we realised
  58. over the course of the years is actually
  59. some of the biggest gamechanging
  60. technological innovations took place
  61. not only by FinTech startups
  62. but by large technology firms.
  63. Think about firms like
  64. Amazon, or Tencent, or Ant Financial
  65. and many others who have started now
  66. looking at financial services.
  67. And in the future
  68. there’s actually a big chance that
  69. some of the providers of financial services,
  70. at least the interface
  71. that clients will be using
  72. may be these large technology firms.
  73. So definitely an area to watch.
  74. And what are some of the advantages
  75. these large technology firms have
  76. over some of these startups?
  77. Not only do they have the funding,
  78. they have the backing,
  79. and the talent, and the pool, capital pool
  80. to actually support these new businesses,
  81. but to certain extent,
  82. they have the trust of consumers.
  83. Think about it,
  84. if you’re happy to buy
  85. all your daily necessities
  86. on Amazon, or on Taobao,
  87. wouldn’t you use them as well
  88. to actually, you know,
  89. buy insurance products?
  90. If you’re actually using the Facebook Messenger
  91. to actually talk to your friends and family
  92. wouldn’t you use them as well to send money
  93. to friends and family as well?
  94. Well, as you can see,
  95. it’s going to be a very interesting ecosystem
  96. developing over the coming years
  97. not only with FinTech,
  98. but also by TechFin.
  99. Definitely an area to watch.

1.3 FinTech Evolution 1.0: Infrastructure

  1. Finance and technology
  2. have been inextricably intertwined
  3. since the very beginning.
  4. If we look at the earliest days of finance
  5. dating back thousands of years ago,
  6. the initial impact of technology
  7. was in the context of building systems
  8. of keeping records of government finances
  9. or payments for taxes
  10. and agricultural production
  11. or building facilities
  12. and one of the first physical technologies
  13. to develop was money
  14. the simple coin
  15. or thousands of years later,
  16. not long after 1000 A.D.,
  17. paper money.
  18. Money is a form of technology
  19. that allows us to physically handle
  20. the ideas embedded in finance.
  21. So, if we think about financial technology,
  22. the history is very, very long indeed
  23. and in addition to coins,
  24. paper money, systems of writing
  25. or accounting,
  26. major points include the evolution
  27. of the joint stock company
  28. or the corporation,
  29. forms of financing like banking,
  30. or more recently, stocks, bonds,
  31. things that we would call
  32. securities or derivatives.
  33. Derivatives are financial instruments
  34. whose value derives
  35. from some underlying financial instrument
  36. but if we think of today’s fintech,
  37. the modern period of fintech
  38. and financial technology
  39. begins around 150 years ago
  40. and we generally mark the date as 1867.
  41. Why 1867?
  42. 1867 was the date of the establishment
  43. of the first transatlantic telegraph cable.
  44. That transatlantic telegraph cable
  45. made instantaneous communication
  46. between the major markets
  47. of New York and London,
  48. or London and Paris,
  49. or even eventually several decades later,
  50. Shanghai, or Hong Hong, and London possible.
  51. This was the basic infrastructure
  52. that underlies all of today’s
  53. not only financial technology
  54. but much of today’s communications
  55. and media developments as well
  56. and it’s somewhat ironic
  57. that in the past five years
  58. more undersea cable has been laid
  59. than in the entire previous 150 years combined.
  60. Why?
  61. Because more physical cables are necessary
  62. to handle the ever greater flows of data
  63. speeding around the world
  64. and underpinning
  65. the development of finance.
  66. Many would say
  67. that the period from the 1860s
  68. up to the start of the First World War
  69. was a first period of
  70. financial and economic globalisation
  71. much like the past 30 years or so from today.
  72. That period of
  73. financial and economic globalisation
  74. was underpinned by technological infrastructure
  75. like the transatlantic telegraph cable
  76. which in fact has been called
  77. the Victorian internet.
  78. Now, if we think about
  79. technological developments
  80. in financial services,
  81. probably our next significant milestone
  82. really comes during the Second War World.
  83. During the Second World War,
  84. significant effort spent particularly
  85. in the context of developing codes
  86. for secure communications
  87. particularly of military and intelligence operations
  88. as well as, significant efforts
  89. to develop systems to break those codes
  90. and it was this process of encoding
  91. and breaking codes that led
  92. to come of the groundbreaking thinking
  93. in computer technology
  94. and in fact,
  95. eventually in artificial intelligence or AI
  96. which is one of the most exciting developments
  97. happening in financial technology today.
  98. But it wasn’t until the period
  99. after the Second War World
  100. as the world economy is rebuilding
  101. that we begin to see progressive development
  102. of those early computer technologies
  103. to eventually lay the ground work
  104. for the sorts of fintech
  105. and regtech developments
  106. which we see today.

1.4 FinTech Evolution 2.0: Banks

  1. The first era of modern fintech,
  2. fintech 1.0 was about
  3. building the underlying infrastructure
  4. that supports today’s global financial markets.
  5. The second major era
  6. of the modern fintech evolution,
  7. what we call fintech 2.0,
  8. started in 1967.
  9. 1967 marks two very important dates
  10. in the evolution of finance
  11. and also technology.
  12. The first, is the establishment of the first ATM,
  13. the first automated teller machine
  14. by Barclays Bank in the UK.
  15. That automated teller machine allowed,
  16. over the next several decades,
  17. a transformation in the relationship
  18. that people had with money and with finance.
  19. The second, just as significant,
  20. is the launch of the first handheld calculator
  21. by Texas Instruments.
  22. The first handheld calculator
  23. was transformational in the way
  24. that finance, on a day-to-day basis operated
  25. And, of course, the handheld calculator
  26. is also the ancestor of today’s smartphone,
  27. perhaps the transformative technology
  28. in the context of fintech.
  29. 1967 thus marks a period where we begin
  30. to see a process of digitization.
  31. Digitization is taking processes and systems,
  32. which were formerly analogue,
  33. analogue things like handwriting
  34. or physical calculation of money,
  35. and digitising them, transforming them
  36. into a digital environment.
  37. And from these early beginnings,
  38. we can see in global financial markets,
  39. three very significant trends
  40. that come together
  41. in today’s global financial markets.
  42. The first, the late 1960s and early 1970s,
  43. the establishment of a series of
  44. domestic and international
  45. electronic payment systems.
  46. These payment systems allow
  47. large value payments
  48. to take place today on a real time basis,
  49. which underpins massive volumes
  50. of transactions around the world.
  51. From across border standpoint,
  52. perhaps the most significant of these
  53. is an organisation,
  54. which we’ll turn to in module two,
  55. called SWIFT.
  56. SWIFT is an organisation,
  57. which provides protocols
  58. to enable communications
  59. between domestic digital payment systems.
  60. In addition to these developments in payments,
  61. we also see, in 1971,
  62. the establishment of NASDAQ.
  63. NASDAQ was the world’s 1st electronic stock exchange.
  64. Today, there are almost no stock exchanges
  65. or other financial exchanges,
  66. which are not electronic.
  67. Basically, in the context of both payments,
  68. as well as, in stock and other securities markets,
  69. the process of digitization,
  70. which began in the late ’60s and early 1970s,
  71. has fundamentally transformed
  72. the way these markets work,
  73. so much so that today, it is very difficult
  74. to find a human being actually trading securities
  75. with another human being in stock markets.
  76. At another level, if we think of
  77. today’s global foreign exchange markets,
  78. markets in which people buy one currency,
  79. like the dollar and sell another,
  80. like the yen or the RMB,
  81. these markets today
  82. do approximately 5.5 trillion dollars
  83. a day, every day of the year in transactions
  84. and almost none of this
  85. takes place in the form of cash.
  86. It is almost entirely digital book entries
  87. taking place between the computer systems
  88. of major financial institutions.
  89. This process, however,
  90. is one that was in fact
  91. well established by the late 1980s.
  92. A major market crash occurred in 1987.
  93. That market crash today,
  94. our best explanation,
  95. is what is called programme trading.
  96. Programme trading involves preset computerised
  97. buy and sell orders,
  98. and so when stock prices
  99. drop to a certain level,
  100. this would trigger automatic selling
  101. by computer programmes,
  102. which would then trigger more price drops,
  103. triggering more sales and eventually resulting
  104. in our first major coordinated
  105. global market crash.
  106. Now, across the 1980s,
  107. we began with the origins of online banking
  108. in parallel to the emergence of the internet
  109. and certainly by
  110. the beginning of the 21st century,
  111. a number of banks already had
  112. over a million plus online banking customers,
  113. but today, we are waiting to see
  114. what will be the first financial institution
  115. with over a billion online financial services customers.
  116. If we think in terms of developments,
  117. we can think that 1999 marked a previous high.
  118. 1999 was the internet bubble,
  119. the dotcom crash
  120. and it highlights something
  121. that’s very important
  122. in the context of today’s fintech.
  123. As human beings,
  124. we tend to overestimate the short term impact
  125. of new technologies
  126. and underestimate the long term impact
  127. of new technologies.
  128. After all, if we think to
  129. some of the 1999 internet bubble companies,
  130. companies like Amazon or Google.
  131. Certainly, at that time,
  132. we overestimated their short term impact,
  133. but looking back almost 20 years,
  134. almost no one would have imagined
  135. how significant
  136. Google, Amazon, and other similar sorts
  137. of technology companies have become.
  138. This period of building the digitization
  139. of the financial system
  140. hit a major turning point
  141. in 2008 with the global financial crisis.

1.5 FinTech Evolution 3.0 & 3.5: Startups & Emerging Markets

  1. Fintech 3.0 and 3.5.
  2. 2008 marks a major turning point
  3. in the evolution of fintech.
  4. The global financial crisis of 2008
  5. started in the subprime real estate markets of the United States
  6. and spread throughout the global financial system
  7. to major developed markets around the world,
  8. as well as impacting developing
  9. and emerging market countries
  10. through the economic slowdown
  11. that from the standpoint of trade,
  12. was just as severe as the slowdown
  13. that we saw in the 1930s
  14. in the aftermath of the 1929 crisis.
  15. From the standpoint of fintech,
  16. the global financial crisis had a number of
  17. very important features.
  18. The first, was that it resulted in large numbers
  19. of job losses in the financial sector,
  20. particularly amongst younger people.
  21. This drove many of those people
  22. who had been looking forward
  23. to very good careers
  24. in the context of financial sector
  25. to seek to pursue other opportunities.
  26. And it’s certainly the case today
  27. that if one meets someone
  28. who has just left a large financial institution,
  29. it is very frequently the case
  30. that they have gone to
  31. start a new fintech startup company.
  32. So, first impact of the global financial crisis,
  33. job losses
  34. forcing people in the financial sector
  35. to look for new opportunities.
  36. The second was that
  37. a reaction to the global financial crisis
  38. involved large numbers of
  39. regulatory changes designed to prohibit
  40. or prevent the sorts of problems
  41. that emerged in 2008
  42. from happening again.
  43. These massive amounts of regulatory changes
  44. resulted in a dramatic decrease in profitability
  45. of financial institutions
  46. as well as massive increases
  47. in compliance and regulatory cost
  48. for those institutions.
  49. In many ways, the only way
  50. that these new requirements
  51. could be addressed was via technology.
  52. The third impact of the global financial crisis
  53. really was to speed up something,
  54. which was already happening,
  55. which was a loss or a drop in trust
  56. in traditional financial institutions.
  57. Since 2008,
  58. certainly amongst people under 35,
  59. it is now generally recognised
  60. that they would be more comfortable
  61. doing financial transactions
  62. with a technology company,
  63. whether Facebook or Alibaba
  64. or Tencent or Google
  65. than with a traditional financial institution
  66. like HSBC, Barclays or Bank of America.
  67. These features together
  68. along with one other important point
  69. and that important point
  70. is marked by 2007.
  71. 2007 was the launch of the first iPhone.
  72. The iPhone, of course,
  73. has now sold over a billion
  74. around the world and the iPhone
  75. and other forms of smartphones
  76. combined with other factors resulting from
  77. the global financial crisis set the stage
  78. for the transformation in fintech,
  79. which we’re seeing today.
  80. Now, if we think about this combination,
  81. the combination has led to
  82. an explosion in startups.
  83. One very high profile startup
  84. is perhaps one of the most unique
  85. and one that we’ll turn to in module two,
  86. Bitcoin and other forms of cryptocurrency
  87. based on blockchain
  88. or distributed ledger technologies.
  89. Others have involved various forms
  90. of alternative finance like P2P lending, peer-to-peer lending,
  91. or forms of crowdfunding,
  92. a range of different payments.
  93. But in fact,
  94. startups in the context of fintech
  95. are even not new.
  96. If we think back to 1999,
  97. one of those internet bubble startups
  98. was a company called PayPal.
  99. PayPal today is one of the world’s
  100. most significant payment services providers
  101. along with traditional firms
  102. like Visa or MasterCard
  103. or even before that, Bloomberg.
  104. Bloomberg today
  105. is the world’s most valuable
  106. private information services firm.
  107. Bloomberg founded by
  108. the former mayor of New York City,
  109. Michael Bloomberg, started in 1981.
  110. Bloomberg started in technology
  111. in an investment bank called Salomon Brothers.
  112. The technology that he developed
  113. was a secure communication service
  114. that still underlies the vast majority
  115. of cross border transactions around the world.
  116. So, this period of fintech 3.0,
  117. the explosion in startups
  118. since the global financial crisis is new
  119. in terms of numbers, volume, transformative potential
  120. but we have seen fintech startups before.
  121. The other area where the transformation
  122. has been perhaps even more dramatic
  123. is in the context of developing countries
  124. and emerging markets.
  125. 2007 marks the launch of M-Pesa.
  126. M-Pesa is a mobile phone-based payment system,
  127. which transformed access to finance
  128. of a large portion of the population of Kenya
  129. and it’s when we combine these ideas
  130. of mobile payment with the smartphone
  131. that we see the transformation
  132. of financial services
  133. that is taking place particularly in China
  134. with Alibaba, Tencent and others.

Industry Showcase: Collaboration between Financial Institutions and Startups (The FinTech Association of Hong Kong)

  1. Collaboration plays a very important role
  2. in the way FinTech has developed in the last few years,
  3. in particular, in Hong Kong.
  4. Initially, we saw a lot of work done by FinTechs
  5. trying to do it on themselves,
  6. looking to disrupt financial services
  7. and also the banks at one stage,
  8. looking to be more guarded
  9. about how they use technology,
  10. especially FinTechs with them.
  11. But as the reality dawned on the markets,
  12. such as Hong Kong,
  13. which is largely business-to-business,
  14. people realised that
  15. there needs to be a lot of collaboration.
  16. Banks need a longer time for transformation,
  17. but they also need to be able to be more agile
  18. with regards to adopting new technologies,
  19. and for FinTechs,
  20. they don’t have a long runway,
  21. and secondly, they also aren’t able
  22. to have the required capital
  23. if they want to face off with banks,
  24. in particular,
  25. where larger creations are impacted.
  26. So, it’s quite important that
  27. collaboration is done in this field.
  28. Secondly, what we are seeing now is
  29. banks are not just collaborating with FinTechs,
  30. but they’re going a step ahead
  31. and some of them are acquiring them
  32. through strategic investments.
  33. So, they’ll have a large investment,
  34. and these FinTechs are solving
  35. just the banks’ need for strategy
  36. in Hong Kong or across the region.
  37. Three, very importantly,
  38. is cross-regional collaboration.
  39. So, you see certain strands of different FinTechs
  40. in different regions,
  41. and what banks are trying to do
  42. is try to find these FinTechs here.
  43. If you have a FinTech which is very strong
  44. in the B2B section of AI in Hong Kong,
  45. they look to use that from Hong Kong,
  46. and they might use another
  47. coming in from Singapore.
  48. So, it becomes very important for them
  49. to have teams that can collaborate this.
  50. Lastly, it is very important
  51. that compliance plays a role
  52. in making this effort of collaboration
  53. between banks and FinTechs faster.
  54. Onboarding is a very important factor
  55. and of compliance,
  56. time can be shortened.
  57. It gives the start-ups
  58. more time to present a POC
  59. and be onboarded with the banks.
  60. So, collaboration plays an important role,
  61. and we should be aware that
  62. banking is going to change
  63. not by disruption,
  64. but through collaboration.

1.6 FinTech Typology

  1. As we’ve seen,
  2. the evolution of fintech has impacted
  3. every aspect of finance everywhere in the world
  4. and I think
  5. from the standpoint of looking at fintech,
  6. it makes sense to look at
  7. the main areas where fintech is focused on
  8. in the literature, as well as in business.
  9. And those can be summarised
  10. as finance and investment,
  11. operations and risk management,
  12. payments and infrastructure,
  13. data security and monetization,
  14. and technology in the customer interface.
  15. And in each of these areas,
  16. technology has both created new areas,
  17. as well as transformed
  18. existing businesses and processes.
  19. If we think of finance and investment,
  20. often we’ll think of forms of alternative financing
  21. like crowdfunding or P2P lending,
  22. or most recently, ICOs.
  23. ICOs are initial coin offerings
  24. and are a topic that we’ll turn back to in module 3
  25. but as we saw in discussing the evolution,
  26. technology has not only
  27. created new forms of financing
  28. but via digitization,
  29. it has also transformed
  30. traditional forms of financing.
  31. Think of stock market trading
  32. and the fact that today,
  33. in the world’s high frequency algorithmic stock markets,
  34. that hundreds of thousands of transactions take place
  35. every single second
  36. of every day that the markets are open.
  37. And this simply cannot happen without technology.
  38. And of course,
  39. areas like hedge funds and investment funds
  40. are some of the biggest spenders on technology,
  41. a constant arms race to gain a slight edge
  42. in trading and investment
  43. in order to drive profitability.
  44. So, fintech has transformed
  45. both new forms of finance
  46. but it has also changed
  47. traditional forms of finance.
  48. If we look at operations
  49. and risk management, pre-crisis,
  50. much of operations and risk management
  51. in the financial services industry
  52. focused on quantitative risk modelling techniques
  53. such as VAR or value at risk systems,
  54. which depended upon sophisticated computer systems
  55. developed across the 1990s
  56. but in many ways,
  57. the 2008 crisis showed the limitations
  58. of many of these approaches.
  59. Since 2008, financial institutions,
  60. as well as regulators,
  61. have continued to be amongst the largest spenders
  62. of any industry in the world on IT,
  63. focusing in particular,
  64. on new regulatory requirements,
  65. as well as risk management systems
  66. but also competition from new entrants
  67. whether those new entrants are startups,
  68. or they are established technology,
  69. media, or communications firms.
  70. Payments and infrastructure is a very high profile area.
  71. For many of us, electronic payments
  72. will be one of our primary areas
  73. of direct interaction with fintech.
  74. Most of us have made payments
  75. over the internet,
  76. or using our mobile phone,
  77. or using a QR code;
  78. simple techniques,
  79. which allow instantaneous payments
  80. both in electronic form,
  81. as well as increasingly using a variety of forms
  82. of mobile and smart technology.
  83. But in addition to this,
  84. these 5.5 trillion dollar a day
  85. global currency markets take place
  86. in a completely digitised environment
  87. and new technologies like blockchain,
  88. or distributed ledger technology,
  89. which we’ll turn to in module 2,
  90. as well as in module 6,
  91. are transforming
  92. traditional large value digital payments.
  93. All of this digitization
  94. brings with it both opportunities,
  95. as well as risks.
  96. On the opportunity side,
  97. digitization of ever increasing sources
  98. and forms of data
  99. has allowed a new process of datafication.
  100. Datafication is the application of
  101. sophisticated analytics systems,
  102. big data, or artificial intelligence and the like,
  103. in order to analyse large volumes of data
  104. to come up with new approaches or new ways
  105. to monetize those sources of data
  106. but at the same time,
  107. with data increasingly in digital-only form,
  108. risks of security continue to increase.
  109. If everything is digitised,
  110. the risk of hacking,
  111. the risks of cybersecurity
  112. have moved from being a business risk
  113. to a financial stability risk
  114. to a national security risk today.
  115. And these are areas
  116. that are driving both technology,
  117. as well as new entrants, new businesses,
  118. new opportunities, and of course,
  119. the customer interface.
  120. How we interact with these forms of finance
  121. provides not only
  122. greater opportunities for individuals
  123. in terms of convenience and access
  124. but also, new business technologies.

1.7 Emerging Economics: Opportunities and Challenges

  1. As we’ve seen,
  2. technology over the past decade
  3. has transformed finance around the world.
  4. It brings with it massive opportunities,
  5. and those opportunities
  6. are perhaps nowhere greater than
  7. in developing countries and emerging markets.
  8. If we think of the story of Africa,
  9. of M-Pesa In Kenya,
  10. and mobile financial services,
  11. the impact of mobile financial services
  12. through old-style mobile phones
  13. has been very significant.
  14. But, there’s a big limitation
  15. in terms of what you can do
  16. with an old-style mobile phone,
  17. compared to what can be done
  18. with a modern smartphone.
  19. The power of a modern smartphone
  20. dwarfs the power
  21. of a room-sized IBM mainframe from the 1970s.
  22. The computing power that
  23. we can hold in our hands today
  24. is unprecedented in the history of the world,
  25. and from the standpoint of
  26. what one can do in finance
  27. with that technology,
  28. the transformation which is
  29. already taking place in Asia
  30. is nothing short of amazing.
  31. Why?
  32. Because throughout much of Asia,
  33. we have two very significant features.
  34. One, very often, high penetration rates,
  35. high ownership rates of smartphones,
  36. combined with availability of broadband internet access.
  37. This combination of smartphones
  38. with broadband internet access
  39. combines with a feature of
  40. many emerging markets around the world,
  41. which is often inefficient
  42. traditional banking and financial systems.
  43. And this combination allows emerging markets
  44. to leapfrog a hundred years of developments
  45. which took place in western markets
  46. in a short period of a few years.
  47. And perhaps the best example we have
  48. seen is in the context of China.
  49. Today, if we think of the major internet
  50. and e-commerce firms in China,
  51. firms like Alibaba, Tencent, Baidu,
  52. or even traditional financial institutions like Ping An,
  53. today, the numbers of customers
  54. using their financial services
  55. are absolutely staggering.
  56. It has transformed
  57. the interaction of the individual
  58. with technology in finance,
  59. and at the same time
  60. is transforming the financial systems
  61. and the economies of emerging markets.
  62. One that we’ll turn back to in Module 4,
  63. we’ve seen in the context of India.
  64. Using a variety of
  65. technological infrastructure design elements
  66. to support expansion of finance
  67. to literally hundreds of millions of people
  68. who did not previously have access
  69. to financial services.
  70. This is perhaps the most important story
  71. that we’ve ever seen
  72. in the context of financial inclusion,
  73. economic growth,
  74. and financial transformation.
  75. And it’s exciting
  76. because many of the developments
  77. are being led for the first time in Asia.
  78. At the same time,
  79. these tremendous changes and opportunities
  80. also bring with them new risks.
  81. An idea that things can move faster
  82. than we’ve ever seen before.
  83. And if we look at the things
  84. that have changed in fintech,
  85. it is the combination of new entrants,
  86. startups and technology companies
  87. with speed of change
  88. that is driving so much of the opportunities
  89. and the transformations.
  90. But, this combination of
  91. new entrants and speed of technology
  92. allows something else to happen as well,
  93. and that is for risks
  94. to grow ever more rapidly
  95. than they have ever done before.
  96. In an example,
  97. a new banking startup
  98. launched by a technology company
  99. can acquire,
  100. in a period of a weekend, millions of customers,
  101. taking in one weekend
  102. the amount that would have taken
  103. in a traditional developed market decades
  104. for a financial institution to achieve.
  105. This is the risk that we call
  106. moving from too small to care
  107. to too big to fail,
  108. and it can now occur in record time.

1.8 From Too-Small-To-Care to Too-Big-To-Fail

  1. We are now going to be
  2. talking about an important notion.
  3. The notion of going from too small to care
  4. to too big to fail.
  5. This notion is important,
  6. because it fits into
  7. what you have been learning so far
  8. in our first module.
  9. Financial technology
  10. is a 150-year-old industry
  11. that has been divided
  12. across 3 different eras.
  13. The first era was a 100 years old,
  14. the second era was 40 years old,
  15. and the third era,
  16. in which we are today,
  17. will be about a decade old.
  18. Now what you start noticing is that
  19. each of these era
  20. are shorter than the previous one.
  21. And what this means,
  22. is that technology is changing faster,
  23. and is penetrating into the public
  24. at a faster rate than ever before.
  25. And that’s something that
  26. you can actually notice
  27. from your own perspective.
  28. Let’s take the phone, for example.
  29. It took many decades for land lines
  30. to reach a million customers in the US.
  31. However, it only took a few years
  32. for mobile phones
  33. to reach the same penetration
  34. in that country.
  35. Now let’s bring that to finance
  36. and let’s think about banks.
  37. Acquiring half a million customers
  38. is a very big feet.
  39. But KakaoTalk
  40. has been able to do that in only 2 days.
  41. Now if you take challenger banks
  42. or regular banks,
  43. it would typically take them many years
  44. for them to acquire
  45. the same number of customers.
  46. Finally, I can also give you
  47. the example of Bitcoin.
  48. Most of you must have heard of Bitcoin,
  49. if not already owned some Bitcoins.
  50. And here the example is very clear.
  51. As an asset class,
  52. Bitcoin is becoming more valuable
  53. faster than any other asset classes
  54. that previously have led to a bubble.
  55. What Bitcoin has done in only one year,
  56. it took many more years
  57. for other classes to do.
  58. Therefore, those three examples
  59. are an illustration
  60. that technology is changing
  61. more and more rapidly,
  62. and therefore regulators
  63. need to adapt themselves.
  64. Let me show you a graph.
  65. On the Y-axis,
  66. you have the size of a company,
  67. and on the X-axis,
  68. you have the time it takes
  69. for that company to grow.
  70. Now, normally what we are used to understand,
  71. is the notion of organic growth.
  72. You will grow
  73. because of the results of your business,
  74. and every now and then,
  75. you may grow from an acquisition.
  76. And financial institutions that you may know,
  77. such as tier one players like Citi, HSBC, etc.,
  78. have grown as financial institution
  79. over the last 100 plus years.
  80. And therefore,
  81. their path of growth is very predictable.
  82. For regulators, that’s actually very useful,
  83. because regulators don’t necessarily always regulate
  84. and enforce action
  85. against certain companies.
  86. Indeed, what you will see now
  87. on the X-axis,
  88. is that you go from too small to care,
  89. too large to ignore, to too big to fail.
  90. What this means is that
  91. if you are between too small to care
  92. and too large to ignore,
  93. regulators may or may not,
  94. at their own discretion,
  95. choose to enforce an action against you,
  96. if you are breaching a rule.
  97. The reason of that discretion
  98. is because not everything
  99. is worth suing for.
  100. If you are only having
  101. one or two consumers that you are hurting,
  102. this is not worth the regulator’s time,
  103. compared to a breach
  104. that would impact over a million persons.
  105. However, the second problem is
  106. companies that become too big to fail.
  107. The notion of too big to fail
  108. is deeply rooted
  109. into the financial crisis,
  110. in the same way that the financial crisis
  111. has led to the emergence of FinTech.
  112. But in a similar fashion,
  113. the emergence of mobile phone
  114. has also favorized the emergence of FinTech,
  115. and with it,
  116. the mobile phone is a distribution channel
  117. for financial technology companies.
  118. Because now financial technology companies
  119. can use that infrastructure
  120. to more quickly reach you
  121. as a consumer,
  122. they can go to too small to care
  123. to too big to fail overnight,
  124. creating a regulatory blind spot for regulators.
  125. This is the example that we take,
  126. for example, in China with Yu’e Bao.
  127. Yu’e Bao went from
  128. zero to 90 billion US dollar
  129. of assets under management
  130. in only nine months.
  131. They did in nine months
  132. what it took over seven decades
  133. for large tier-one asset managers to achieve.
  134. Therefore, regulatory sandboxes
  135. are one of the first tools for regulators
  136. to resolve the risk created from startups
  137. that go from too small to care to big to fail,
  138. but that’s only the first step.
  139. The second step will be
  140. about building smart regulation
  141. on top of regulatory sandboxes,
  142. and we will cover that topic
  143. in the RegTech module of this course.

1.9: Introduction to Regulation

  1. Fintech is the intersection
  2. of finance, technology, and regulation.
  3. Financial services
  4. even before the 2008 global financial crisis
  5. was already one of the world’s
  6. most heavily regulated industries.
  7. And as a result,
  8. when we think about fintech,
  9. we have to also think about
  10. regulation and the environment
  11. in which finance and technology operate.
  12. And fintech,
  13. because of the changes in new entrants
  14. as well as,
  15. speed of technological development
  16. poses major challenges for policy makers
  17. in determining
  18. how best to regulate markets
  19. in order to best achieve the objectives,
  20. the opportunities of financial inclusion,
  21. of democratisation of finance,
  22. of financial transformation,
  23. while at the same time,
  24. minimising the risks
  25. of both financial fraud, misconduct,
  26. as well as, financial crises.
  27. And if we think back
  28. to before the global financial crisis,
  29. prior to the global financial crisis,
  30. regulators and policymakers
  31. tended to have a very positive attitude
  32. towards financial innovation.
  33. There’s a famous quote from Paul Volcker,
  34. former Chairman of the U.S. Federal Reserve,
  35. where he says that,
  36. “the only important financial innovation
  37. that he had seen in his lifetime was the ATM machine.”
  38. Why?
  39. The ATM machine fundamentally improved
  40. people’s day-to-day lives,
  41. and this was in contrast
  42. to some of the financial innovations that we saw
  43. prior to the global financial crisis,
  44. many of which were central
  45. to that crisis happening.
  46. Things like credit derivatives,
  47. or credit default obligations
  48. or asset backed securities,
  49. all of which were financial innovations,
  50. but also innovations
  51. which brought new risks.
  52. Because of the global financial crisis,
  53. for the past 10 years,
  54. regulators and policymakers around the world
  55. have taken a very cautious approach
  56. to financial services regulation,
  57. focusing on building
  58. new costly regulatory frameworks
  59. to prevent crises,
  60. to prevent misconduct,
  61. to protect customers,
  62. but at the same time,
  63. triggering developments in fintech,
  64. but also making innovation
  65. more challenging and more difficult.
  66. And so, over the past several years,
  67. as regulators have become more comfortable
  68. with the opportunities
  69. and potential transformative developments
  70. taking place in fintech,
  71. they have also become more friendly
  72. from the standpoint of regulation.
  73. But from the standpoint of regulation,
  74. as we’ll see throughout the rest of the course,
  75. there’s a continual process
  76. of balancing objectives
  77. like protecting consumers,
  78. protecting the safety and soundness
  79. of the financial sector,
  80. protecting against financial crises
  81. with objectives of financial inclusion,
  82. competition, innovation, and development.
  83. And it is this regulatory environment that
  84. much of fintech takes place in today.

The Future of RegTech and 6 Technologies Impacting It (by Thomson Reuters)

  1. Hello. My name is Julia Walker.
  2. I’m the head of Market Development
  3. for Regulatory Technology across Asia-Pacific.
  4. So, what is RegTech?
  5. Well, I like to think about RegTech
  6. being FinTech’s best friend,
  7. or at least the more sensible better half.
  8. Perhaps we can think about it this way:
  9. if FinTech is the rock star,
  10. RegTech is the band member
  11. happy to hang out in the background.
  12. However, RegTech has more recently started
  13. to take center-stage,
  14. usually increasing regulatory challenges
  15. in the financial sector,
  16. and the need to apply technology to solve them.
  17. As you’ll see in this module,
  18. the future of RegTech
  19. is one of positive financial innovation,
  20. as well of benefits for financial regulation.
  21. It’s the combination of both opening the door
  22. for the automation of regulation.
  23. In this module, we will show
  24. six new emerging technologies,
  25. and how these will redefine the future of RegTech.
  26. In module one, we provided you with a macro-trend
  27. of increased regulatory activity
  28. and enforcement since 2008.
  29. However now I wanted to help you
  30. with three simple illustrations
  31. to grasp the complexity behind regulatory adherence.
  32. As you can see in this graph, regulation is complex,
  33. voluminous and interconnected.
  34. When you understand that large tier one universal banks
  35. operate in over 60 jurisdictions,
  36. it’s easier to picture the difficulty
  37. to comply globally.
  38. Especially with new rules
  39. having cross-jurisdictional affect such as GDPR.
  40. In addition, compliant obligations
  41. are always in a state of flux.
  42. Regulators’ proactivity around the world
  43. can be seen by the number of regulatory changes.
  44. Regulatory alerts increased from 8,000 in 2008
  45. to 56,000 in 2017,
  46. an average of 216 alerts per day.
  47. The global financial crisis of 2008
  48. highlighted the systemic risk
  49. within the global financial system,
  50. serious conduct issues and a push to
  51. tighten anti-money laundering controls.
  52. Regulators in the United States
  53. and Europe have imposed 342 billion in fines
  54. on banks for misconduct,
  55. including the violation of anti-money laundering rules,
  56. and it is likely to top 400 billion by 2020.
  57. And it’s not just the fines causing headaches,
  58. conduct issues are also leading to revenue loss.
  59. A Hong Kong-based financial services company
  60. has estimated that bad behaviour
  61. has erased 850 billion in profits
  62. for the top 50 global banks.
  63. In New York, a 185 million dollar fine was issued
  64. for failing to protect client assets,
  65. and it’s just not finances getting fined.
  66. RegTech can also help other industries
  67. especially when we know that regulatory enforcement
  68. is on the rise for corporates.
  69. 18 billion has been imposed to a car manufacturer,
  70. and 60 million to
  71. a Japanese-based electronics company.
  72. On the backdrop of
  73. these macro changes in cost pressures
  74. is a number of new emerging technologies.
  75. In our discussion today,
  76. we want to look at some of the underlying trends
  77. that will shape the global financial system
  78. over the next 10 to 20 years,
  79. and remove the pain points associated
  80. with compliance obligations.
  81. Blockchain.
  82. Immutable, consensus driven,
  83. transparent, distributed, work flow enabled,
  84. and trust being replaced with truth.
  85. Blockchain could potentially
  86. power the identity market place
  87. and has the potential to modernise
  88. or even replace existing trading,
  89. clearing, and settlement operations.
  90. Smart contracts and smart oracles will exist
  91. to enable customers to use blockchain
  92. to streamline post-trade life-cycle,
  93. and other capital market use cases.
  94. Digitised assets with blockchain
  95. as a potential new way to digitise
  96. and maintain records for government
  97. and other relevant industries.
  98. Cognitive computing and artificial intelligence.
  99. We are just at the beginning of a revolution
  100. that will touch every business,
  101. and every life on this planet.
  102. The data volumes are exploding.
  103. More data has been created
  104. in the past two years
  105. than in the entire previous history of the human race.
  106. At the moment however,
  107. less than 0.5% of all data is ever analysed and used.
  108. As this quantum of data grows
  109. the need for standards,
  110. structure and integration does also.
  111. The need for smart machines
  112. to synthesise and make sense of data
  113. and support the human cognitive
  114. and decision-making process is therefore paramount.
  115. The internet of things.
  116. Electronic sensors are showing up
  117. in devices all around the world.
  118. We now have self-driving cars that can navigate
  119. their way through traffic.
  120. Even regular cars track with AI
  121. in how fast they’re going.
  122. But how can this enormous world of information
  123. generated by machines and sensors
  124. be used for better financial investment,
  125. trading decisions, and risk management?
  126. How can this data be aggregated
  127. and anonymized to protect individual privacy
  128. while retaining its most useful, actual information?
  129. Customers must have the confidence that
  130. their data is collected, stored, and used
  131. in a manner that benefits them,
  132. and does not jeopardise their privacy.
  133. Open source and API economy.
  134. APIs are fueling
  135. the customer-driven platform revolution.
  136. They’re becoming a primary customer interface
  137. for technology-driven products and services,
  138. and a key channel for driving revenue
  139. and brand engagement.
  140. APIs are a set of rules
  141. that govern how one type
  142. of computer software communicates with another.
  143. They enable bank customers to
  144. share their personal financial information
  145. with third parties to generate opportunities
  146. for better deals on financial products
  147. and to use transaction data to access
  148. and compare products easily.
  149. From the bank’s perspective,
  150. the technology enables them to remain competitive
  151. by providing them with opportunities
  152. to provide improved customer-friendly services
  153. and reach out to untapped markets.
  154. The Cloud.
  155. Everyone is looking at the Cloud,
  156. and for good reason.
  157. Cloud computing eliminates the capital expenditure
  158. of buying hardware and software and setting up
  159. and running on-site data centres.
  160. Big data.
  161. Firms need help organising
  162. and collecting enterprise and third party data
  163. to derive new insights.
  164. Data tagging allows investors, regulators
  165. and market participants
  166. to organise and analyse massive amounts of data
  167. and information more efficiently
  168. by associating pieces of information
  169. with keyword tags.
  170. The rapid changes and maturation of technologies
  171. from big data management to advanced analytics
  172. to machine learning,
  173. to artificial intelligence,
  174. and robotic process automation are
  175. fueling product innovation
  176. and providing customers potential opportunities
  177. to further transform operations.
  178. So there you go,
  179. six of the key technologies that you need to be aware of
  180. as we look at the future of RegTech
  181. but before I leave you,
  182. I wanted to highlight the three critical considerations
  183. to harness the value
  184. of innovation brought by RegTech.
  185. First, RegTech solutions need to be powered
  186. by trusted, structured and unstructured data
  187. that also has data mastering capability
  188. for effective integration with existing solutions.
  189. Second, advanced analytics
  190. and artificial intelligence
  191. will play a key role.
  192. And lastly, digital identity.
  193. Digital identity is a foundation
  194. to all RegTech solutions
  195. because identity of individual, organisation,
  196. or a physical asset are atomic in governance,
  197. regulatory compliance
  198. and risk-management solutions.
  199. We will cover this in more detail in module five.
