Module 2 Payment, Cryptocurrencies and Blockchain

Welcome to Module 2

2.1 Module 2 Introduction

  1. Welcome back to Hong Kong U FinTech Course.
  2. We are now starting the second module
  3. covering payments and money.
  4. In this course,
  5. you will learn about, first,
  6. how payment has evolved over the years,
  7. second, its importance in the context of FinTech,
  8. and finally, how new technologies
  9. such as distributed ledger technology
  10. is building a brand new payment stack.
  11. Payment is a critical part of finance.
  12. Since the FinTech industry
  13. caught the eyes of investors,
  14. payment has consistently been
  15. the largest sub-sector
  16. in terms of VC investment.
  17. Not only this,
  18. but the media coverage has strongly covered
  19. the impact of e-money
  20. whether in Africa with M-Pesa
  21. and Alipay in China,
  22. two examples that
  23. we will cover further in this module.
  24. Whilst the majority of the FinTech industry
  25. is represented by B2B companies,
  26. payment represents an exception in many respect.
  27. This industry has many layers
  28. and therefore clients and users.
  29. We will reveal and explain
  30. how each are interrelated
  31. and how technology has changed
  32. their model of operation.
  33. Payment is unique in that
  34. we use it every day,
  35. in Europe alone,
  36. people are using their contactless card
  37. over 10 times every day.
  38. It also represents a very social element,
  39. whether it’s gifting or requesting money,
  40. technology is changing the way
  41. we interact with people,
  42. from WeChat red-envelope connecting families
  43. thousands of miles apart
  44. to Venmo bill splitting feature
  45. which is changing the social stigma
  46. over requesting for cash.
  47. However, there is more than the eye can see.
  48. Payment to function
  49. correctly relies on a complex infrastructure
  50. such as telco operators, banks
  51. or international consortiums like Swift.
  52. In this module,
  53. we will put you at the centre of the industry.
  54. We will be entering each layer one by one,
  55. before showing you
  56. the future of the industry
  57. and how and why we believe
  58. it’s now at a tipping point.
  59. You can imagine
  60. each of this course’s module as circle.
  61. You will easily relate to the one closest to you
  62. but you will also learn about
  63. the importance of the underlying infrastructure
  64. found in the more distant circles
  65. which you may not use on a regular basis.
  66. The first circle is the one closest to you
  67. and you are in its centre.
  68. We will introduce payment
  69. from point of view of an individual
  70. and how it has evolved
  71. from barter to cash,
  72. from checks to cards,
  73. and more recently, QR codes and cryptocurrency.
  74. The second circle looks at
  75. non-banks and telecom operators
  76. that have provided payment solution,
  77. leveraging on mobile phones
  78. and person-to-person networks they represent.
  79. These new entrants are providing services
  80. to a previously underserved market,
  81. but with it, raises opportunities and risks.
  82. The third circle is composed of your bank
  83. and your government central bank.
  84. Traditionally a place where
  85. you have deposited your money,
  86. recent mandated changes
  87. such as real-time-gross-settlement, RTGS,
  88. or PSD2 in Europe
  89. are opening opportunities
  90. for new business models
  91. on how your money
  92. can be received, sent, or spent.
  93. The fourth and final circle looks at the world,
  94. cross-border transaction and correspondent banking.
  95. As individuals, you may have experienced
  96. cross-border payments
  97. in the context of remittance,
  98. however, this only represents
  99. a very small fraction
  100. of the global financial transaction initiated
  101. and settled through payment network such as Swift.
  102. Finally, the payment module
  103. will open by introducing the development
  104. of a new payment stack
  105. which has been made possible
  106. by the advancement of a new technology
  107. such as DLT, distributed ledger technology,
  108. but also increasingly being justified
  109. by recent risks identified in the payment rail system
  110. that is now over 50 years old.
  111. This final module will provide you
  112. the basis of operation
  113. of a decentralised system,
  114. its difference with the current status quo
  115. and its potential to complement
  116. or replace the current infrastructure.

Module 2 Learning Objectives

Module 2 introduces the spectrum of electronic and other means of payment, from the traditional (cash) to the most recent (Bitcoin and cryptocurrencies). It highlights to learners the evolutionary context as well as the transformative role of new technologies in both traditional payments as well as alternative money and payment systems and the role of these in our daily lives and in our broader economies.

In Module 2, learners will:

  • Consider the evolution of payment and money, from paper to digital to cryptocurrencies and beyond.
  • Understand the underlying infrastructure of both traditional and new forms of payment.
  • Understand how technology is transforming payment in developing countries and how it is changing payment in developed countries.
  • Analyze the role of blockchain and cryptocurrencies in developing new means of money and payment.

2.2 Individual Payments

  1. We will now be looking at
  2. the evolution of payment
  3. from the perspective of usage of an individual.
  4. Most of us are using money
  5. to make and receive payments.
  6. However, technology has changed
  7. both the form of money takes,
  8. but also how it’s being transferred.
  9. In short, you can think of four phases
  10. that we will all discuss in more details.
  11. This first phase was barter.
  12. The second phase was commodity money.
  13. The third phase was coinage.
  14. And the fourth phase was dematerialized payments.
  15. The evolution of money
  16. as a mode of payment
  17. is over 10,000 years old.
  18. The first phase started in 9000 BC,
  19. up until 600 BC.
  20. It was the one of barter.
  21. People directly exchanged goods and services.
  22. There was no money as a standard
  23. or medium of exchange.
  24. This system, however, had limitations,
  25. including the capacity of carrying goods,
  26. and transporting it across long distances
  27. to make exchanges
  28. or standardise commerce.
  29. This evolved into the second phase
  30. of commodity money.
  31. With certain items being selected
  32. to perform transaction.
  33. For example, in 1100 BC,
  34. China used small cast replicas
  35. of goods, token, made out of bronze.
  36. However, you may be more familiar with
  37. example of other commodities being used,
  38. such as silver or gold.
  39. Even the word salary
  40. is actually related to that era.
  41. Back then, Roman soldiers were paid in salt
  42. and their salt was a salary.
  43. From 600 B.C.,
  44. coinage has been introduced in Lydia, today’s Turkey,
  45. but it will take another 1000 to 2000 years
  46. until paper money starts to be introduced,
  47. first in China, in the 700s,
  48. and then in Europe,
  49. with Sweden leading the way in the 17th century.
  50. Paper money represents an example of
  51. how technology, the printing press,
  52. allowed to store varying amounts,
  53. from one US dollar to 100 US dollar,
  54. on the same size of paper.
  55. In addition, European banks started to guarantee
  56. that the bearers of bank notes
  57. had to be paid in gold equivalent.
  58. One of the previous limitation of money,
  59. that it was light,
  60. and therefore flying away in the wind
  61. when you do commerce,
  62. became an advantage,
  63. because now instead of paying cargos
  64. with heavy bags of coins
  65. that were inconvenient and risky,
  66. you could pay with small bills
  67. that were light and stored a lot of value.
  68. The dematerialization of payment
  69. increased in speed and method
  70. from the 1950s onward,
  71. as technology has become more commoditized.
  72. Payment was increasingly digital,
  73. and the volume of non-cash transaction
  74. has been steadily rising ever since.
  75. Here is a timeline of how
  76. payment innovation has evolved,
  77. and with it, your behaviour.
  78. 1946, the first credit card is created
  79. with it before being popularised by
  80. card networks in the ’50s.
  81. The chequebook is introduced
  82. about the same period.
  83. In 1980s,
  84. the ATM network becomes
  85. interconnected and global
  86. with millions of withdrawal points available.
  87. In the 1990s,
  88. the EMV standard creation allows
  89. to enhance security
  90. and data storage on cards.
  91. In 2000, mobile money
  92. provides a payment solution in developing countries,
  93. or banking services in developed nation.
  94. The dot-com period
  95. and the rise of internet
  96. and e-Commerce companies
  97. has started the trend of complete digitization
  98. of cash transaction.
  99. Plastic cards have been replaced
  100. by e-wallets or virtual cards.
  101. But it kept on going.
  102. 2009, Bitcoin is created
  103. as a decentralised currency,
  104. stored across the internet.
  105. 2010, introduction of
  106. contactless cards and payments,
  107. which seven years later,
  108. are one of the most popular method of payment.
  109. 2015 onward,
  110. the popularisation of wearable devices,
  111. mobile wallets and cryptocurrencies continues.
  112. Payment methods have changed
  113. and with it all behaviours.
  114. From the Amazon supermarket without checkouts,
  115. to Venmo true-value proposition
  116. of not being a bill splitting app,
  117. but instead addressing the social stigma
  118. to request for money.
  119. Predicting the future of payment is difficult,
  120. and it is not anymore linked just to money,
  121. but to technology itself,
  122. that is changing the methods
  123. available to you.
  124. Indeed, cryptocurrencies
  125. were not even considered
  126. less then a decade ago.
  127. And even today,
  128. data is becoming the new oil
  129. and allowing a new type of barter system
  130. where people are giving
  131. their personal information,
  132. in returns of goods and services.
  133. Therefore, payment and money
  134. is constantly evolving,
  135. even more rapidly today.
  136. And we will point you toward
  137. the further reading materials
  138. for you to keep updated
  139. your knowledge on that sector.

2.3 Developing Countries and DFS: The Story of Mobile Money

  1. Hello, I’m Ross Buckley,
  2. the King and Wood Mallesons Chair
  3. of International Finance Law at UNSW Sydney,
  4. and I’m here to talk to you about
  5. the story of mobile money
  6. and digital finance in developing countries.
  7. I want to tell this story in terms of four questions.
  8. What? Where? How? and Why?
  9. So, let’s start with “What?”.
  10. What is mobile money?
  11. Well, it’s e-money.
  12. It’s an electronic credit on a mobile device
  13. that represents a unit of real money
  14. that typically sits in a trust account
  15. in a commercial bank somewhere.
  16. So, it’s an electronic representation of paper money,
  17. and that being electronic allows you
  18. to do all sorts of useful things with it,
  19. like save more readily,
  20. make remittances,
  21. send money back to your village,
  22. pay bills,
  23. do all sorts of things that
  24. otherwise would take you time
  25. and cost you more money.
  26. Where was it developed?
  27. Well, it came about because one day,
  28. telecommunications companies realised
  29. that their software tracked in real time airtime credits.
  30. They knew exactly how many minutes
  31. a prepaid customer still had in credit,
  32. and if they knew that,
  33. they knew how many currency credits the customer had.
  34. They realised this software could well adapted
  35. to do the same thing.
  36. And so, mobile money was born.
  37. Initially, in a in a few countries,
  38. including the Philippines,
  39. but it achieved lift off in Kenya a decade ago in 2007.
  40. It was a Vodafone product, M-Pesa,
  41. and within three or four years,
  42. the majority of Kenya’s GDP was flowing
  43. through M-Pesa every year.
  44. The “How” question, how does it work?
  45. Well, it’s based on cash-in and cash-out agents
  46. who are the same small shopkeepers who sell airtime.
  47. These are tiny stores,
  48. maybe three metres by three metres,
  49. by the side of the road
  50. that sell tinned meat and soft drinks,
  51. and cigarettes and airtime,
  52. and is also act as agents for the mobile money service.
  53. So, a customer can walk in,
  54. pass some cash across the counter,
  55. and get a credit
  56. that shows up immediately on their phone
  57. and they can walk away happy,
  58. knowing they’ve got
  59. some electronic money on their phone
  60. that they can do things with,
  61. that they couldn’t so readily do with the paper money.
  62. That’s one way a credit comes about,
  63. but the more often way is
  64. the government makes a transfer payment,
  65. a welfare payment directly to the phone,
  66. because paper-based payments
  67. have all sorts of problems for governments
  68. in developing countries,
  69. and electronic payments
  70. are a much more efficient method.
  71. Often, central banks will want
  72. this service offered by their banks
  73. because they trust the banks,
  74. they know the banks,
  75. they know how to regulate them.
  76. But, banks around the world
  77. are not typically good at providing services
  78. to poor customers.
  79. The entities that are already doing that
  80. are the TELCOs.
  81. They are very efficient at selling services
  82. to very poor people and generally,
  83. if you’re a government,
  84. you’ll get the innovation
  85. in the mobile money space
  86. from your telecommunications companies
  87. more than your banks.
  88. The fourth question is,
  89. why does it matter?
  90. And it matters because without electronic money,
  91. paying an electricity bill or a school bill
  92. can require a parent to take the whole day off work,
  93. travel for a few hours on a bus,
  94. stand in a queue for a few hours,
  95. travel a few hours home.
  96. This is not typically transformative
  97. for people who live in the capital city
  98. or in the big cities,
  99. it transforms the life of people
  100. who live in the countryside
  101. who don’t have access to bricks and mortar branches.
  102. It enables them to send money home to a relative,
  103. to save money safely,
  104. to pay bills in their microbusiness,
  105. whatever they need to do,
  106. to do it quickly and safely and cheaply.
  107. In some states in India,
  108. before mobile money was introduced,
  109. up to 45% of government welfare payments
  110. would go missing
  111. because you had a largely illiterate population
  112. and a paper-based system.
  113. The electronic auto trail of mobile money
  114. gets around that problem.
  115. In Papua New Guinea,
  116. teachers are regularly taking off days off school
  117. so they could cash their paychecks,
  118. before mobile money systems allowed them
  119. to get access to their pay without having to,
  120. again, get on a bus for a few hours
  121. to the nearest major town.
  122. The buzzword is financial inclusion.
  123. It’s about including these previously excluded people
  124. in the financial services
  125. and allowing them to do things more efficiently,
  126. and thereby promote economic growth
  127. and reduce poverty.
  128. What are the challenges with it?
  129. Well, it’s worked well in east Africa.
  130. Kenya, Tanzania, Uganda,
  131. it’s worked very well.
  132. In other places, the record is more patchy.
  133. In many countries,
  134. governments transfer payments
  135. to mobile money accounts,
  136. people go to the cash-in and cash-out agent,
  137. take the cash out, and then transact in cash.
  138. That fails to realise most of the goals
  139. of financial inclusion, you know,
  140. they’re not getting the efficiency benefits,
  141. you’re not getting the network effects,
  142. you’re not getting the vibrant ecosystem,
  143. and it also causes a real problem for agents
  144. because agents are mainly handing out cash,
  145. and unless the other part of their business
  146. provides them with a ready inflow of cash,
  147. they’re going to run out of cash often
  148. and they’re not going to be able
  149. to meet redemption requests,
  150. and then people get frustrated with the system.
  151. So, lots of people around the world
  152. are working to try and solve this.
  153. They’re working by developing
  154. new technological products,
  155. customer education programmes,
  156. or in the case of the programme I lead at UNSW,
  157. we work with central banks
  158. in partnership with the UN Capital Development Fund around the world,
  159. we work with poor country central banks,
  160. helping them get their regulation more adapted,
  161. more suitable to digital finance.
  162. But, there are main roadblocks
  163. to vibrant digital financial ecosystems
  164. in many countries,
  165. and one of the fundamental ones of these
  166. is a failure to adapt products to local needs
  167. and to understand local customer journeys,
  168. and I’ll discuss this with you later.
  169. Thank you.

2.4 Developing Countries and DFS: Regulation of Mobile Money

  1. Hello I’m Ross Buckley,
  2. the King and Wood Mallesons Chair
  3. of International Finance Law at UNSW Sydney.
  4. I’m here to talk to you about
  5. the regulation of mobile money
  6. and digital financial services in developing countries.
  7. The first thing we need to do
  8. with mobile money is keep it safe.
  9. We need to protect the float.
  10. The float being the body of actual money
  11. that represents the electronic credits
  12. on the mobile devices.
  13. In Common Law countries,
  14. this is relatively easy to do,
  15. using the institution of a trust.
  16. You have a trust deed which
  17. we’ve given you a version of
  18. in the publication that’s up on your slides.
  19. There’s a trust deed,
  20. either money is deposited
  21. under that deed in a commercial bank,
  22. and the deed provides for the role of a protector.
  23. The protector will typically be the Central Bank,
  24. although it could be somebody else.
  25. They will have the duty of
  26. inspecting the trust arrangements
  27. and insuring, most importantly,
  28. that there is a 1:1 ratio
  29. between issued electronic money
  30. and actual money sitting in the trust account,
  31. various other rights of the protector as well.
  32. And the protectee you need
  33. because the beneficiaries of the trust
  34. are the customers,
  35. they’re not going to understand all this,
  36. and they’re not going to enforce their own rights.
  37. So as I said, in common law countries,
  38. relatively easy to do.
  39. In Civil Law countries, not so simple,
  40. because usually the trust is not there.
  41. But you can get to the same end
  42. using a mix of mandates,
  43. contracts, fiduciary contracts,
  44. and sometimes direct regulation as needed.
  45. There’s no one size fits all solution
  46. for Civil Law countries,
  47. you need to craft a solution for each jurisdiction.
  48. But again on your slides,
  49. there’s an article that we’ve written,
  50. that you can easily draw down off SSRN,
  51. that deals with all of those technical issues.
  52. Once you’ve kept the float safe,
  53. there’s only two other, in my view,
  54. core pieces of regulation you need.
  55. One is Consumer Protection Regulation
  56. and the other is Money Laundering
  57. and Terrorism Financing Regulation.
  58. You need the latter because you need it.
  59. There’s an international regime
  60. that you eventually see a country on a blacklist
  61. if it doesn’t have adequate AML/CTF regulation.
  62. And the important thing to do in dealing with that,
  63. is to use proportional risk-based assessment.
  64. In most poor countries
  65. you are not going to be able to comply,
  66. you’re not going to be able to
  67. reach the very highest standards of
  68. Know-Your-Customer certification.
  69. You’re going to have to, you know, cut some corners
  70. and decisions are going to have to be made.
  71. But the international regime allows for that.
  72. The problem often is that regulators
  73. try to enforce standards that are higher
  74. than is required by the international regime
  75. and are not appropriate for their own countries.
  76. So we need a little bit of regulatory innovation
  77. and courage often in that space.
  78. Consumer Protection is
  79. essential for the substantive reason
  80. that without it,
  81. people won’t trust the system.
  82. If people are losing their money,
  83. they won’t keep using it.
  84. And mobile money and digital finance
  85. needs network effects.
  86. It needs a lot of people using it
  87. so that it’s beneficial for everybody.
  88. The easy part for consumer protection is rules.
  89. It’s relatively easy to craft a good set of rules.
  90. The difficult part is recourse mechanisms.
  91. People have got to have some way
  92. to get their problems solved.
  93. And any effective recourse regime, in my view,
  94. needs a free-to-call telephone number
  95. that’s manned a good number of hours a day
  96. by someone with authority
  97. to resolve customer problems.
  98. If people have to pay to make the call
  99. and wait a long time online,
  100. they’re going to hang up.
  101. If it’s a free call
  102. but it’s not answered for an hour,
  103. they’re going to hang up.
  104. They’re not going to get their problem solved.
  105. And central banks need to police this.
  106. Central banks actually need to
  107. sort of play the role of mystery shopper, I think,
  108. and pretend to be customers and find out
  109. how well these recourse mechanisms work.
  110. Because they’re really essential to consumer trust
  111. in a digital financial ecosystem.
  112. I also wanted to talk about the difference
  113. between FinTech in rich countries
  114. and digital finance in poor countries,
  115. because they come from quite different places.
  116. FinTechs in rich countries are typically startups.
  117. They’re young entrepreneurs seeking to get rich.
  118. Digital Financial Services in poor countries are partly,
  119. occasionally driven by that, but not so often.
  120. They’re mostly driven by government policy.
  121. Governments have embraced
  122. this idea of financial inclusion
  123. and they’re encouraging their banks
  124. and their telcos to provide it.
  125. So rich country regulators
  126. are trialling regulatory sandboxes,
  127. safe places in which entrepreneurs can take risks
  128. with reduced licencing obligations,
  129. and they’re trialling other measures
  130. to promote innovation.
  131. And in some poor countries,
  132. regulators are doing this,
  133. but something much more is needed
  134. of regulators in poor countries.
  135. And that’s a major mindshift,
  136. a major change of perspective.
  137. So that they actually get out of the capital city,
  138. they get into their own villages
  139. where these innovations are needed,
  140. and they talk to their own people and find out
  141. what the customers in their countries really need.
  142. Because this is what’s holding the development
  143. of Digital Financial Ecosystems up in many countries.
  144. It’s the offering of inappropriate products.
  145. These countries are small.
  146. The telecommunications companies
  147. naturally want to roll out the same products
  148. across multiple countries,
  149. but the local Central Banks’ role in part
  150. is to encourage the provider
  151. to adapt the product for the local market.
  152. And that’s a deeply different and innovative role
  153. for a central bank
  154. and one that they’re not accustomed to do.
  155. But experience teaches us if they don’t do it,
  156. it’s a real risk that the innovation will not thrive.
  157. We’ve written about the customer journey,
  158. we’ve written about building consumer demand
  159. for Digital Finance.
  160. You can find those references on the slides.
  161. So I just want to conclude by emphasising
  162. that for Digital Finance
  163. to flourish in many countries,
  164. a big change of mindset is required by the central bank.
  165. They will typically be the primary regulator,
  166. they’re typically very prudent,
  167. because their job is to preserve the safety
  168. and soundness of the entire system.
  169. But mobile money doesn’t typically
  170. raise systemic stability issues.
  171. It’s not a risk on that scale.
  172. It doesn’t need regulation of that weight.
  173. It needs light and subtle regulation,
  174. a maintenance of a watching brief
  175. principally by the central bank,
  176. and that requires a degree of regulatory courage.
  177. Thank you.

Additional Resource Insights

Please click the link to download the presentation slides for Video 2.4 Developing Countries and DFS: The Regulation of Mobile Money.

2.5 RTGS Systems

  1. We will now explain
  2. how Real Time Gross Settlement has benefited
  3. the development of FinTech Innovation.
  4. We will do so by introducing two use cases,
  5. one on the payment side,
  6. and the other one on the lending side.
  7. However, before we start,
  8. what is RTGS, or real-time gross settlement?
  9. In short, it allows for transaction between banks
  10. to happen in real time
  11. and settle on a one-to-one basis.
  12. This is to be contrasted with batch transaction
  13. where banks settle the net amount owed
  14. at a pre-determined interval
  15. such as the end of the day or the week.
  16. Practically, you may have already
  17. experienced this yourself.
  18. For example, if you are in Hong Kong
  19. and you wish to make a transfer
  20. to a friend who has an account
  21. in another bank as yours,
  22. if you initiate the payment transaction in the afternoon
  23. it will only arrive the following day.
  24. This is because
  25. the payments are processed in batch.
  26. However, if you are in the UK
  27. and viewing this course,
  28. you will know that bank-to-bank transfers
  29. are instant and free.
  30. This is because Faster Payment,
  31. or an RTGS equivalent,
  32. has been introduced in 2014.
  33. The necessity to move from
  34. a batch to a real time world
  35. is driven by two factors.
  36. Firstly, technological capacities,
  37. which have become more robust and scalable.
  38. Secondly, customer expectation of instant delivery.
  39. As we move to mail to e-mail,
  40. we remove latency and cost to communicate.
  41. The same was due to happen in payments.
  42. Because RTGS systems
  43. constitute a core component
  44. of any national payment stack,
  45. the reform process is slow
  46. and once engaged,
  47. takes decades to be fully implemented.
  48. And whilst the majority of countries
  49. have RTGS system in place,
  50. a few more large players are still coming,
  51. such as China and Australia.
  52. Now the question really becomes:
  53. How can RTGS support FinTech Innovation?
  54. And here are two use cases
  55. that we’ll now bring to you.
  56. First, peer-to-peer payment.
  57. The whole companies,
  58. such as Venmo in the US,
  59. that are facilitating payment transfer between individuals,
  60. can only fully be successful
  61. if real time payment actually happens.
  62. Think about this,
  63. if you were to split the bill with a friend,
  64. however that bill splitting
  65. and that request to a friend
  66. would take one day or more to clear,
  67. the gratification of being able
  68. to receive the funds straightaway would not happen.
  69. And therefore, the capacity of FinTech startups
  70. to process real time payment,
  71. either from within their account,
  72. or between accounts,
  73. becomes an important value proposition
  74. for their capacity to deliver
  75. a better user experience.
  76. The second example is peer-to-peer lending.
  77. RTGS is actually linked closely
  78. to the development
  79. of the peer-to-peer lending industry in the UK.
  80. You may see peer-to-peer lending as a method
  81. for people to borrow funds
  82. when they’re not credit worthy,
  83. and when banks do not want to lend them.
  84. However, research has shown
  85. that one of the core benefits
  86. of peer-to-peer lending,
  87. is not so much on the credit worthiness
  88. as well as the interest charged on the loan,
  89. but it’s the speed at which
  90. the loan is being delivered on your bank account.
  91. And this for example
  92. has been clearly seen in the UK.
  93. Some peer-to-peer lenders in the UK
  94. charge high interest on the loans they provide,
  95. and one of the questions was,
  96. why do people in the UK
  97. rather go on an online peer-to-peer lending platform
  98. and pay a higher interest rate,
  99. compared to what they would otherwise
  100. qualify on a high street bank?
  101. And the answer was speed and convenience.
  102. Once you are authorised
  103. to get a peer-to-peer loan in the UK,
  104. you would be credited on your account
  105. in the same second.
  106. As a result, the funds would be available
  107. for you to spend straightaway.
  108. And for the capacity of the peer-to-peer lender
  109. to make a bank-to-bank transfer instantly
  110. as soon as the loan has been approved,
  111. has only been made possible
  112. because of real-time gross settlement.
  113. In other words,
  114. RTGS in the context of peer-to-peer lending,
  115. allow for convenient and instant payment
  116. of the loan into the account of the borrower.
  117. Now if we go further,
  118. we can look at the evolution of
  119. payment mechanisms and options,
  120. and this will continue to keep on growing.
  121. For example, most recently
  122. the Bank of England
  123. will be opening the RTGS system
  124. to non-bank Payment Service Provider.
  125. This will further increase competition
  126. and therefore consumer
  127. will allow to have more innovation.
  128. And as FinTech start-ups
  129. will be able to deliver new payments methods.
  130. Not only this,
  131. but this schedule of reform
  132. planned for the UK
  133. will also include how Blockchain and API
  134. can be interfaced with their RTGS systems.
  135. In other words,
  136. RTGS represent a critical infrastructure layer
  137. supporting FinTech innovation
  138. even though you may not directly be aware of it
  139. as it operates in the background
  140. and serves as a payment rail.

2.6.1 The ABCDs of Alternative Finance (Part 1)

  1. In his 2014 letter to JP Morgan’s shareholders,
  2. Jamie Dimon famously issued a warning
  3. to its shareholders, employees
  4. and even traditional competitors.
  5. Silicon Valley is coming.
  6. At the bank’s investor day
  7. he added more colourfully,
  8. when I go to Silicon Valley,
  9. they all want to eat our lunch.
  10. Every single one of them is going to try.
  11. During the last few years,
  12. alternative finance is slowly transforming
  13. the offering of financial services
  14. and the providers of such services
  15. in two main ways.
  16. New business models
  17. and new technologies.
  18. It’s critical to understand
  19. the four key and interrelated technologies
  20. that have allowed alternative finance to flourish.
  21. These can be known as the ABCDs driving FinTech,
  22. namely, artificial intelligence, or AI,
  23. blockchain, cloud computing, and data.
  24. Let’s deal with each of them in reverse order.
  25. Banks for the longest time
  26. have relied upon and generated
  27. a tremendous amount of information.
  28. These include information from and about customers,
  29. their identity, their transactions,
  30. their net worth
  31. and even their relationships and location.
  32. Yet a lot of this has been gathered
  33. by using paper forms
  34. filled by customers and bank staff
  35. and not easily searchable
  36. or manipulated for analysis.
  37. The digitization of information
  38. from paper into data,
  39. from physical pulp to digital ones and “0”s
  40. means that such information
  41. can more easily stored,
  42. transmitted, searched, processed,
  43. analysed and displayed.
  44. This digitization allows for
  45. online capital marketplaces
  46. to be more easily created and operated
  47. where gatherers can more cost effectively
  48. process and analyse the data
  49. for those who need the capital
  50. and then display the relevant information
  51. on the new platforms
  52. for the potential providers of capital
  53. to make their own investment decisions.
  54. At the same time,
  55. digital form filling and tracking
  56. of the online customer activity
  57. allows both these online platforms
  58. as well as, virtual banks and e-brokerages
  59. to scale more quickly with less manual labour
  60. and space resources
  61. that would otherwise be required for,
  62. for example, traditional bank branch networks.
  63. Customer data includes online behaviour
  64. such as the time and location of logging in
  65. and transactions
  66. as well as, other online activities
  67. such as web browsing, e-commerce
  68. and social media use.
  69. Increasingly, offline behaviour
  70. is also being tracked through data
  71. from internet of things for IoT devices
  72. such as wearable smart watches,
  73. smart cars and smart home devices
  74. such as Amazon Echo.
  75. For example, in the world’s biggest retailer, Walmart,
  76. 2.5 petabytes of data every hour is processed.
  77. One petabyte is 10 bytes with 15 “0”s afterwards.
  78. The other has been called the new oil
  79. that is being bought and sold
  80. by gatherers and users
  81. and increasingly fueling the AI engine
  82. that we will discuss subsequently.
  83. In the past, businesses such as financial institutions
  84. had to build their IT systems
  85. using different enterprise level software
  86. that was developed or licenced
  87. at high cost over time
  88. and hosted on large servers on premises.
  89. With the advent of cloud computing,
  90. software resides at data centres on servers
  91. run by companies dedicated managing such servers
  92. which also provide value-added services
  93. such as cyber security protection.
  94. This means that new businesses
  95. such as alternative financial providers
  96. no longer need to dedicate high capital expenditure
  97. to expensive infrastructure
  98. and can focus on improving client experience
  99. and can dynamically scale their server usage
  100. in accordance with their rates of growth.
  101. Cloud computing also allowed
  102. new business models to blossom.
  103. Software as a service or SaaS businesses
  104. bypass the traditional vendor model
  105. of software development and sales
  106. that require marketing software licences
  107. at higher upfront fees
  108. and then again each time a new version
  109. or upgrade is introduced.
  110. Software that resides in the cloud
  111. can now be marketed at a lower upfront cost
  112. based on a subscription model.
  113. And software upgrades can be automatically
  114. made on a continuous basis
  115. which gives the client one less reason
  116. to switch vendors.
  117. This means that online capital marketplaces
  118. as well as, startup virtual banks
  119. like UK Starling Bank
  120. require less upfront cost
  121. to prototype new business models
  122. and user interfaces
  123. to roll out more quickly and cost effectively
  124. and to do the same
  125. when scaling to new jurisdictions.
  126. In addition, the cloud
  127. enables connected IoT devices
  128. to gather data and stream more services
  129. including financial services
  130. to customers through new interfaces
  131. like smart watches,
  132. voice-activated speakers
  133. and smart homes and smart cars.
  134. Cloud computing effectively allows
  135. for institutional level technology support
  136. to retail businesses
  137. whereby user customers can now transact
  138. on their office computer on the road
  139. via their smartphones or smart cars
  140. or at home in their pyjamas.

2.6.2 The ABCDs of Alternative Finance (Part 2)

  1. Distributed ledger technology or DLT
  2. continues to have an evolving impact
  3. on alternative finance.
  4. The oldest example of double-entry bookkeeping
  5. can be found in the publication in 1494
  6. of Franciscan Friar Luca Pacioli
  7. which allowed for reliable documentation
  8. of both creditor and debtor
  9. in a standardised manner.
  10. The white paper on Bitcoin
  11. by the mysterious Satoshi Nakamoto in 2008
  12. was similarly revolutionary
  13. in establishing a cryptocurrency that seeks to solve
  14. the double spending problem
  15. intrinsic in a currency
  16. based on software without the need
  17. of a trusted authority or central server.
  18. Nakamoto postulated a ledger that is distributed
  19. via a peer-to-peer network
  20. which records transactions
  21. by way of blocks.
  22. Each block is validated
  23. by different node computers in the network
  24. through solving cryptographic mathematical puzzles,
  25. or hashes, at which time a new block
  26. with a new cryptographic hash,
  27. timestamp, and data would be added to the chain
  28. which is transparent to all users.
  29. Data on the block is immutable
  30. and effectively cannot be altered
  31. unless all prior blocks in the chain
  32. are altered by consensus
  33. or agreement of the network majority.
  34. Blockchain is the underlying technology
  35. behind cryptocurrencies
  36. that have expanded dramatically beyond Bitcoin.
  37. In this way,
  38. this technology has created
  39. a new form of digital asset
  40. as well as a new alternative finance method
  41. to raise capital for new projects,
  42. with the so-called crypto exchanges
  43. comprising new forms of online capital marketplaces.
  44. In addition, blockchain technology could form
  45. the basis of new capital markets infrastructure.
  46. NASDAQ is using blockchain technologies
  47. to secure record keeping of ownership
  48. of private companies and transfers.
  49. In December 2017,
  50. Australia’s ASX announced that it would replace
  51. its stock exchange registry,
  52. settlement and clearing system
  53. with blockchain technology.
  54. An even more ambitious use of blockchain technology
  55. through the creation
  56. of distributed autonomous organisations or DAOs
  57. that allow for automatic execution
  58. upon specific conditions
  59. via smart contracts,
  60. with innovative governance mechanisms
  61. based on direct voting and consensus.
  62. If implemented to its fullest extent,
  63. DAOs and its efforts to disintermediate
  64. could impact not only the venture capital market
  65. but also the very concept of the joint stock company
  66. and even some functions of government.
  67. The term artificial intelligence was coined
  68. at the now famous Dartmouth summer research workshop in 1955.
  69. Two initial clarifications would be helpful.
  70. First, it should be noted that
  71. we refer to the narrow or weak AI
  72. that relates to algorithm performing specific tasks,
  73. as opposed to general or strong AI
  74. that reflect broader human intelligence
  75. and decision-making.
  76. Second, there are different strands within AI,
  77. including Natural Language Processing or NLP
  78. which relates to language, often written,
  79. and Machine Learning where
  80. systems learn from experience
  81. by being trained with data
  82. as opposed to being rules-based.
  83. There are many techniques within Machine Learning,
  84. including neural networks which comprise
  85. nodes of weighted interconnectedness
  86. inspired by the human brain, and deep learning,
  87. which refers to algorithms based on neural networks
  88. arranged in deeper layers.
  89. Computer vision for image recognition
  90. is a good example of machine learning.
  91. After a long so-called AI winter of lacklustre activity,
  92. AI has blossomed due to
  93. a confluence of events
  94. that made both Fortune and Forbes
  95. name 2017 the year of AI.
  96. First, instead of expensive large supercomputers
  97. required for the AI algorithmic processing,
  98. researchers started using
  99. relatively cheap graphical processing units or GPUs
  100. originally developed for video games
  101. with increased computational power
  102. when used in parallel.
  103. Second, data storage cost continues to fall
  104. while data is being gathering at alarming rates
  105. through online activity and connected devices,
  106. thereby allowing for
  107. more structured and unstructured data
  108. to be gathered, stored, and used
  109. to train the machines.
  110. Third, most major cloud companies,
  111. such as Amazon’s AWS, Google Cloud,
  112. Microsoft’s Azure, IBM Cloud
  113. and Alibaba’s Aliyun
  114. incorporate AI into their services,
  115. often including machine learning frameworks
  116. on some open source basis
  117. to allow their clients to experiment
  118. and incorporate into their operations.
  119. For example, AI is rapidly changing
  120. Alternative Finance user interfaces,
  121. from facial and voice recognition
  122. for biometric identity management
  123. to chatbots that can provide
  124. personalised recommendations.
  125. Algorithmic matching of needs,
  126. pricing, and predictive analytics are also being used.
  127. AI also allows some alternative finance companies
  128. to create new business models
  129. that focus on analytics of the customer data
  130. rather than building platforms
  131. to provide financial fund flow.
  132. For example, some Chinese companies
  133. which started primarily as P2P lending companies
  134. have pivoted to provide credit analysis and scoring
  135. that serve the marketplace lending
  136. conducted by institutional investors and lenders.

2.7 Building A New Stack

  1. In the 19th Century, we developed
  2. the current payment stack
  3. based upon physical money like gold,
  4. paper money representing gold,
  5. instruments like securities,
  6. negotiable instruments, checks,
  7. all cleared through a banking system
  8. that collected fund transfers
  9. and settled across the accounts of a central bank
  10. which was responsible for the monetary supply.
  11. Across the 20th Century,
  12. we digitised this system.
  13. We transformed it from a system
  14. which was based on physical analogue elements
  15. to a digital system of payments,
  16. of eventually, mobile payments, RTGS systems,
  17. and digital electronic payments
  18. totalling over five trillion US dollars a day.
  19. At the beginning of the 21st Century,
  20. we are applying a process of datafication,
  21. applying techniques of big data,
  22. artificial intelligence and other mechanisms
  23. to use the digitised data to transform
  24. the payment experience to provide
  25. a seamless mobile lifestyle experience.
  26. We however are now going further.
  27. We are looking at developing
  28. an entirely new payment stack
  29. through the use of technology.
  30. Technology today is no longer a barrier
  31. to redesigning a payment system.
  32. Instead, our existing institutional frameworks
  33. are the barrier and, looking forward,
  34. we can easily see an increasing use of
  35. real-time payments
  36. which could either take place
  37. across the banking system
  38. or new entrants like tech companies
  39. or directly eliminating the role of banks themselves,
  40. the idea of disintermediation,
  41. and we are actually seeing
  42. these processes in real life in the context of China
  43. with Alipay, with WeChat Pay,
  44. in the context of the UK
  45. and an increasing number of jurisdictions
  46. with faster payment systems
  47. allowing real-time, instantaneous payments
  48. between individuals and companies
  49. both through the banking system
  50. or outside of the banking system,
  51. and, looking forward,
  52. we can imagine
  53. how this fundamental payment infrastructure
  54. can be the base of a new stack based on payment
  55. supporting e-commerce,
  56. supporting lending,
  57. supporting investment.
  58. If we think of e-commerce,
  59. one of the fundamental killer apps to e-commerce
  60. is simple, digital, and in particular,
  61. mobile-based payment systems.
  62. On the basis of those mobile-based payment systems,
  63. one can also build digital lending frameworks
  64. both for consumer lending for individuals,
  65. as well as for small and medium-size enterprises
  66. using data analytics technology to collate data
  67. to do real-time cashflow analysis
  68. to determine credit worthiness
  69. and finally, pulling all of these pieces together,
  70. we can build systems of investment,
  71. systems which allow both individuals
  72. as well as the wider economy
  73. to better invest their funds
  74. in order to support the development of the economy.
  75. On the basis of this framework,
  76. we are also seeing
  77. an increasing number of jurisdictions
  78. led by the European Union
  79. developing what is called open banking.
  80. Open banking allows access to banking data
  81. and banking infrastructure by new entrants,
  82. by tech firms, by startups,
  83. and this poses a major challenge
  84. to the traditional model of banking
  85. and, at the same time as we look at digitising
  86. and datafying the system,
  87. we are also looking at new technologies like blockchain,
  88. and new forms of money and payment
  89. like digital currencies or cryptocurrencies.

2.8 Cryptocurrencies

  1. Hi there, time to talk about
  2. the very exciting topic of cryptocurrencies.
  3. You’re probably hearing of the cryptocurrencies
  4. in the news or the newspapers,
  5. or your friends are talking about it.
  6. And there’s definitely a lot of buzz around
  7. the broader cryptocurrency space globally.
  8. But first, what is a cryptocurrency?
  9. Well, a cryptocurrency is a digital asset
  10. that serves as a medium of exchange or store value
  11. that is generally based on a blockchain
  12. and you use cryptography as its security feature.
  13. What makes it quite interesting
  14. is that in many cases,
  15. it is not issued by any central authority
  16. rendering it in, let’s say,
  17. immune to government interference or manipulation.
  18. Well, what’s important to understand
  19. is actually cryptocurrencies are nothing new.
  20. People have been working on the topics
  21. since 1980s or ’90s.
  22. Many active groups were related to the military
  23. or more crypto-focused groups, like one of the,
  24. like the Cyberpunks, for example.
  25. But really, the big game-changer took place
  26. towards the end of 2008,
  27. where an individual, or a group of individuals,
  28. we don’t know yet, called Satoshi Nakamoto,
  29. published a white paper called
  30. “Bitcoin, A Peer-to-Peer Electronic Cash System”,
  31. which was really the basis of Bitcoin.
  32. And Bitcoin was born.
  33. And a lot of things have happened since then,
  34. yeah, more today,
  35. more than 1,500 different cryptocurrencies in the market.
  36. But what was really innovative with Bitcoin is
  37. that it solved one of the big problems
  38. that we had at that point,
  39. which was the double-spending issue,
  40. the risk that a digital currency can be spent twice.
  41. Think about it, if I send you an email,
  42. there’s a copy of that email on my computer,
  43. and a copy on yours.
  44. Well, that’s practical for emails,
  45. that doesn’t go well
  46. if you’re actually sending money to somebody else.
  47. Or else, there’ll be an endless supply of money.
  48. And the beauty of Bitcoin is it was finally able
  49. to solve that double-spending problem.
  50. And that was a big game-changer.
  51. Over the years, as obviously Bitcoin has gathered
  52. increasingly more traction,
  53. it has its ups and downs,
  54. from a volatility perspective,
  55. or even government and public’s attention to focus on.
  56. But many would argue that the last year, 2017,
  57. was really the year that Bitcoin

  59. aimed to enter the mainstream and generate
  60. the really large amount of public awareness
  61. And many would say that
  62. we probably passed now the point of no return

Industry Showcase (NEW*): Introduction to Digital Asset Market – Gatecoin

  1. Hi, everyone.
  2. I’m Brad, the Chief Operating Officer of Gatecoin.
  3. Gatecoin is a a Bitcoin and Ethereum token exchange
  4. designed for professional and retail investors.
  5. Today, I wanted to spend some time delving
  6. into a few topics around cryptocurrency exchanges.
  7. You might be wondering to yourself,
  8. why do cryptocurrency exchanges exist?
  9. So, for me, similar to stock exchanges,
  10. which issue in primary marketplaces securities
  11. to buy and sell,
  12. a crypto-marketplace is a place
  13. for people to buy and sell digital assets.
  14. Likewise, similar to a traditional exchange,
  15. where companies have the ability to raise new money
  16. through issuing securities or debt,
  17. a digital market exchange is a place where,
  18. through using instruments such as ICOs,
  19. companies can also raise money
  20. through pre-selling their revenue.
  21. Essentially, providing early stage companies
  22. and start-ups, a framework to raise money
  23. while avoiding regulatory compliance
  24. and other intermediaries, such as venture capitalists,
  25. banks, or stock exchanges.
  26. The regulatory attitudes and frameworks
  27. on both exchanges and ICOs
  28. vary wildly around the world.
  29. However, each exchange also has its own specific rules
  30. and regulations for trading and listing.
  31. While these standards often vary wildly
  32. from exchange to exchange,
  33. projects will need to meet specific criteria
  34. to foot the individual exchange
  35. before they actually list their tokens.
  36. ICOs of companies are done in a primary market,
  37. directly between projects and their investors,
  38. whereas subsequent trading in tokens
  39. and other digital assets, such as Bitcoin and Ethereum
  40. are executed in a secondary market
  41. directly between traders.
  42. The secondary market is
  43. often the most crucial component of the market,
  44. as the supply and demand in these markets
  45. drives the liquidity and the pricing of the tokens
  46. and other digital assets.
  47. There are different types of digital assets.
  48. So, from my perspective,
  49. there are two types of exchanges:
  50. a decentralised exchange and a centralised exchange.
  51. At Gatecoin, we’re a centralised exchange.
  52. That means everyone’s together.
  53. We have one central authority
  54. where your users will come on-board and trade with.
  55. A decentralised exchange is where users
  56. are actually generally working peer-to-peer,
  57. and there is no one central authority creating the rules
  58. or managing the system.
  59. To put it simply, while there’s a decentralised exchange
  60. and a centralised exchange,
  61. there’s also one differentiating facet.
  62. There’s the crypto to crypto and the fiat to crypto.
  63. In a crypto to crypto exchange,
  64. people credit their crypto onto the exchange,
  65. and they can trade between each other, crypto assets.
  66. In a fiat to crypto, people credit their fiat,
  67. or their real-world money such as Euros,
  68. Australian dollars, Hedge KD, or USD onto the exchange,
  69. and then they convert it
  70. or they buy and sell the digital assets.
  71. And they can also do it to crypto to crypto trading.
  72. So, how does a digital asset exchange differ
  73. from a traditional stock exchange?
  74. Here, we’ll have to look at it
  75. into three main characteristics.
  76. Custody, settlement, and trading-outs.
  77. For custody and settlement,
  78. a traditional stock exchange will act as the executor
  79. when the trade happens.
  80. However, custody and clearing are dependent
  81. on a broad network of trust partners, such as banks
  82. and other custodians and clearing houses,
  83. who will hold the assets on behalf of the traders.
  84. At this point, there are no regulatory requirements,
  85. and the fact that the industry’s still in
  86. it’s stage of development,
  87. digital exchange act more as a marketplace
  88. than an exchange, doing all three elements of execution,
  89. custodian, and clearing on each and every transaction.
  90. When considering trading-outs,
  91. traditional stock exchanges often begin
  92. at 9:30 in the morning
  93. and end at 4:00 p.m. at the end of the day.
  94. Digital asset exchanges trade for 24 hours,
  95. 7 days a week.
  96. Time in clearing is also a major difference,
  97. with the traditional exchanges often clearing
  98. on a T-plus-one or a T-plus-three cycle.
  99. Now, what that means is today plus three days,
  100. or today plus one day.
  101. Clearing, in a digital exchange,
  102. can nearly be instantaneous.
  103. There are different types of tokens.
  104. At the moment, there is no unified
  105. classification for tokens.
  106. However, we can still broadly classify tokens into several types:
  107. security, utility, real cryptocurrencies,
  108. which we could also call payment tokens,
  109. and potentially also, asset-backed.
  110. Simply put, securities tokens represent assets such as
  111. participation in real physical underlying companies,
  112. assets, earning streams, or an entitlement to dividends
  113. or interest payments.
  114. In terms of their economic function,
  115. the tokens are similar to equities, bonds or derivatives.
  116. The issuance of a security token is often called an SEO.
  117. When considering if a token is either a security
  118. or a utility, the key test will often be tax.
  119. Consider the token if it pays dividends, interest,
  120. or allows the claim of rights of ownership
  121. in the company or an underlying asset.
  122. If so, it’s likely to be a security
  123. and regulated in Hong Kong
  124. with a regulator such as the SFC.
  125. On the other hand, a utility token gives holders
  126. the access to services provided by the project,
  127. essentially prepaying
  128. or pre-purchasing the future project
  129. or service the company creates or delivers.
  130. Unlike securities tokens, utility tokens are not actually
  131. designed as investments,
  132. and this is why they do not currently fall under
  133. any regulatory restrictions yet.
  134. Other key questions is do the projects pay tax
  135. on the revenue of the funds raised on the projects
  136. or the projects to be delivered or services rendered?
  137. If so, it’s also likely to be a utility token.
  138. Cryptocurrencies or payment tokens.
  139. These are based on fundamental blockchains
  140. such as Bitcoin and Ethereum,
  141. and is often considered as a means of payment.
  142. Usually the tokens of these categories
  143. have no further functions or links
  144. to other developmental projects.
  145. Speaking broadly, the purpose of cryptocurrency
  146. is to be an item of inherent value.
  147. For instance, to cash or gold.
  148. They are simply designed to enable an exchange,
  149. be it a purchase, sales, or other financial transaction.
  150. They are intended to provide
  151. many of the same functions
  152. as long established currencies
  153. such as the USD, Euro or Yen.
  154. They do not have the backing of any government
  155. or any other other body.
  156. What makes a good exchange?
  157. There are many elements to look out for
  158. before establishing an account
  159. on a digital asset exchange.
  160. However, there are key elements I’d consider
  161. you should pay careful attention to first.
  162. We fundamentally believe
  163. there are two kinds of exchanges,
  164. those that have been hacked
  165. and those that will be hacked in the future.
  166. By the very nature of the industry that we’re part of,
  167. the people will keep trying
  168. to take advantage of security weaknesses.
  169. So don’t be surprised, be prepared.
  170. It’s very important to understand the security
  171. of the company and ask questions like,
  172. do you have a dedicated security team?
  173. How do you manage a platform?
  174. What are the security procedures or SOPs
  175. in your company to prevent you from being hacked?
  176. How can I remove my holdings off the exchange
  177. and back onto my own wallet?
  178. A higher liquidity in the marketplace is preferred
  179. since it brings about the following advantages,
  180. better and fairer prices for everyone.
  181. Market stability, quicker transactions,
  182. an increased accuracy for technical analysis.
  183. Also be aware that recent developments have shown
  184. that it’s possible for an exchange
  185. to take advantage or fake their liquidity.
  186. So also be cautious, and if it’s an exchange
  187. that’s gone from being a very low liquidity
  188. to one of the deepest markets in the world overnight,
  189. then ask yourself, does it all make sense?
  190. Finally, it’s clear that Bitcoin and Ethereum
  191. and many other cryptocurrencies are opening the doors
  192. to a new world of digital asset which we think
  193. has the potential to someday become
  194. a leading currency around the world.
  195. At the moment, even the oldest of cryptocurrencies
  196. are still maturing and only time will tell
  197. where this new asset class is heading.
  198. From what we can tell, there’s plenty of room
  199. for advancement both as an asset class
  200. and the market infrastructure needed to exchange it.
  201. Regardless of your predictions for the future,
  202. it’s hard to ignore that Bitcoin
  203. has already revolutionised the digital world.

Industry Showcase (NEW): Stablecoins (Feron Labs)

  1. Hi everyone, my name is Dmitri Senchenko
  2. and I’m the founder of Feron Labs.
  3. Today I am going to talk about cryptocurrencies
  4. with price stabilisation mechanisms,
  5. also known as stablecoins.
  6. Before we get into price stabilisation,
  7. let’s have a look at what makes for good money.
  8. The classic answer to this question
  9. is that money needs to satisfy three core functions,
  10. it needs to perform well as a medium of exchange,
  11. store of value and unit of account.
  12. So how do cryptocurrencies measure up?
  13. When talking about “medium of exchange”,
  14. we refer primarily to the ability to transact.
  15. Existing blockchains provide this functionality
  16. at a basic level, and the challenge today
  17. is to achieve commercial-grade speed
  18. and scalability without sacrificing decentralisation.
  19. A lot of talent and capital is being deployed
  20. at this problem, and significant progress
  21. is expected in the very near future.
  22. In terms of store of value, current technology by
  23. and large already allows cryptographically secure storage
  24. and retrieval of assets within a robust
  25. and durable distributed architecture.
  26. Ability to serve as a unit of account is linked directly
  27. to price stability, and this is arguably
  28. where cryptocurrencies are at their worst.
  29. Here, we enter the domain of monetary policy.
  30. All cryptocurrencies have one by definition,
  31. but they are quite primitive and obviously fail
  32. at their task.
  33. This is where stablecoins come in.
  34. They seek to achieve price stability
  35. through sophisticated monetary policy,
  36. and to implement it on blockchain protocols
  37. that fulfil the “medium of exchange”
  38. and “store of value” functions.
  39. This combination has the potential
  40. to become truly usable money,
  41. and to compete against conventional currencies
  42. in practically every facet of the economy.
  43. This goal is extremely ambitious,
  44. but there is no shortage of tech talent
  45. or venture capital ready to back it.
  46. Stablecoins are an extremely young and diverse field
  47. of research, but the majority of approaches
  48. to date can be divided into three categories.
  49. First, we have the fiat-collateralised models.
  50. You place fiat currency on deposit
  51. at a conventional financial institutions,
  52. issue a corresponding amount of stablecoins
  53. and institute a fixed exchange ratio
  54. between the two at the level of your target price.
  55. Stability is achieved through direct arbitrage
  56. between the market price and the exchange ratio.
  57. Such coins are, indeed, extremely stable,
  58. but they give up all pretence of decentralisation.
  59. They are completely hostage to the institution
  60. that holds their collateral and are little more
  61. than a technological extension to fiat money,
  62. much like a debit card.
  63. Next, we have the crypto-collateralised models.
  64. Instead of keeping fiat in a bank,
  65. you lock other cryptocurrencies in a smart contract.
  66. Now, you are dealing with volatile collateral
  67. that is no longer the same as the asset
  68. against which you’re stabilising.
  69. This has two major consequences.
  70. First, you can no longer have a fixed exchange ratio
  71. between your collateral and your stablecoin.
  72. Instead, you need to know how much your collateral
  73. is worth at any given time
  74. and to adjust the ratio accordingly.
  75. This is done by means of a price feed,
  76. which is a tool that delivers external market data
  77. into the blockchain.
  78. Secondly, you need to hold a substantial amount
  79. of excess collateral as protection against a decline
  80. in its value.
  81. Having to hold these risky assets
  82. through all market conditions carries a cost,
  83. which has to be levied somewhere within the system.
  84. Early results suggest that crypto-collateralized models
  85. can achieve stability under some conditions,
  86. but the key question is whether the cost
  87. of this stability can be made low enough
  88. for large-scale issuance and adoption.
  89. Finally, we have the uncollateralised models.
  90. Most of them build on the theory that a change in price
  91. can be counteracted by an appropriate change
  92. in money supply.
  93. For instance, if price declines by 10% and money supply
  94. is then shrunk by the same 10%,
  95. price should eventually revert to its original level.
  96. A simplified example of such a mechanism involves a pair
  97. of internally generated assets, a stablecoin
  98. and a volatile coin.
  99. The latter is an asset
  100. that confers some sort of a financial benefit,
  101. on its holder like interest or dividends,
  102. but is not price-stable.
  103. You then have a smart contract that receives signals
  104. from a price feed and offers to exchange appropriate amounts
  105. of stablecoins for volatile coins and vice versa,
  106. thereby shrinking or expanding money supply.
  107. While avoiding use of external assets
  108. can bring significant benefits, the trade-off here
  109. is reliance on very strong behavioural assumptions,
  110. leading to questions about stability
  111. and proneness to death spirals.
  112. In December of 2018,
  113. US regulators placed severe restrictions
  114. on algorithmic issuance of volatile coin-like securities,
  115. forcing a number of projects to rethink their mechanism.
  116. No stablecoin of this type has been launched to date.
  117. Feron Labs works on distinct approach
  118. within the uncollateralised category.
  119. We do not define stability relative to an external asset,
  120. or set an absolute price target.
  121. It is a single-coin model that consists
  122. of a native algorithmic central bank
  123. that offers users a range of riskless deposits.
  124. This opportunity to earn a riskless return attracts
  125. a significant portion of the system’s speculative liquidity.
  126. When price expectations decline
  127. and users want to sell their coins,
  128. they will first need to withdraw them from deposits.
  129. As the central bank detects increased outflows of capital,
  130. it sharply raised interest rates,
  131. offering a strong incentive to keep coins on deposit.
  132. Thus, liquidity is prevented from reaching exchanges
  133. and turning into sell orders, alleviating price pressure.
  134. In effect, price volatility
  135. is turned into the more benign interest rate volatility.
  136. So what is the ultimate goal of stablecoins?
  137. Arguably, it is creating a model that can function
  138. as fully self-sovereign money
  139. – money that is just as usable as conventional currency,
  140. but one that you, as the holder of the encryption key,
  141. truly own and control.
  142. It would be money that no institution
  143. can take away from you.
  144. Every transfer from your account would occur only if
  145. and when you give it your consent.
  146. In a way, it is the original Bitcoin dream,
  147. and Bitcoin’s early popularity
  148. has shown that it is a widely shared one.

Legal and Regulatory Implications of Cryptocurrencies (King & Wood Mallesons)

  1. Cryptocurrency is
  2. one of the most over-hyped terms of our generation.
  3. Cryptocurrency is also a bit of a misnomer.
  4. Very few cryptocurrencies
  5. operate in any way that resembles
  6. fiat or real central bank-backed currency.
  7. None are recognised in Hong Kong
  8. as currency or money.
  9. So some people like to call them virtual commodities,
  10. cryptographic tokens or digital assets,
  11. which are far more neutral,
  12. but we’ll stick with cryptocurrencies today.
  13. Let’s take a step back.
  14. What we typically mean
  15. when we talk about cryptocurrencies
  16. is a record of some kind of right,
  17. typically on a blockchain.
  18. That right can be transferred peer-to-peer,
  19. or via an exchange.
  20. It’s incredibly secure,
  21. but generally it’s not anonymous.
  22. It’s pseudonymous.
  23. So what does a cryptocurrency do?
  24. What is it?
  25. It entirely depends on
  26. what right travels along that blockchain
  27. as ownership passes from one person to another.
  28. In some cases, that right is really limited,
  29. particularly for cryptocurrencies like Bitcoin.
  30. In other cases, it could give you a profit,
  31. or even represent a share in a company,
  32. could be a loyalty point or help you
  33. make payments across the world.
  34. The combination of blockchain
  35. and smart contract technology,
  36. on platforms like Ethereum,
  37. as well as many others,
  38. has made the creation and trading of these rights
  39. incredibly efficient and lucrative
  40. as an early stage funding tool,
  41. a bit like what Kickstarter was
  42. for physical products.
  43. However, there is often a misconception
  44. that new technology,
  45. especially when it lives and breathes online,
  46. and particularly when it is decentralised,
  47. is not subject to any laws.
  48. It lives in the cloud, so no laws apply.
  49. This couldn’t be further from the truth.
  50. In fact, it typically means
  51. that much more law applies,
  52. and it often overlaps and sometimes even conflicts.
  53. At the crux is what is the right
  54. that I am recording on the blockchain?
  55. And this can have
  56. a number of legal and regulatory implications.
  57. However, the key things you need to look at
  58. are financial regulatory laws,
  59. such as restrictions on selling securities.
  60. In Hong Kong,
  61. an important part of the definition of securities
  62. includes collective investment schemes,
  63. which is a very broad concept
  64. and exists in many jurisdictions.
  65. It can capture a wide range of arrangements,
  66. but in essence, it generally refers
  67. to where you draw money in
  68. to generate profit for participants.
  69. In some countries, the securities definitions
  70. are so broad and flexible
  71. that they can adapt
  72. based on market behaviour and intent.
  73. The United States is a great example of that.
  74. In others, more law is emerging
  75. that expands the securities regimes.
  76. And in others such as Mainland China,
  77. total bans are in place for ICOs,
  78. but not necessarily blockchain-based shares
  79. and other instruments.
  80. Certain cryptocurrencies are regulated in other ways.
  81. For example, a gambling chip on a blockchain
  82. is still a gambling chip.
  83. An exchange selling cryptocurrencies that are securities
  84. is still a securities exchange that requires a licence.
  85. A blockchain that records
  86. and transmits personal data,
  87. is still regulated by privacy laws.
  88. In certain countries,
  89. cryptocurrencies are also treated like currency,
  90. which carries a lot of other consequences.
  91. But there are numerous other laws applied,
  92. such as contract and fraud.
  93. So if someone lies to you in an offer document,
  94. or over-promises on something
  95. that they cannot deliver,
  96. you could potentially sue them.
  97. And we are already seeing a number private actions,
  98. and in some countries even class actions in this space.
  99. Tax and anti-money laundering laws also apply.
  100. The greater issue is how these laws interact.
  101. Blockchains typically exist in multiple countries,
  102. because their records are
  103. duplicated in different servers
  104. in different parts of the world.
  105. So extreme care is required in creating them,
  106. and we’ll see a lot more interesting law
  107. develop to resolve laws that collide.

Industry Showcase (NEW): Behaviour Analysis and Valuation of Cryptocurrencies (Santiment)

  1. Hello, my name is Maksim,
  2. and I’m a Founder and CEO of Santiment.
  3. Our goal as a company is
  4. to give our users 360 degree overview
  5. of the entire crypto market
  6. and its biggest driving forces.
  7. Now, since crypto markets are so novel,
  8. and the technology behind it so unique,
  9. I believe we can no longer rely
  10. just on the traditional metrics and fundamental data.
  11. So what do we do at Santiment?
  12. We supply our users with terabytes of
  13. related data
  14. for the cryptocurrency from various sources.
  15. What are the sources?
  16. For instance, we gather data
  17. directly from the blockchains,
