  28. to visualise different market behaviours.
  29. Today, I’ll show you a few of these metrics
  30. that probably no one else has shown you before.
  31. And first, for the quick disclaimer,
  32. I’m not an financial advisor,
  33. and nothing I share with you today
  34. should be taken as direct investment advice.
  35. It is just to give you real-life data
  36. to show the case.
  37. The crypto market has been in a long term,
  38. like more than one year,
  39. bear market, or a correction.
  40. Some people call it correction.
  41. Valuing crypto assets is a very challenging task.
  42. For, such as most tokens or coins,
  43. are supposed to have a primary purpose,
  44. more than just trading,
  45. like to have to be some utility.
  46. Unfortunately, as it’s still not the case,
  47. majority of the projects struggle in this mass adoption,
  48. and it is very focused on the crypto community,
  49. and the use of the tokens are still mostly speculative,
  50. and this situation can be very hard to accurately
  51. valuate a crypto asset.
  52. From market projections or valuations,
  53. you can see more clearly,
  54. and in the lifetime, what’s happening to the project.
  55. Just like in traditional markets,
  56. when humans deal with things of value,
  57. they become greedy or they can experience fear,
  58. like in crypto we call it FOMO,
  59. fear of missing out,
  60. but they rarely stay put
  61. and act rationally.
  62. In the crypto, as interactions
  63. get recorded on the blockchain,
  64. so, whenever people act,
  65. everything’s recorded on blockchain,
  66. and it’s a unique chance, the first time in history,
  67. then we can analyse,
  68. and this data is available to everyone.
  69. It’s just a question how you
  70. gather and package together.
  71. So let’s start with some examples,
  72. and the first example will be
  73. of how metric is so-called MVRV Ratio.
  74. MVRV Ratio, it’s calculated by dividing
  75. Total Market Cap by so-called Realised Cap.
  76. And the Realised Cap, it’s a very unique measurement.
  77. It’s total acquisition cost,
  78. or the total amount of money
  79. that all token holders paid
  80. for the tokens they currently hold in their wallets.
  81. And the ratio between current market value
  82. and acquisition cost is MVRV Ratio.
  83. So here is a MVRV Ratio for Bitcoin.
  84. The ratio itself is in yellow,
  85. and the price is in green.
  86. As you see, in the moment,
  87. the MVRV is less than one,
  88. actually around zero, comma, 85,
  89. which means there’s a current valuation,
  90. of the entire network,
  91. is less than the amount the token holders paid for it.
  92. Think for a moment.
  93. It’s kind of irrational.
  94. People paid more what market now values as the price,
  95. but this is the reality,
  96. and this is not the first time.
  97. Looking back in the history,
  98. you can see on the chart,
  99. and we saw MVRV fell below one
  100. at the end of 2014,
  101. when the Bitcoin dived below 300,
  102. and stayed there for actually quite long time,
  103. almost one year,
  104. and in the range of
  105. between 200 and 300 dollar per Bitcoin.
  106. As we know now,
  107. that time was perhaps
  108. one of the best accumulation periods.
  109. So if you believe, and we do believe,
  110. that crypto markets are in a cyclical mode,
  111. similar to traditional markets,
  112. here again, we need to remember
  113. the cycles in cryptos are much faster and tighter,
  114. MVRV right now, dived below one,
  115. somewhere back in November,
  116. 2018, when the Bitcoin went below,
  117. all the way down to three and a half thousand.
  118. If we believe market cycles,
  119. and we see the cycle appearing,
  120. alone, this metric can give you
  121. much deeper insight and understanding
  122. of behaviour of the crypto currency,
  123. token like Bitcoin.
  124. And they have this metric, actually,
  125. for other coins too.
  126. Now, let’s look at some other metric.
  127. We call it Ethereum Miners Statistics.
  128. It’s a completely new way
  129. to measure the behaviour of network participants.
  130. You will never find it in traditional finance.
  131. This chart illustrates activity
  132. of Miners of the Ethereum network.
  133. The green line is the price, again,
  134. is newest dollar per Ethereum token,
  135. and the red line is a total balance
  136. of all Ethereum Miners.
  137. It means how much Ethereum tokens
  138. they hold in their balances.
  139. Now, if you look carefully, you will see that,
  140. after year of stagnation in a long bear market,
  141. the market changed,
  142. and the price of Ethereum started to increasing
  143. back in 2017.
  144. Now, typically, when that happens,
  145. a rational person would think,
  146. “Oh. price is increasing, of course,
  147. so I’ll keep it; I will become rich,”
  148. but this is not how the human mind works.
  149. You’ve experienced one year of depression,
  150. and one year of suffering,
  151. and you’ve seen your money,
  152. like, decreasing in value,
  153. so the first direction,
  154. how you emotionally do,
  155. emotion like irrational,
  156. and this describes your behaviour,
  157. you’ll start selling, and dumping,
  158. like to get some money back.
  159. And you see it on the chart
  160. directly how it happens.
  161. The price starts increasing,
  162. but the Ethereum Miners holdings are decreasing,
  163. and it happens for some time, actually,
  164. for three or four months.
  165. Well I call it,
  166. and it is actually a quite known term,
  167. there is so-called Wall of Worry.
  168. Wall of Worry, where market grows in value,
  169. but people still don’t believe in it.
  170. But few months later,
  171. the price still keeps rising,
  172. and you see, the Miners were like,
  173. “Wow, now I guess it’s something for real,”
  174. and their holdings start increasing,
  175. from some specific point of time.
  176. If you’ll very carefully look at the chart,
  177. you’ll see that’s exactly the time when
  178. the price of Ethereum coin
  179. has some kind of lost and dropped.
  180. And after this, when it start increasing,
  181. their holdings for Ethereum Miners were increasing.
  182. This is the moment of recognitions.
  183. The whole market recognised
  184. there is something really big going on,
  185. and ever since I see the chart,
  186. the holding of Ethereum Miners only increasing.
  187. So, as you can see,
  188. Santiment data can be used by anyone
  189. who wants to understand
  190. how crypto markets work,
  191. how human natural participants
  192. react to market changes,
  193. and it’s both educational and pragmatic.

2.9A What is Blockchain? (Part 1)

  1. Cryptocurrencies, blockchain, ICOs.
  2. These are three terms
  3. that are in the headlines daily all over the world.
  4. Blockchain is the underlying technology
  5. which came to prominence with
  6. the launch of Bitcoin in 2009,
  7. but what is blockchain?
  8. Blockchain combines two long-standing
  9. technological developments.
  10. On one side, distributed ledger technology,
  11. and on the other, cryptography.
  12. If we look at Bitcoin, if we look at cryptocurrencies,
  13. cryptocurrencies at their base
  14. are blockchain systems combining
  15. distributed ledger systems and cryptography.
  16. Distributed ledger system,
  17. what is a distributed ledger system?
  18. For a system like Bitcoin,
  19. the distributed ledger
  20. means that the information in the system
  21. are not stored in one single place.
  22. Rather, they exist in multiple locations,
  23. multiple identical ledgers
  24. throughout the users of the system.
  25. So, if we think about this idea of ledgers,
  26. the traditional example is to think
  27. of something like a bank.
  28. A bank is a place where
  29. a certain amount of money is stored,
  30. it is a single place,
  31. it is a silo, it is a single ledger.
  32. At the other extreme, are distributed ledgers.
  33. Distributed ledgers mean that there is no single place
  34. where the information, the valuables,
  35. the data are stored,
  36. rather they are stored
  37. across a variety of identical locations.
  38. In between these structures of
  39. centralised and distributed,
  40. we also have network-based structures
  41. where perhaps you have a single centralised structure
  42. and a variety of spokes,
  43. a hub and spoke structure
  44. whereby the individual spokes connect to the hub.
  45. So, distributed ledger technology
  46. combined with cryptography.
  47. Cryptography is a technology that involves
  48. the secure storage,
  49. the encryption of information.
  50. It has a very long history with important points
  51. going back to code breaking,
  52. particularly in the Second World War.
  53. If we combine distributed ledger technology
  54. with cryptography, we have a system
  55. of secure distributed ledgers
  56. where entries have to be proven,
  57. proven through the use of a variety of structures
  58. which then encrypt the data into blocks.
  59. So, transactions 1 through 50,
  60. packaged in a block, encrypted together.
  61. The next set of transactions build on that first block,
  62. transactions 51 through 100
  63. encrypted as a second block.
  64. This structure provides
  65. a number of very important attributes
  66. to a blockchain-based system.
  67. In particular, it provides for security.
  68. The layers of cryptography across multiple blocks
  69. make it very hard, but importantly not impossible,
  70. to necessarily break those blocks
  71. making blockchain potentially a highly secure system.
  72. Second, it’s a permanent system.
  73. In other words, each of those transactions
  74. is recorded permanently in each of those blocks.
  75. That means that there is always a traceable history
  76. of all of the financial transactions
  77. going back to the very beginning.
  78. So, with each Bitcoin,
  79. you can trace back the life of that Bitcoin
  80. from it’s creation and into each account
  81. that it has been transferred to over time.
  82. And finally, transparency.
  83. Transparency means that the combination of visibility
  84. allows you to see what is happening in the blockchain.
  85. This combination of security, permanence,
  86. transparency, makes blockchain a potentially
  87. very powerful platform technology
  88. across a number of areas.

2.9B What is Blockchain? (Part 2)

  1. Now, if we look at blockchains,
  2. within this general structure
  3. we will often have a third level added.
  4. So, DLT plus cryptography plus smart contracts.
  5. What are smart contracts?
  6. Smart contracts are automated systems
  7. that on the occurrence of pre-determined actions
  8. something else happens.
  9. If I provide A, you provide B
  10. we pre-agree that A and B will be added together
  11. to create a new C,
  12. and this occurs on an automatic basis,
  13. this is a smart contract.
  14. There is an old joke that smart contracts
  15. are neither smart nor contracts,
  16. they are not smart because they are automated,
  17. they happen automatically,
  18. on the occurrence of something / events.
  19. And they are not necessarily contracts,
  20. but that is a more complicated legal question for later.
  21. Within this idea of blockchain,
  22. we can also add in a second important determination.
  23. Blockchains can either be
  24. permissionless, or permissioned.
  25. A permissionless blockchain, like bitcoin,
  26. means that it is open,
  27. anyone can participate that downloads the software.
  28. You download the software, you become a node,
  29. you’ll have a full picture of the ledger,
  30. that distributed ledger is distributed to your node,
  31. anyone can enter.
  32. But, we also have what are called
  33. permissioned blockchains.
  34. A permissioned blockchain,
  35. involves requirements or governance structures
  36. or restrictions on entry.
  37. In other words, only individuals or organisations
  38. or computers or devices which have been pre-approved
  39. can join into the network,
  40. can access the information
  41. and can potentially contribute transactions.
  42. Now, when we think about blockchain,
  43. it may or may not involve cryptocurrencies.
  44. A cryptocurrency will involve a blockchain,
  45. but a blockchain does not necessarily
  46. involve a cryptocurrency.
  47. In other words if we think of a blockchain based system
  48. at its base, it is a distributed ledger
  49. which is encrypted, maybe with an additional layer
  50. of smart contracts on top.
  51. Those individual data entries, can be anything.
  52. The communications between those data entries
  53. do not necessarily involve any sort of currency.
  54. One of the most interesting and powerful applications
  55. for this sort of thing,
  56. is in production processes,
  57. the food market where the providence of a chicken,
  58. or a bottle of whiskey
  59. can be proven by the blockchain system
  60. from its creation, its history, its movements
  61. documented throughout that system.
  62. So, any eventual possessor
  63. can document both the origin
  64. as well as the lifespan of that particular chicken,
  65. bottle of whiskey, diamond, artwork,
  66. whatever it may be.
  67. And that is the real power of blockchain.
  68. To build systems
  69. which are potentially highly secure,
  70. permanent and highly transparent.
  71. But, blockchain is not the solution
  72. for every problem, why?
  73. Because not every blockchain is created equally.
  74. not every blockchain is necessarily secure.
  75. Big blockchain systems like Ethereum or Hyperledger
  76. or R3’s quarter, or bitcoin,
  77. these are highly secure.
  78. But if I create a blockchain in my basement,
  79. probably not that secure.
  80. Just because it’s a blockchain, doesn’t mean it’s secure.
  81. Second, from the standpoint of
  82. permanence and transparency,
  83. this raises two problems.
  84. One, is the garbage in, garbage out problem
  85. in other words if you put that information in,
  86. it’s in there forever, and that is a big problem
  87. in the context of building histories,
  88. building information, the permanence problem.
  89. And finally, privacy concerns.
  90. If information goes into,
  91. a permissionless public blockchain,
  92. that information may be permanently
  93. on public display and access,
  94. and this can create all sorts of problems
  95. in that not necessarily do we want
  96. every piece of information permanently on view.
  97. So, looking at blockchain,
  98. and this is something that we talk about
  99. a great deal throughout this course,
  100. and in other courses.
  101. Blockchain is a very important technology,
  102. being used across all aspects
  103. of the financial sector and beyond.
  104. But it’s not the solution for every problem,
  105. but it is giving us an excuse to re-look,
  106. to reconsider many of our existing systems
  107. and infrastructure to build better systems.

Industry Showcase: The Benefits from New Payment Stacks – Applications of Ripple for Standard Chartered Bank

  1. Standard Chartered Bank
  2. believes in using new and advanced technologies
  3. to bring a better experience to our clients.
  4. Since our first collaboration with Ripple
  5. in December 2015,
  6. we have made significant progress
  7. in enhancing the overall security
  8. of trade finance invoicing through the adoption of
  9. distributed ledger technology.
  10. We, together with Axis Bank,
  11. launched a cross-border payments platform in 2017
  12. which connects corporates
  13. between Singapore and India.
  14. Ripple allows Standard Chartered
  15. to provide our clients real-time payment services
  16. with visibility, traceability and full transparency.
  17. With all transacting parties
  18. connecting to the same blockchain network,
  19. customers are able to process payments in real-time
  20. with end-to-end tracking,
  21. pre-validated transaction,
  22. and see all the fees upfront.
  23. It helps reduce the risk of late payment
  24. as well as to provide certainty
  25. as to when and how much of the money
  26. would arrive at the beneficiary.
  27. As blockchain records cannot be altered
  28. once they are created,
  29. the technology helps
  30. financial institutions’ reconciliations of payments,
  31. improve working capital allocations
  32. and reduce the amount of time
  33. back-office staff spent handling errors.
  34. Standard Chartered has been using Ripple’s solution
  35. in many parts of its business,
  36. including global trade payments,
  37. e-commerce payments,
  38. international payroll and pensions,
  39. and international corporate dividends.
  40. Whether it is a global supply chain
  41. doing real-time cross border transactions,
  42. a corporate selling goods globally over the Internet
  43. or an international firm paying salaries
  44. to its overseas employees,
  45. Ripple makes payment easier, faster,
  46. and more efficient than ever before.

Module 3 Alternative Finance

Welcome to Module 3

3.1 Module 3 Introduction

  1. Hi, my name is Brian Tang,
  2. and I’m the founder of
  3. Asia Capital Markets Institute based in Hong Kong.
  4. I’ve been a corporate finance lawyer for nearly 20 years,
  5. having worked in New York and California
  6. during the dotcom period
  7. at Wall Street firm Sullivan & Cromwell,
  8. as well as, at Credit Suisse
  9. where I worked on some of the largest
  10. and first-of-its-kind transactions.
  11. These included the IPOs of CCB and ICBC,
  12. the privatisation of alibaba.com
  13. and the setting up Credit Suisse’s investment banking
  14. joint venture in China.
  15. And since I was in Silicon Valley,
  16. and now at a FinTech-focused startup,
  17. I think I can just lose the tie.
  18. Alternative finance, according to the
  19. Cambridge Centre for Alternative Finance,
  20. can broadly be described as
  21. comprising financial channels and instruments
  22. that emerge outside the traditional financial system.
  23. In my chapter in The Fintech Book,
  24. which has recently been translated
  25. and published in China,
  26. I have called some of these emerging
  27. financial channels, online capital marketplaces.
  28. Alternative finance can also refer
  29. to the new investment classes
  30. being sought to be created,
  31. including equity and loans to SMEs,
  32. and more recently, crypto assets.
  33. In this module, we will discuss the following.
  34. First, a brief history of finance and its innovations.
  35. Second, the digitization of financial services.
  36. Third, the opportunities and challenges
  37. of distributed peer-to-peer models of finance,
  38. such as crowdfunding,
  39. marketplace lending and token sales.
  40. Fourth, the rise of the Chinese TechFins
  41. in its new models and products.
  42. And five, how the banks are now positioning themselves
  43. as technology companies.

Module 3 Learning Objectives

Module 3 addresses the digitization of finance and investment and the development of alternative finance and investment mechanisms, including P2P lending, crowdfunding and initial coin offerings (ICOs).

In Module 3, learners will:

  • Understand the digital transformation of traditional securities and investment markets over the past 30 years.
  • Consider the major forms of technology impacting finance today.
  • Analyze the evolution of major forms of alternative finance, including P2P and marketplace lending, crowdfunding and initial coin offerings (ICOs).
  • Learn about new business models, particularly the impact of technology firms entering into finance.

3.2 A Brief History of Financial Innovation

  1. Finance can and should be
  2. an enabler of commerce and investment
  3. in growing the real economy
  4. as well as to provide individuals
  5. with more personal security
  6. against life’s vicissitudes.
  7. Money or currency consists of
  8. a stored value instrument that is scarce.
  9. Throughout the ages,
  10. these have ranged from cowry shells
  11. to gold and minted metal coinage.
  12. In the west,
  13. money lenders have existed since the Roman Empire
  14. where individuals have made
  15. private loans to those who needed it.
  16. There has long been widespread
  17. religious and moral condemnation against usury.
  18. The charging of interest for loans
  19. with different religions taking different stances
  20. on the permissible extent of such interest.
  21. For example, the Islamic faith prohibits
  22. any charging of interest whereas
  23. the Judaic faith permits charging interest to non-Jews.
  24. Modern legislation focuses more on setting limits
  25. on the amount of permissible interest set.
  26. Florence sat at the centre of the Renaissance
  27. due in part to the extensive trade
  28. through the Italian city
  29. leading to over 100 banks being there
  30. by the end of the 14th Century.
  31. The Medici banking empire
  32. was one such example
  33. and was the banker for the Catholic Church.
  34. Its expansion was aided by the invention
  35. of double-entry bookkeeping by
  36. Franciscan friar, Luca Pacioli
  37. which allowed for reliable documentation
  38. for both creditor and debtor.
  39. The important role of ledgers
  40. will be revisited with the advent
  41. of blockchain technology in this century.
  42. The Tang and Song Dynasty emperors
  43. in the seventh century started using paper
  44. which their forefathers in China invented
  45. as bank notes or jiaozi,
  46. that were originally used as promissory notes
  47. and became a lightweight alternative
  48. to heavy and metallic coinage.
  49. To address counterfeiting,
  50. a variety of techniques involving different inks,
  51. papers and seals were used.
  52. Property and being able to prove ownership
  53. is critical as sources of collateral for finance.
  54. Pawn shops such as the medieval practise
  55. of so-called Lombard banking
  56. dealt in the delivery of goods and custody
  57. and many legal jurisdictions developed
  58. centralised land record registries
  59. that enabled more lending to take place.
  60. As international trade grew,
  61. the financing of those ocean expeditions
  62. of vast durations and distances
  63. involved substantial risk.
  64. With the invention of the joint stock company
  65. shares and bonds could be
  66. issued to the general public to fund projects
  67. and the Dutch East India Company
  68. listing in the Amsterdam Stock Exchange
  69. paved the way in the 17th Century.
  70. Similar venues were set up across the ocean
  71. at coffee shops along London’s exchange alley,
  72. and under a buttonwood tree on Wall Street,
  73. and sea captains and merchants also met
  74. at Lloyd’s Coffee House at London’s Tower Street.
  75. These soon evolved into powerful institutions,
  76. today’s London Stock Exchange,
  77. New York Stock Exchange
  78. and Lloyd’s of London
  79. with impressive architecture to reflect their influence.
  80. Within these buildings,
  81. trading runners or deer would dash to
  82. nearby brokerage offices
  83. to deliver stock quotes
  84. from the brokers on the exchange floor.
  85. With the invention of electricity,
  86. technologies like the telegraph
  87. and then the telephone soon meant
  88. proximity to the exchanges proved less critical.
  89. In 1971, Nasdaq became the first electronic exchange
  90. with many other securities and
  91. commodities exchanges following suit.
  92. A new electronic and digital era was dawning.

3.3 Digitization of Financial Services

  1. Financial investment and trading
  2. can best be understood as
  3. comprising of two groups.
  4. First, retail investment and trading
  5. of public companies is conducted
  6. by the general public on exchanges
  7. through brokers and dealers.
  8. Second, institutional investment and trading
  9. involves large financial institutions
  10. who buy and sell publicly listed securities
  11. between each other.
  12. In the meantime,
  13. the investment into private companies
  14. has been the remit of specialised investors,
  15. such as venture capital and private equity firms,
  16. with so called exit or liquidity events
  17. being an initial public offering
  18. into the public markets
  19. or trade sale of the business.
  20. The venture capital firms
  21. on Sand Hill Road in Menlo Park, California,
  22. were likely instrumental in investing and growing
  23. what become known as Silicon Valley.
  24. Including their successes in hardware
  25. being turned into software and the internet
  26. that gave rise to the dot com boom.
  27. One major development at that time
  28. was when online brokerage firm E*Trade
  29. was able to get allocations
  30. to some of the hot tech IPOs.
  31. Electronic banking also started expanding
  32. as major banks extended services to consumers
  33. who were increasingly coming online
  34. with the lowering costs
  35. and increasing ease of access.
  36. At the same time,
  37. institutional trading expanded dramatically,
  38. with alternative trading systems and dark pools
  39. allowing financial institutions to trade
  40. large blocks of securities at one time
  41. to minimise pricing impact.
  42. At the same time,
  43. proprietary trading at desks invested and develop
  44. automated algorithmic trading programmes,
  45. as well as high frequency trading programmes
  46. that use computational power
  47. to devise trading strategies
  48. and automate trading in ways no human could.
  49. The global financial crisis in 2007
  50. changed the landscape dramatically.
  51. After the bursting of the sub prime mortgage bubble,
  52. and the rapid devaluation of financial instruments,
  53. such as mortgage backed securities
  54. and credit default swaps,
  55. many major financial institutions collapsed,
  56. or needed to be bailed out by their governments.
  57. Lending then dried up by many traditional lenders,
  58. and many governments sought to encourage
  59. the emergence of new players and business models
  60. that provided alternative finance
  61. to individuals and businesses.
  62. At the same time,
  63. 2010’s flash crash
  64. saw the Dow Jones Index drop
  65. nine percent within minutes
  66. and was subsequently attributed
  67. to high frequency traders initialling,
  68. spoofing algorithms
  69. that placed thousands of orders
  70. with intention of cancelling them.
  71. The automatic shutting down
  72. of many automated computerised traders
  73. due to the extreme drop
  74. also led to a rapid loss of liquidity.
  75. In the European Union,
  76. challenger banks were encouraged
  77. and has culminated
  78. in open banking policies such as
  79. the payment services directive,
  80. which forces banks to produce customer data
  81. to make it easier for customers
  82. to switch financial providers.
  83. Some of these banks were also virtual banks,
  84. with no branches at all.
  85. In the United States,
  86. a stark reminder that
  87. it was not business as usual
  88. can be seen in the before and after pictures
  89. of UBS’s famed trading floor in Stanford, Connecticut.
  90. This was once the largest in the world
  91. with 5,000 stock, bond and currency traders.
  92. The global financial crisis resulted,
  93. amongst other things,
  94. in the passing of Dodd-Frank,
  95. which prohibited proprietary trading by banks,
  96. and the Jump Start Our Business Startups Act,
  97. or JOBS Act in 2012,
  98. which legitimised the concept
  99. of equity crowdfunding in law.
  100. The era of distributed peer-to-peer models
  101. of finance was born.

3.4 FinTech and Funds

  1. As we all know, FinTech is really disrupting
  2. many verticals of the financial services industry,
  3. from investment banking,
  4. asset management, retail banking.
  5. But one area that it’s also affecting as well
  6. is the hedge fund industry.
  7. For many years, the hedge fund industry
  8. was the most entrepreneurial,
  9. at the cutting edge of technology.
  10. And obviously, a lot of Fintech innovations
  11. are benefiting the broader hedge fund industry as well.
  12. There’s many examples.
  13. Think about it.
  14. Imagine I’m a fund manager.
  15. Normally I try to access data
  16. that is available publicly
  17. and I’m trying to use.
  18. But what if I can use some of the big data analytics
  19. and artificial intelligence solutions
  20. to try to really generate insights?
  21. Imagine using satellite imagery to try to look at
  22. what’s happening on, let’s say, on oil containers
  23. and how much oil there are on the ships.
  24. Or actually looking from satellite imagery
  25. how many cars are in the parking lot
  26. and use that to try to actually forecast
  27. a company’s actually earnings.
  28. Or imagine being able to use
  29. some of the latest technology to read,
  30. go through all the news articles
  31. around the world available online
  32. or all the tweets and Facebook messages
  33. and be able to come up with a sentiment analysis
  34. of what are people really seeing
  35. and thinking around the world.
  36. Well, what’s really interesting as well,
  37. that’s one example

Industry Showcase: Ensuring Compliance From the Start: Suitability & Funds (Investment Navigator)

  1. Over the last few years we have seen
  2. and experienced a wave of regulations
  3. in the financial services industry
  4. all around the world.
  5. Typically, new regulations strive
  6. for an increase of investor protection.
  7. Because of this wave of regulations,
  8. it is increasingly difficult
  9. for financial service providers
  10. to stay compliance
  11. when conducting investment advice
  12. whether it is in person or via robo-advisory.
  13. Having pre- and post-trade checks in place
  14. is not optional for banks,
  15. it’s mandatory to stay in business.
  16. FinTech can help to establish such robust processes
  17. by replacing error-prone
  18. and inefficient manual compliance checks
  19. with automated solution along the investment
  20. and advisory value chain.
  21. In our case of investmentnavigator.com,
  22. technology enables
  23. the entanglement of client context,
  24. regulatory rules and product data
  25. to assess suitability on the individual instrument level.
  26. By acting upon suitability feedbacks,
  27. banks can not only significantly reduce regulatory risks
  28. and save time through the automation,
  29. but also level up the client experience.
  30. They get enabled to offer
  31. the most cost-efficient suitable investment instrument
  32. for the respective client context.
  33. A very tangible benefit also for the client.
  34. Behind the scenes,
  35. the cloud-based rule engine
  36. is the technological heart of Investment Navigator,
  37. which allows us to deliver coherent suitability feedbacks
  38. into every application along the investment
  39. and advisory value chain.
  40. It is therefore strategic for us
  41. to provide our clients the complete freedom
  42. to consume our Suitability Service
  43. via one or several channels.
  44. We currently maintain customised webpages,
  45. an API to integrate into proprietary advisory tools
  46. a core-banking adapter and an Excel add-in.
  47. To sum-up, as a B2B FinTech company,
  48. we believe that by pairing technology
  49. with subject matter intelligence,
  50. we simplify financial advice.
  51. That’s how FinTech creates a “win-win” situation
  52. for financial service providers, as well as, customers.

3.5 Crowdfunding – Rewards, Charity and Equity

  1. The concept of crowdfunding
  2. is not as new as many may think.
  3. The Statue of Liberty was famously known as
  4. a gift from the French people
  5. to commemorate the American Revolution.
  6. What is less known is that there was
  7. not enough funding for the base of the statue
  8. and thus was actually crowdfunded by
  9. Joseph Pulitzer by placing appeals
  10. in the New York World newspaper in 1885
  11. seeking contributions.
  12. The rest, as they say, is history.
  13. Crowdfunding’s modern incarnation gained popularity
  14. when Kickstarter launched in 2009
  15. as an online platform to fund
  16. creative projects campaigns
  17. in exchange for rewards.
  18. Although not its’ initial main target audience,
  19. Kickstarter became a favourite amongst
  20. hardware enthusiasts who wanted to
  21. fund minimum-batch-order production runs
  22. of electronics in Shenzhen
  23. and sought other enthusiasts to help with funding
  24. and receive part of the batch run as rewards.
  25. Gradually it became used more for pre-marketing
  26. and product validation of
  27. prototype consumer electronic, and other products,
  28. and even incorporated by hardware accelerators
  29. and venture capitalists.
  30. Some of the most funded Kickstarter campaigns
  31. were to fund smartwatch Pebble,
  32. the Connected Coolest Cooler,
  33. a Baubax, the travel jacket
  34. made for electronic accessories.
  35. Crowdfunding has been used
  36. to benefit charities as well.
  37. With sites like gofundme
  38. having raised three billion U.S. Dollars
  39. from over 25 million donors
  40. across two million campaigns.
  41. In China, Tencent’s ’99 Charity Day’ in 2017
  42. raised approximately 930 Million Yuan
  43. or 126 Million U.S. Dollars from
  44. over 12 million Wechat users
  45. for charities running 6700 programmes
  46. over a three-day period.
  47. Many other crowdfunding platforms
  48. have since emerged with different levels of curation.
  49. For example, IndieGoGo does not have
  50. a minimum all-or-nothing funding goal,
  51. and does not require a working prototype.
  52. More specialised platforms around sectors
  53. or themes have also emerged.
  54. Yet, before too long,
  55. some backers began to question
  56. this rewards approach.
  57. In late 2012 Oculus Rift’s 19 year-old founder
  58. raised 2.4 million U.S. Dollars
  59. offering it’s virtual reality
  60. head-mounted display as rewards.
  61. Yet, less than two years later,
  62. he sold the company to Facebook for two billion dollars.
  63. Were rewards backers chumps?
  64. If they could have received substantial upside
  65. by receiving equity in Oculus Rift,
  66. rather than V.R. Headgear?
  67. In 2012 Congress passed the JOBS Act
  68. which amongst other things legitimised
  69. the concept of equity crowdfunding.
  70. And in 2015, the SEC passed the Title IV regulations.
  71. Many other countries around the world followed suit
  72. to pass crowdfunding laws to encourage innovation,
  73. and to allow direct retail investment into
  74. early stage companies to augment current reliance
  75. on private placement exemptions from
  76. professional investors like venture capital firms,
  77. and high-net-worth individuals.
  78. The World Bank has predicted that
  79. crowdfunding investments will be
  80. a US$96 billion a year market
  81. in developing countries alone by 2025.
  82. And Goldman Sachs’ analysts have heralded
  83. crowdfunding as potentially the most disruptive
  84. of all the new models of finance.
  85. In essence, online capital marketplaces
  86. have three main facets.
  87. First, digital access by the general public
  88. to learn more about,
  89. and for the information to be posted by projects online.
  90. Second, easy to use and secure fund flow
  91. from the general public to support these projects.
  92. And third, marketplaces where there is
  93. sufficiency of information for decisions to be made
  94. about the project and its’ promoters that
  95. enable matchmaking of projects
  96. to the potential supporters.
  97. So unlike traditional stock markets which
  98. relied upon brokers and dealers as intermediaries,
  99. individual investors could transact directly
  100. which lead to many to describe this as
  101. disintermediation and democratisation of finance.
  102. Entrepreneurship and SMEs are well recognised as
  103. important drivers of employment and innovation.
  104. And television shows like Shark Tank in the U.S.
  105. And Dragon’s Den in the U.K. popularised
  106. and encouraged the notion of entrepreneurs
  107. seeking such early stage funding for their projects
  108. and the general public’s willingness and ability
  109. to participate in these kinds of investment decisions
  110. from their living room chairs.
  111. If securities in the form of equities
  112. or investment products are being offered
  113. existing securities
  114. and collective investment scheme laws and regulations
  115. require perspectives to be prepared for
  116. public offers unless they are private placements
  117. made to sophisticated investors.
  118. Crowdfunding legislation introduced specifically
  119. recognised such limitations of existing laws
  120. and are designed to allow SMEs and entrepreneurs
  121. to be able to more cost-effectively tap
  122. funding from the general public
  123. with specific consumer protection elements built in.
  124. Examples of these elements include
  125. limiting the size of qualified companies,
  126. capping investor, investment amounts,
  127. and requiring investor cooling-off periods.
  128. Minimum disclosure and lockups
  129. as well as licensing the marketplace portals.
  130. Yet, equity was just the first financial instrument
  131. to utilise this new distributed
  132. peer-to-peer model of finance.
  133. More was to come.

3.6 P2P and Marketplace Lending

  1. Crowdfunding for debt emerged
  2. almost in parallel with crowdfunding in equity.
  3. Known as peer-to-peer lending or P2P lending
  4. or crowdlending, the size of this market
  5. was perceived to be even bigger,
  6. as it encompasses not just unsecured business
  7. and SME loans but the personal finance sector.
  8. In the aftermath of the global financial crisis,
  9. many governments became very supportive
  10. of this form of alternative finance
  11. after the loss of trust in traditional banks.
  12. The basic concept is simple.
  13. In a low-interest-rate environment,
  14. many individual savers
  15. sought alternative avenues for investment
  16. at a time when banks had reduced their lending.
  17. Through an online platform,
  18. borrowers are listed to be matched by lenders
  19. who benefit from a higher return
  20. due to the lower overhead costs.
  21. It seems like a win-win situation
  22. for borrower and lender.
  23. The concept can be traced back to Kiva,
  24. a micro-lending platform inspired
  25. by microfinance founder Muhammad Yunus,
  26. which allowed loans to be made to individuals
  27. in rural communities within developing countries.
  28. For example, you can help a farmer in Africa
  29. by lending him money to buy a particular cow.
  30. In the United Kingdom,
  31. platforms such as Zopa
  32. and Funding Circle expanded P2P lending rapidly.
  33. In the United States,
  34. companies like Prosper
  35. and LendingClub grew exponentially,
  36. with the latter being the first
  37. P2P lending company to IPO in 2014.
  38. Credit analysis is critical in this sector
  39. of unsecured loans
  40. which do not enjoy government guarantees.
  41. How do you know the individuals
  42. or businesses will repay the loan?
  43. To address the risks, loans often have
  44. short tenors, and some platforms
  45. introduced secured loans and trust accounts.
  46. There was also a regulatory lacuna as loans
  47. are not on their face securities.
  48. This was a situation that changed
  49. in the United States in 2008
  50. when the SEC extended jurisdiction
  51. over such P2P-lending platforms.
  52. Two trends began emerging.
  53. First, many categories of loans emerged
  54. on specialised platforms,
  55. from consumer lending to business lending,
  56. real-estate lending, student loans
  57. and payday loans linked to wages paid.
  58. Second, individual lenders started getting dwarfed
  59. by institutional lenders and investors,
  60. so that instead of being called peer-to-peer lending,
  61. the sector became known as marketplace lending.
  62. In the meantime, the combination
  63. of a lighter regulatory touch,
  64. rising middle class
  65. and the lack of investment alternatives
  66. led the P2P-lending market in China to explode.
  67. It was primarily when CreditEase listed
  68. its Yirendai unit in 2015 that the West realised
  69. the size of loans originated
  70. and the number of borrowers in China dwarfed that
  71. of the largest US platform lenders.
  72. However, problems emerged in this area
  73. of China’s shadow banking,
  74. and the 2016 Blue Book of Internet Finance found
  75. 1,263 namely 1/3 of China’s P2P platforms problematic.
  76. For example, in 2017, a Chinese court concluded
  77. that P2P-lender Ezubo defrauded
  78. more than 900,000 investors of 50 billion yuan,
  79. or US $7.6 billion, under a Ponzi scheme,
  80. and two of its founders were
  81. sentenced to life imprisonment
  82. and 24 executives were jailed for three to 15 years.
  83. China’s regulators have since been tightening
  84. P2P-lending rules,
  85. such as requiring custodian banks to be appointed,
  86. imposing borrowing and interest caps, and recently
  87. preventing microfinance lending companies and banks
  88. from respectively raising and funding P2P loans.
  89. The new generation of P2P-lending companies
  90. appear to be veering away as lending platforms
  91. to become innovative credit analysis services
  92. that incorporate analysis of social media usage
  93. and online purchase patterns that can be used
  94. by traditional banks and bank syndicates.
  95. Similarly, P2P insurance has evolved
  96. and has been described as evolving from
  97. a distribution model to a carrier model
  98. and now a self-governing model.
  99. The evolution of P2P lending
  100. provides useful lessons
  101. for the development of the current darling of
  102. distributed peer-to-peer models of finance,
  103. namely, cryptocurrencies and token sales.

3.7 The Rise of Chinese TechFins

  1. In December 2016, Alibaba’s founder Jack Ma,
  2. coined the phrase “TechFin”,
  3. “FinTech takes the original financial system
  4. and improves its technology”,
  5. Ma Yun told South China Morning Post’s
  6. China Conference in Hong Kong.
  7. “TechFin is to rebuild the system with technology.”
  8. Another way to think about it is that
  9. where financial institutions
  10. and FinTech startups seek to improve
  11. or upend the existing financial system in FinTech,
  12. tech companies approach this issue
  13. from a different and more consumer and
  14. technology centric perspective in TechFin.
  15. It is instructive to compare the rise of
  16. American tech giants to those from China,
  17. to better understand their difference
  18. in origins and approaches.
  19. China’s tech giants are commonly known as
  20. the BATs: Baidu, Alibaba and Tencent.
  21. In the United States, a commonly known acronym
  22. is the GAFAs: Google, Apple, Facebook and Amazon.
  23. During the dotcom boom and web 2.0,
  24. today’s Silicon Valley tech giants grew
  25. mainly using advertising or “eyeball” models
  26. that relied on generating more viewers.
  27. Examples of these include
  28. search engines like Google
  29. and social media portals like Facebook.
  30. Apple was then primarily a hardware manufacturer,
  31. and e-commerce pioneers like
  32. marketplace platform eBay,
  33. which acquired PayPal in 2002
  34. to power it’s transactions,
  35. and online bookseller Amazon booked the majority
  36. of its transactions via credit cards.
  37. In contrast, in China
  38. where credit card penetration remained low,
  39. the tech giants incorporated transactions
  40. into their platforms from the very beginning.
  41. Hangzhou based Alibaba started as
  42. a business-to-business or B2B platform,
  43. and when it transitioned more
  44. to a business-to-consumer or B2C platform,
  45. with the introduction of online retailer Taobao,
  46. it extended its sister company’s payment system,
  47. now known as Alipay, to consumer transactions.
  48. Instead of Amazon’s money-back guarantee,
  49. Alibaba provides comfort to its retail consumers
  50. by keeping payments of goods in a deposit account
  51. that is not released until receipt
  52. and approval of the goods by the customer.
  53. Yu’eBao or “leftover treasure” was born
  54. when Alibaba started offering interest
  55. to customers who kept their funds in this account
  56. at rates higher than bank deposits.
  57. The Wall Street Journal reported that in just four years,
  58. Yu’eBao had accrued 370 million account holders
  59. and 211 billion dollars in US assets
  60. to be twice the size of the next largest
  61. money market fund JP Morgan Asset Management.
  62. Shenzhen-based online gaming company Tencent
  63. built in micropayments early because
  64. its freemium business model was based on
  65. gamers making in-game payments for
  66. its free-to-play games,
  67. to buy say, awesome virtual swords.
  68. When the company transitioned into mobile
  69. and introduced instant messaging
  70. through QQ for youth and then WeChat,
  71. they captured an amazing userbase
  72. of nearly one billion active users
  73. who learned to transact online through inventions
  74. like hongbao where users can give
  75. and receive traditional red packets for mere cents.
  76. WeChat now also allows users to use
  77. and transact via third party mini-apps,
  78. including financial ones,
  79. without even exiting the instant messaging ecosystem
  80. to open new apps.
  81. In 2015, Tencent launched WeBank,
  82. China’s first online only bank,
  83. using sophisticated credit analysis
  84. based on WeChat social media usage
  85. and online purchase patterns,
  86. that is also offered to service traditional lenders.
  87. Quick Response codes, or QR codes,
  88. are also rapidly changing China into a cashless society.
  89. Being built into WeChat and many other apps,
  90. China’s smartphone users can use scannable codes
  91. to facilitate offline-to-online,
  92. or O2O transactions, connect with others
  93. to replace the venerable business card,
  94. as well as to make payments.
  95. Financial supermarkets are also proliferating.
  96. For example, Alibaba’s Zhao Cai Bao
  97. offers fixed term deposit products
  98. from third-party financial institutions or individuals,
  99. mutual funds, and stock market information.
  100. These compare with distribution platforms
  101. traditionally accessible only to
  102. to institutional fund managers
  103. and to existing retail sales distributions
  104. via banks or retail fund managers.
  105. In the meantime, in the West,
  106. the GAFAs are not sitting idly by.
  107. Facebook’s messaging app WhatsApp
  108. is incorporating a lot of
  109. WeChat’s innovative features.
  110. Apple has since developed a tremendous
  111. and very sticky online ecosystem
  112. with App Store and iTunes.
  113. And Amazon’s Prime has paved the way
  114. for subscription based models,
  115. for content such as its competitor Netflix,
  116. to show that users are willing to pay
  117. for quality movie, television and music content.
  118. Most importantly, they are also investing heavily
  119. in the next generation
  120. of ABCD technologies and infrastructure, namely,
  121. artificial intelligence, blockchain, cloud, and data.
  122. How long will it be before GAFA are inspired
  123. by their Chinese BATs counterparts to use
  124. their digital nous, consumer-centric mindsets
  125. and broad user-base clout to directly
  126. participate and compete in finance?
  127. Tech giants have brands that are trusted
  128. especially amongst younger financial consumers
  129. at a time when banks have lost trust
  130. after the global financial crisis.
  131. Yet, traditional finance and banks are used to
  132. working within highly regulated environments
  133. that many tech companies have not yet
  134. fully come to grips with.
  135. The Silicon Valley ethos that
  136. “it is easier to ask for forgiveness
  137. than to seek permission”
  138. works just fine in the internet industry,
  139. where lines of code can be corrected
  140. and changed overnight on a website.
  141. It works less well
  142. in a highly regulated industry like finance.

Additional Video Resources: Chinese TechFin

1. What is Alipay? (2.30 Mins) 

3.8 ICOs

  1. Well, let’s talk now about a very exciting topic of ICOs.
  2. ICOs, ICOs, ICOs, you’ve probably heard this term a lot
  3. in the media or from friends.
  4. Well, there was actually a lot of buzz in the media
  5. in recent months about this topic.
  6. Believe it or not, only in 2017,
  7. more than $6 billion were raised by ICOs.
  8. But what is an ICO?
  9. An ICO is short for Initial Coin Offering,
  10. often referred to as a token sale.
  11. It’s a limited period of time in which
  12. a company sells a predefined number of
  13. digital tokens to the public.
  14. But what is this really?
  15. You know to really simplify things
  16. a good analogy is, well if we make a comparison
  17. to what happens in real life?
  18. Let’s take a private club,
  19. let’s say tomorrow I want to build a private club
  20. and I come to you and I say,
  21. hey, I want to build a private club.
  22. Here’s where the swimming pool’s going to be,
  23. this is where the restaurant is going to be
  24. and these are where the tennis courts are going to be
  25. and it’s going to be the best private club in the world.
  26. But in order to enjoy the facilities,
  27. you need to be a member.
  28. If you want to…
  29. you have to be a member to be able
  30. to enjoy the facilities.
  31. Yes, you still need to pay for your breakfast,
  32. or when you’re at the tennis club,
  33. but to join the club, you need to be a member.
  34. And that is really if we really generalise what ICOs are.
  35. They are selling these memberships
  36. to this ecosystem that people can use.
  37. But you would say,
  38. why is somebody doing this?
  39. Why wouldn’t they simply issue shares
  40. in the company for capital?
  41. Well, there’s a couple differences.
  42. In most ICOs, the tokens that you’re issuing
  43. have no economic rights, no voting rights
  44. and they’re generally outside the scope
  45. or the traditional regulatory framework.
  46. This is different from when you issues shares
  47. in exchange of equity for your business
  48. where you often get a calmer exposure
  49. to your business,
  50. you have voting rights
  51. and there’s a pretty well clear and established path
  52. of how you can raise money in that way.
  53. And this is actually a very interesting way
  54. of actually generating value where people
  55. are trying to build their ecosystems
  56. and actually doing ICOs for doing so.
  57. But why do you think
  58. a lot of entrepreneurs are doing this?
  59. But one of the big advantage of an ICO
  60. is it doesn’t dilute you.
  61. When I’m issuing shares for capital
  62. I actually kind of giving away
  63. part of my business to my investors.
  64. The beauty of an ICO is actually you’re issuing
  65. these tokens that allow these purchasers
  66. of these tokens to access the ecosystem,
  67. but without really diluting your equity.
  68. And this has been pretty big game-changer
  69. because many believe this actually can enable us
  70. to actually democratise capital in fund raising.
  71. In a traditional world if you’re launching a business
  72. you hope you have some friends or contacts
  73. who have money who are happy
  74. to invest in your business.
  75. The beauty of an ICO allows individuals
  76. from all around the world to raise money
  77. if they have a good idea and a good project.
  78. Whether you’re in Santiago, Chile,
  79. Moscow, Russia,
  80. Johannesburg, South Africa,
  81. or Hong Kong, China,
  82. doesn’t really matter.
  83. The world is actually where you can go raise money.
  84. Obviously there’s a lot of regulatory requirements
  85. and restrictions, but I think you get my point.
  86. And what’s been really interesting
  87. is over the last 12 to 18 months
  88. since the beginning of the ICO craze,
  89. we have really seen the industry turn swarming off.
  90. Initially it was a lot of young entrepreneurs
  91. who had an idea, wrote a white paper,
  92. and were able to raise quite a bit of money.
  93. What we have seen since then is the industry
  94. becoming definitely more institutional.
  95. We have seen the emergence of best practises
  96. like in the space of KYC and the AML
  97. Know Your Client and Anti Money Laundering.
  98. We have seen the emergence of best practises
  99. when it comes to the government and transparency.
  100. For example how these ICO companies are being run.
  101. What is a transparency that has been given
  102. to people who own these tokens?
  103. So really there’s a lot of exciting space
  104. going on in this space.
  105. There will be definitely more
  106. regulatory enforcement cases moving forward.
  107. Tax authorities will look at it.
  108. And actually the regulatory space,
  109. this entire space may become
  110. more regulated as well.
  111. But this is definitely an area to watch
  112. if you’re interested in the broader startup,
  113. blockchain and cryptospace.

Industry Showcase (NEW) – Collaborative and Contextual Banking (WeBank)

  1. Hello, today I’ll be talking about
  2. how finance is becoming far more intuitive
  3. and customer-oriented, but just as importantly,
  4. how technology is informing new types
  5. of financial business models and services.
  6. Finance is becoming contextualised,
  7. brought into the business scenarios where it is needed
  8. and it is being provided by both traditional
  9. and non-traditional players collaborating together.
  10. My name is Tyler Aveni and I serve as
  11. the Head of International Partnerships for WeBank.
  12. WeBank is the Tencent-affiliated digital bank
  13. based in Shenzhen, China.
  14. We are the first privately-owned bank
  15. as well as, the first internet bank here in China as well.
  16. This is to say that
  17. since receiving our banking licence in 2014,
  18. we have never opened a physical bank branch
  19. to serve our customers.
  20. Instead, we leverage on an intelligent technology stack,
  21. digital channels, and a host of
  22. different business partners and their resources,
  23. to offer retail banking solutions.
  24. These are both business-to-consumer products,
  25. like savings accounts and credit to individuals,
  26. as well as, white-labeled products
  27. and other technological solutions
  28. we offer to institutions.
  29. Our most popular product, called Weilidai,
  30. is a micro-credit product which we offer exclusively
  31. through the texting and social media platforms
  32. WeChat and QQ to Chinese users.
  33. In the case of credit,
  34. we are using our technology to understand
  35. the behavioural and financial interactions of users
  36. to reduce fraud and build a more complete picture
  37. of someone’s credit worthiness.
  38. This helps improve access to financial services
  39. for underbanked customers
  40. who may not have conventional credit bureau data.
  41. On the funding side, we can do this
  42. through a loan syndication model
  43. wherein dozens of bank partners contribute
  44. the majority of funding for each micro loan
  45. using new methods like blockchain technology.
  46. And what this equates to, essentially,
  47. is this complex array of collaborating institutions
  48. in cutting-edge technology, operating behind the scenes,
  49. all so that we can effectively provide
  50. this extremely easy to understand
  51. and accessible product.
  52. And so too, around the world,
  53. we see that technology
  54. is the driving factor behind the changes
  55. we are seeing in banking and finance.
  56. It then holds that financial institutions need to learn
  57. from their large IT counterparts and their development.
  58. More than just bolstering in-house core competency
  59. in some of these new technologies though,
  60. this really also means embracing
  61. an ideology of collaboration,
  62. the same kind of collaboration we have seen
  63. in open-source software projects
  64. and how they have brought long-term benefits
  65. to IT industry leaders and their users alike.
  66. Banks, as a full-service model,
  67. are very sticky institutions by design.
  68. You, as a customer of the bank,
  69. face a lot of obstacles in changing providers,
  70. and may even lack information
  71. to know on what grounds
  72. to make that selection to begin with.
  73. This is quickly changing,
  74. and giving way to several new models of banking
  75. which are much more collaboration focused.
  76. The first model simply reduces banks
  77. to a risk management function
  78. and provides security of your money
  79. as a kind of public utility.
  80. This may be used in the case of retail businesses
  81. that decides to offer financial services
  82. and open accounts for customers.
  83. Behind the scenes, any funds that might be held
  84. would be protected and managed by a partner bank
  85. in exchange for a fee.
  86. The second, is a type of marketplace banking.
  87. With the advent of Open Banking frameworks
  88. such as what is being rolled out in the UK now,
  89. or the Second Payment Services Directive
  90. or PSD2 across Europe,
  91. users are gaining more explicit control
  92. over their digital information,
  93. meaning customers can more easily transfer their data
  94. from one institution to another.
  95. This data can be standardised
  96. and offered as functionality
  97. in the form of tools like APIs and SDKs.
  98. So in marketplace banking,
  99. the banks still remained a single channel for banking,
  100. but many other FinTechs and financial institutions
  101. can offer their particular financial product,
  102. whether it be a loan, a payment tool,
  103. a rewards mechanism and so on,
  104. through this channel or marketplace.
  105. This allows users to compare and then easily
  106. swap in-and-out comparable services,
  107. all linked to their bank account
  108. in a Lego building like manner.
  109. And then, third, in contextual banking,
  110. you have financial products
  111. that are still being designed,
  112. managed, and provided by a bank
  113. or another financial institution.
  114. However, they are being provided
  115. in the business scenario that they are required.
  116. For instance, we see this when we are offered a credit
  117. or loan product during an online purchase.
  118. In this sense, finance is seen as an enabler
  119. of the transactions within our lives.
  120. Making a trip to a bank,
  121. or in the digital equivalent, moving to another app,
  122. is not an activity that really offers the user
  123. some inherent benefit necessarily.
  124. And so, this poses a direct challenge
  125. to a long-held belief in banking,
  126. that banks’ strongest asset
  127. is its relationship to customers.
  128. Wherein marketplace banking sees
  129. an unbundling of the full-service bank,
  130. to allow FinTechs to specialise in specific products
  131. or financial services and plug-in to the platform,
  132. contextual banking sees a separation of value
  133. along different lines.
  134. Digital services, businesses that are a part
  135. of the shared economy, e-commerce and so on,
  136. these companies provide access to data,
  137. understanding of their customer,
  138. and massive distribution.
  139. By embedding the financial products into these
  140. business scenarios with some of the additional data,
  141. banks and supporting technology intermediaries
  142. can create unique products that are tailored
  143. to the specific situation.
  144. In fact, at WeBank, our mobile application,
  145. a combination of a savings account
  146. and wealth management products,
  147. remains the only direct channel for servicing customers.
  148. All of our other products, including the Weilidai,
  149. the micro-credit product mentioned earlier,
  150. are offered through partner platforms.
  151. And so, it is not just BigTechs and FinTechs
  152. that become part of the future financial landscape,
  153. but in fact, most businesses will
  154. increasingly become participatory
  155. in some of these business models
  156. because finance is a big part of our lives.
