Module 4 FinTech Regulation and RegTech

Welcome to Module 4

4.1 Module 4 Introduction

  1. In Module 4, we turn to regulation of FinTech,
  2. and a new development,
  3. what is called RegTech or regulatory technology,
  4. and if we look at this module,
  5. we’re going to begin by looking at
  6. why do we regulate financial markets
  7. before turning to the particular challenges
  8. of regulating FinTech.
  9. From there we’ll move on
  10. to what we think is one of the most exciting areas
  11. of interaction between finance technology
  12. and regulation, and that is RegTech.
  13. We’ll look at the RegTech ecosystem
  14. and also ideas of smart regulation
  15. and the outlook for future design of financial systems.

Module 4 Learning Objectives

Module 4 addresses the range of regulatory considerations and approaches in the context of FinTech. It highlights the core regulatory objectives and the relationship between the post-2008 financial regulatory reform process and FinTech. It focuses in particular on the concept of RegTech – “regulatory technology” – and the RegTech ecosystem comprising financial institutions, startups, and regulators, and introduces “Smart Regulation” as the redesigning financial infrastructure and regulatory systems on the basis of new technologies, including Big Data, cloud, AI and blockchain.

In Module 4, learners will:

  • Understand major financial policy and regulatory objectives and their implications for FinTech.
  • Explore RegTech and the RegTech ecosystem, in order to understand one of the key trends in financial and regulatory transformation.
  • Consider how regulatory systems and financial infrastructure could be redesigned on the basis of new technologies to deliver better results both from the standpoint of efficiency as well as resilience
  • Think about new regulatory approaches such as regulatory sandboxes, India Stack and Smart Regulation.

4.2A FinTech Regulation (Part 1)

  1. The first question is really
  2. why do we regulate financial markets in the first place?
  3. In fact,
  4. even why do we regulate financial markets at all?
  5. And as we’ve seen
  6. with fintech’s evolution generally,
  7. the 2008 global financial crisis
  8. was a game changer
  9. for financial regulation.
  10. Prior to the 2008 global financial crisis,
  11. there was a general consensus
  12. in favour of largely market-based approaches
  13. to financial market regulation.
  14. Many of these derived from
  15. the ideas of what is called
  16. the efficient markets hypothesis.
  17. The idea of the efficient markets hypothesis
  18. is that markets will price in
  19. all available information
  20. with prices of financial assets
  21. providing for efficient allocation
  22. of financial resources.
  23. The idea is that market mechanisms provided
  24. there is sufficient information
  25. available to markets
  26. will function to
  27. both price, financial assets,
  28. as well as, allocate financial resources
  29. to their most valuable uses.
  30. That idea was based on
  31. a number of assumptions.
  32. First, that there is
  33. perfect information available in markets,
  34. second, that there are
  35. no transaction costs in markets
  36. third, that there is
  37. perfect competition in markets,
  38. and fourth, that there were
  39. rational market participants.
  40. All of these ideas together
  41. would lead towards
  42. efficiently functioning financial markets
  43. which would properly
  44. support the functioning
  45. of the overall economy.
  46. The problem was that
  47. certainly even prior to 2008,
  48. we already knew that
  49. information was not perfect,
  50. that there are transaction costs
  51. in acquiring information
  52. or enforcing transactions in markets,
  53. and that competition
  54. is by no means perfect.
  55. And much of law and regulation
  56. prior to the global financial crisis
  57. focused on improving information quality,
  58. on enhancing competition,
  59. on reducing transaction costs
  60. to reduce what are called market failures.
  61. The idea is that law and regulation
  62. would be used
  63. to reduce problems in markets
  64. to help those markets
  65. function in a better way.
  66. 2008 global financial crisis
  67. fundamentally changed the way
  68. that we think about
  69. finance and its regulation.
  70. In particular, the 2008 global crisis
  71. and the hundreds of billions of dollars spent
  72. on bailing out large banks around the world
  73. highlighted a problem
  74. which is called systemic risk.
  75. Systemic risk is the risk that
  76. the collapse of an individual financial institution
  77. will cause the collapse of
  78. the entire financial system,
  79. which will in turn
  80. cause the collapse of the economy.
  81. This is exactly what we saw in 1929
  82. and the 1930s Great Depression.
  83. It’s also what we saw in 2008.
  84. As a result, since 2008,
  85. there has been a massive amount of attention
  86. on building new regulatory frameworks
  87. to prevent financial crises,
  88. to build confidence,
  89. and to make markets function properly.
  90. So, if we think of regulation today,
  91. its function continues to be
  92. on improving market functioning and efficiency.
  93. It is also about
  94. preventing systemic risks,
  95. maintaining financial stability,
  96. and importantly,
  97. as we’ve already seen,
  98. it is about fairness
  99. to market participants.

4.2B FinTech Regulation (Part 2)

  1. As we just discussed,
  2. the 2008 financial crisis was a game changer
  3. in the way that we look at
  4. regulation of financial markets.
  5. And for the eight or so years after 2008,
  6. policy makers globally,
  7. as well as, regulators around the world
  8. spent much of their time and efforts
  9. on developing new regulatory systems
  10. to prevent the sorts of crisis
  11. that we saw in 2008,
  12. but this 2008 crisis
  13. also triggered an explosion
  14. in the development of fintech.
  15. And that explosion
  16. in the development of fintech
  17. has been a major challenge for regulators,
  18. after all, one element of fintech
  19. is the idea of
  20. disrupting traditional institutions,
  21. traditional industries,
  22. traditional finance,
  23. but a major object of financial regulation
  24. after the 2008 crisis
  25. has been preventing disruption
  26. in financial markets,
  27. financial institutions,
  28. and the financial system.
  29. The same time,
  30. there has been a strong focus on regulation
  31. to support innovation and development
  32. in the financial system,
  33. and so regulators have been forced to
  34. come to terms with the explosion
  35. in new technologies and new participants
  36. in the financial sector,
  37. and to come to grips with
  38. how to regulate the opportunities
  39. as well as, the challenges.
  40. And so far what we have seen
  41. are four major approaches
  42. amongst regulators.
  43. The first approach has largely been
  44. one of doing nothing,
  45. in many ways,
  46. this idea of doing nothing
  47. can be seen as
  48. either a positive or a negative approach.
  49. It can be either permissive or restrictive.
  50. China, prior to the middle of 2015
  51. is usually seen as the major example
  52. of a country taking a permissive approach
  53. through deciding
  54. not to put in place new regulations.
  55. And in many ways,
  56. it was this decision,
  57. which has allowed the explosion of fintech
  58. in the context of China.
  59. But, as we’ve seen before,
  60. that explosion of fintech in China
  61. also brought with it new risks.
  62. The evolution from
  63. too small to care to too big to fail,
  64. that we’ve seen
  65. in the context of payments,
  66. money market,
  67. mutual funds,
  68. and other areas.
  69. As a result, even in the context of China,
  70. the decision over the past several years
  71. since 2015 has been increasingly to build
  72. a new regulatory framework
  73. for digital financial services.
  74. Other jurisdictions
  75. have taken an approach
  76. of not doing anything,
  77. which has largely been restrictive,
  78. requiring new entrants into financial services
  79. with new business models,
  80. new technologies,
  81. and new approaches
  82. to comply with existing regulatory requirements,
  83. which would typically develop
  84. for a very different type of
  85. established financial institution,
  86. banks,
  87. insurance companies,
  88. mutual funds,
  89. and the like.
  90. The end result of this is
  91. often a very restrictive approach.
  92. Coming into the past several years,
  93. regulators have been trying
  94. to balance the objectives
  95. of innovation and growth
  96. with considerations of financial stability
  97. and consumer protection,
  98. and as a result,
  99. they are developing an increasing number
  100. of experimentation-based approaches.
  101. Some involve regulators
  102. establishing contact points
  103. to meet with new entrants,
  104. to learn about technologies
  105. in order to be able to
  106. develop appropriate regulatory responses.
  107. Others have developed
  108. what are called sandboxes,
  109. these are areas for experimentation
  110. in a limited market context
  111. with limited regulation,
  112. in order for both the new company
  113. as well as the regulator,
  114. to learn how best to move forward.
  115. And finally,
  116. an increasing number of jurisdictions
  117. developing new regulatory frameworks,
  118. particularly for the sorts of things
  119. that we’ve seen in Modules 2 and 3.
  120. Things like P2P lending or
  121. alternative payment systems
  122. or forms of crowdfunding.
  123. But jurisdictions like China, India and others
  124. are also looking at
  125. developing entirely new regulatory approaches.

4.3 Evolution of RegTech

  1. The 2008 financial crisis
  2. was one of the key triggers
  3. for the massive acceleration
  4. in the development of FinTech worldwide.
  5. But in addition,
  6. it was the catalyst for the development of
  7. RegTech, or regulatory technology.
  8. And the ideas of RegTech relate to FinTech
  9. but are much more broader.
  10. In other words,
  11. RegTech is the idea of using technology
  12. for regulatory compliance,
  13. regulatory monitoring,
  14. but also, regulatory design.
  15. It is the idea of using technology
  16. to make financial markets
  17. and their regulation more effective.
  18. Now, if we think of RegTech in that way,
  19. the key aspect is that
  20. it is beyond FinTech.
  21. RegTech can apply
  22. in any segment of the economy,
  23. not just in the context of financial regulation.
  24. One can imagine
  25. the application of technology
  26. to environmental regulation,
  27. or traffic regulation,
  28. or airline regulation.
  29. Any area of the economy with regulation,
  30. the application of technology
  31. offers the potential
  32. to transform the effectiveness
  33. of that particular regulatory system.
  34. However, in the context of finance,
  35. RegTech is so far,
  36. the most developed.
  37. In particular, we see RegTech
  38. across traditional financial institutions,
  39. across new startups,
  40. and also across regulators, themselves.
  41. And the global financial crisis
  42. really made this a necessity.
  43. Since 2008,
  44. we have seen an absolute explosion
  45. in new regulations around the world.
  46. New regulations are released
  47. by a major jurisdiction
  48. approximately once every hour.
  49. The end result is that,
  50. every year,
  51. thousands of new regulations come out.
  52. And, for a financial institution
  53. doing business on a cross-boarder basis,
  54. this is one of the most complex influences
  55. on their operations
  56. as well as on their profitability
  57. because, not only is regulation a challenge,
  58. but it is also a cost.
  59. As a result,
  60. the global financial crisis
  61. and its explosion of regulation
  62. has driven the established financial industry
  63. into applying technology
  64. to address their compliance burdens
  65. and their compliance costs.
  66. At the same time,
  67. we have seen an explosion
  68. in new startups, new firms,
  69. which are offering technologies
  70. to help both startups
  71. as well as traditional financial institutions
  72. and even regulators
  73. to better address
  74. their regulatory and compliance burdens.
  75. Finally, regulators themselves
  76. are increasingly using technology
  77. for a range of purposes
  78. not only to do a better job
  79. in their regulatory functions,
  80. but also to increase market efficiencies
  81. and reduce cost in the industry.

4.4 RegTech Eco-system: Financial Institutions

  1. The RegTech ecosystem encompasses
  2. both industry and regulators.
  3. It encompasses
  4. the traditional incumbent financial institutions
  5. like banks,
  6. insurance companies,
  7. and investment banks.
  8. It also encompasses startups
  9. of an increasing range
  10. and it also encompasses regulators.
  11. However, most of the development
  12. that we’ve seen in the area
  13. of RegTech over the past decade
  14. following the global financial crisis
  15. has been focused
  16. in the traditional financial services industry,
  17. in particular, in the banking industry.
  18. And we can see this obviously simply
  19. from the cost of regulation.
  20. Not only have we had thousands of
  21. new regulations in the US,
  22. in Europe,
  23. in Hong Kong,
  24. in Singapore,
  25. and Australia,
  26. in economies all over the world
  27. as a result of the global financial crisis.
  28. But large financial institutions around the world
  29. have paid to date over $300 billion US dollars
  30. in fines for regulatory failures
  31. both resulting from and also in the aftermath
  32. of the global financial crisis.
  33. Compliance has become a major challenge
  34. from the standpoint of the industry
  35. both from the standpoint of
  36. simply keeping up with regulatory changes
  37. and their implications,
  38. but also from the standpoint of
  39. the very, very large fines
  40. that we have seen
  41. coming out of major regulators
  42. since the global financial crisis.
  43. And if we look today at
  44. traditional financial institutions,
  45. if we look at RegTech in banks,
  46. probably two areas highlight best
  47. the necessity of technology.
  48. First, are what are called
  49. know your customer requirements.
  50. Know your customer requirements
  51. are something that every financial institution
  52. everywhere in the world must comply with.
  53. The origin of
  54. know your customer requirements
  55. comes from a series of regulations
  56. that focus on preventing criminal use
  57. of the financial system,
  58. in particular,
  59. things such a money laundering
  60. or the financing of terrorism.
  61. Internationally, we have an organisation
  62. which is called the FATF.
  63. The FATF is the Financial Action Task Force
  64. on Money Laundering
  65. and it establishes
  66. internationally agreed minimum standards
  67. for regulation in financial systems
  68. with which every financial institution
  69. around the world must comply.
  70. Now, the challenge is that
  71. these are simply agreements.
  72. They then have to be implemented
  73. into the individual regulatory systems
  74. of jurisdictions of countries around the world.
  75. The end result is that the AML,
  76. Anti-Money Laundering requirements,
  77. the KYC, Know Your Customer requirements
  78. in the United States,
  79. or Europe,
  80. or Singapore,
  81. or Hong Kong,
  82. or Japan,
  83. Mainland China
  84. are all very similar but not identical.
  85. Now, if you are a large financial institution
  86. which is doing business
  87. across these major economies,
  88. part of your regulatory requirements
  89. are to comply with the requirements
  90. for KYC and AML
  91. in each jurisdiction in which you operate.
  92. And the number of regulators,
  93. particularly those in the United States
  94. have put in place some very large fines,
  95. multiple billions of US dollars
  96. against large financial institutions
  97. like HSBC,
  98. Standard Chartered,
  99. BNP Paribas
  100. for failures to
  101. properly have global systems
  102. to know their customers
  103. and prevent money laundering.
  104. As a result,
  105. financial institutions have had to
  106. implement systems
  107. whereby they can at a moment’s notice
  108. know who all of their,
  109. in many cases,
  110. tens of millions of customers all over the world are,
  111. and where they’ve gotten their money,
  112. and whether they raise any risks
  113. of criminal use of the financial system
  114. or terrorist financing.
  115. And the only way
  116. the financial institutions
  117. have been able to do this
  118. is spending large amounts of money
  119. on employing people
  120. and building technological systems
  121. to standardise account opening processes
  122. and the various reporting requirements
  123. that money laundering
  124. and KYC regulations
  125. around the world apply.
  126. Another area that we’ll see
  127. is what is called MiFID II.
  128. MiFID II is the Markets in Financial Instruments Directive
  129. in the European Union.
  130. And MiFID II requires transparency.
  131. It requires disclosure of
  132. massive amounts of information
  133. in particular about financial institutions,
  134. securities and derivatives trading activities.
  135. A very good example,
  136. Merrill Lynch in 2017 was fined
  137. almost 45 million British pounds
  138. for failing to report
  139. almost 75 million transactions
  140. over a two year period.
  141. Now, that is
  142. roughly 150,000 transactions a day
  143. for every business day
  144. over that two year period.
  145. And the only way that
  146. a financial institution
  147. can possibly keep track of
  148. that amount of trading activity is through
  149. building automated compliance systems
  150. to package and report
  151. the information to regulators.
  152. So, from the standpoint of regulators,
  153. the incumbent financial industry,
  154. particularly banks have so far been
  155. a major driving force.

4.5 RegTech Eco-system: Start-ups

  1. Predicted to be one of the fastest growing sector
  2. of 2018, the RegTech startup ecosystem
  3. has grown rapidly to match that expectation.
  4. Entrepreneurs are driven
  5. by the US$100 billion market opportunity
  6. that represent compliance spending.
  7. This has created a market of over 300 startups
  8. which is fueled by
  9. cumulative US$100 billion of investments
  10. by VCs since 2012.
  11. To better understand the RegTech startup ecosystem,
  12. let’s start with some context.
  13. With the USA setting the tempo
  14. of global regulatory changes
  15. and characterised by a fragmentation
  16. of supervisory bodies,
  17. most of compliance spending will occur there.
  18. However, whilst the USA represents
  19. a strong natural client,
  20. the startup creation activity
  21. is predominantly based in Europe.
  22. As for Asia, the region represents
  23. 1/3 of global compliance spending
  24. but is underserved by home-grown RegTech startups.
  25. The 2008 financial crisis
  26. has represented a strong catalyst
  27. for regulatory changes across the world.
  28. The combination of fines, over 321 billion,
  29. regulatory changes which have tripled
  30. in the last three years
  31. and post-crisis reform implementation
  32. such as Dodd-Frank or Basel III
  33. has forced banks to increase their operating costs
  34. as a response to their new regulatory obligation.
  35. To just give you a sense of scale,
  36. a financial institution like JPMorgan has added
  37. another 14,000 legal and compliance staff since 2012.
  38. It is not unusual for banks
  39. to have 20 to 30% of their employees
  40. working in compliance-related function.
  41. This means that a tier one universal bank
  42. has more compliance officers and lawyers
  43. than Facebook has total employees.
  44. However, the number of recurring fines
  45. occurring post crisis is challenging the effectiveness.
  46. However, the numbers of recurring fines
  47. occurring post-crisis is challenging
  48. the effectiveness of simply adding up
  49. human resources to meet compliance obligation.
  50. Indeed, for each one dollar spent on compliance,
  51. three are being spent on regulatory fine.
  52. It seems that the compliance industry
  53. has difficulty to learn from its mistake.
  54. This is what RegTech startups
  55. are trying to address.
  56. A decade has passed since the crisis
  57. and since then most regulatory changes
  58. have been implemented.
  59. Financial institution are therefore now starting
  60. to look at how to automate compliance obligation
  61. and decrease the added recurring cost
  62. that has built up post crisis.
  63. RegTech startups are answering these demands.
  64. RegTech companies can be classified
  65. in three broad categories.
  66. Each time I will quickly illustrate them.
  67. First, regulatory compliance.
  68. Here we are going to find companies
  69. that are learning about regulatory changes
  70. and telling banks how this is going to impact
  71. their business logic.
  72. Second, risk management.
  73. It’s about identifying, for example,
  74. conduct risk to prevent another Libor scandal.
  75. Third, financial crime.
  76. How can we understand the ultimate beneficiary
  77. behind a shell company to avoid money laundering
  78. or terrorism financing?
  79. Within these three categories,
  80. the vast majority of startups
  81. are found in the regulatory compliance space.
  82. This reflects the fact
  83. that this represents a low hanging fruit for success
  84. mainly because data is available,
  85. it requires limited integration
  86. and it has a lower risk factor
  87. in case of error by a RegTech company.
  88. Similarly to FinTech companies,
  89. the majority of RegTech companies
  90. are B2B providers selling to financial institutions.
  91. Whilst demand from this client base is strong,
  92. it appears that the sales cycle remains long.
  93. On average, we’re talking about 12 months.
  94. Certain exceptions are noted
  95. especially in the context of
  96. upcoming regulatory deadline
  97. such as MiFID II or GDPR
  98. which therefore fast track the sales cycles
  99. and the procurement process.
  100. Additionally, I would like to mention
  101. that RegTech startups can also be found
  102. in two other areas worth mentioning.
  103. Firstly, regulators.
  104. The B2G or business-to-government space is growing.
  105. Regulators globally are engaging RegTech startups
  106. via various channels from hackathons
  107. to accelerators to find solution
  108. to enhance the supervisory and regulatory function.
  109. Similarly to financial institution,
  110. regulators are driven by the cost benefit
  111. provided by RegTech startups
  112. that can reach a factor of up to 10 times.
  113. These savings are actually especially important
  114. when considering the fact that tax payers’ money
  115. is used to finance regulators
  116. and their operating costs
  117. making it a strong public policy case.
  118. However, whilst having regulators as a client
  119. brings a lot of legitimacy,
  120. the lengthy procurement process
  121. is extended by additional tendering rules
  122. relative to sourcing supplies
  123. from the public sector.
  124. Secondly, certain FinTech companies
  125. are directly adding regulatory compliance process
  126. into their product.
  127. For example, in the context
  128. of wealth management space,
  129. funds products are now being sold
  130. and marketed only to pre-qualified investors
  131. by leveraging on their data
  132. to determine suitability, location and investment profile.
  133. Whilst for consumers, this provides
  134. a level of personalization of services;
  135. for financial institutions,
  136. this embeds regulatory compliance
  137. into the sales process
  138. and that’s important
  139. because it allows to avoid fines relative to mis-selling.
  140. Previously what the financial crisis has highlighted
  141. was that compliant and sales function
  142. were operating in silos.
  143. This overview concludes
  144. the RegTech startups ecosystem of Module 4.

4.6 RegTech Start-ups: Challenges

  1. Welcome back.
  2. Let’s talk about the very exciting topic of RegTech,
  3. RegTech, short for regulatory technology.
  4. But first, what is RegTech?
  5. RegTech is the use of new technologies
  6. to solve regulatory and compliance burdens
  7. not only more effectively but also more efficiently,
  8. and this has been an area that has been
  9. really booming in recent months and years.
  10. Why is that?
  11. Well, since the financial crisis,
  12. banks and other financial institutions
  13. have hired thousands of compliance officers
  14. or risk officers to try to help them comply with
  15. the various regulatory requirements around the world.
  16. Well, the good news now is we could use
  17. some of the latest technology,
  18. from artificial intelligence to big data analytics,
  19. to try to tackle some of these issues
  20. in many ways better than humans can.
  21. Let me give you a good example,
  22. anti-money laundering.
  23. Today, trying to stop the proceeds
  24. from criminal organisations or rogue nations
  25. from entering the global financial ecosystem
  26. is a big challenge.
  27. And, despite all our efforts,
  28. of all the different AML procedures
  29. and prophecies that we have in place,
  30. the success has not always been there.
  31. According to a recent study,
  32. we’re only able to capture
  33. less than 1% to 2% of laundered transactions
  34. around the world from entering the financial ecosystem.
  35. Well, the good news is this is hopefully
  36. an area that RegTech can help.
  37. We can use artificial intelligence
  38. and actually try to actually spark
  39. and actually improve the process of how we filter
  40. and actually monitor these transactions.
  41. And this is happening across
  42. various, various, various verticals
  43. of the broader regulatory space,
  44. from KYC, know your customer,
  45. and onboarding to regulatory reporting.
  46. But although RegTech sounds very interesting,
  47. that has also a lot of challenges.
  48. For example, if you’re a RegTech startup
  49. and you’re trying to sell to a bank,
  50. the sale cycles can be very, very long.
  51. Think about it.
  52. You know you’re dealing with
  53. a lot of sensitive information
  54. and actually a lot of regulatory information,
  55. where the downside for a bank for getting
  56. it wrong is actually very big.
  57. So, we actually are seeing these long sale cycles
  58. that are common when you’re trying to sell to a bank
  59. actually be even longer when it comes to RegTech.
  60. Also, not everybody,
  61. when it comes to in risk or compliance functions
  62. are as familiar with RegTech.
  63. While a lot of the business functions of a bank
  64. got familiar with FinTech in the last couple of years,
  65. we’re still in the early stages when it comes to RegTech
  66. with people in risk, compliance, or legal functions.
  67. But also probably more importantly,
  68. if a bank wants to use this new technology
  69. they need to be able to integrate this
  70. inside of the spaghetti of their legacy systems,
  71. all these different systems that the bank has been
  72. implementing over the years and now try to find a way
  73. to use this new technology to come in
  74. and actually be able to use it.
  75. So, there are still a lot of challenges,
  76. but the good news is we finally
  77. have a lot of the solutions available
  78. to help financial institutions,
  79. not only be more effective, but also be more efficient
  80. when it comes to regulations.

4.7 RegTech Ecosystem: Regulators

  1. The RegTech Ecosystem,
  2. in addition to traditional financial institutions
  3. and startups,
  4. also extends to regulators themselves.
  5. Think about it for a moment.
  6. In the context of KYC requirements,
  7. one of the major elements
  8. of a compliance framework,
  9. of the regulatory framework,
  10. is filing of what are called
  11. suspicious transaction reports.
  12. Any time a transaction,
  13. in most jurisdictions,
  14. over 10,000 US dollars is done in cash,
  15. that financial institution will have to
  16. file a report with the regulator.
  17. Likewise, any time
  18. any unusual transaction takes place
  19. with any of a bank’s millions of clients,
  20. a suspicious transaction report must be filed.
  21. Now, think about this
  22. from the standpoint of regulators
  23. that are receiving
  24. thousands of suspicious transaction reports
  25. from banks every single day.
  26. What do they do with these?
  27. Is it simply pieces of paper
  28. that they store in a warehouse?
  29. And historically,
  30. that is very often been the case.
  31. If an event would happen,
  32. a terrorist attack
  33. or a criminal conspiracy of some sort,
  34. regulators would then
  35. trawl back through those records
  36. to see if they could identify
  37. any additional information.
  38. But a better system would be
  39. regulators putting those reports
  40. in a digital format,
  41. which can be immediately
  42. subjected to data analytics,
  43. which would not only be useful
  44. after a terrorist attack occurs,
  45. in the context of tracing the financial flow,
  46. but it could potentially be used
  47. to prevent attacks
  48. or a criminal use
  49. of the financial system.
  50. And that is the idea of RegTech.
  51. Regulators themselves applying technology
  52. to achieve better regulatory outcomes.
  53. After all, anti-money laundering rules
  54. are not about
  55. producing suspicious transaction reports.
  56. They are about
  57. reducing the criminal or terrorist use
  58. of the financial system.
  59. And so,
  60. if technology can be applied
  61. by both the financial institutions themselves,
  62. as well as regulators,
  63. to better achieve that objective
  64. that is a very important development,
  65. and one of the biggest trends
  66. that we’re seeing in the context of
  67. today’s financial markets.
  68. Now, if we look at regulators,
  69. it is, in fact, not new for them
  70. to use technology in regulation.
  71. One of the longer-standing uses of technology
  72. by regulators in the context of regulation
  73. occurs in the context of insider trader,
  74. insider dealing.
  75. Insider dealing takes place
  76. when an insider of a company
  77. listed on a stock exchange
  78. buys or sells shares of that company
  79. on the basis of information,
  80. which is not available to the public.
  81. Remember we saw
  82. with the efficient market’s hypothesis
  83. that the idea of market’s functioning
  84. is based upon availability
  85. and if someone is using information
  86. to profit in markets,
  87. which is not more widely available,
  88. one, that is not good
  89. for market functioning
  90. and market trust,
  91. but it’s also unfair to other participants.
  92. One of the most common places or times
  93. that insider dealing takes place
  94. is in the context of
  95. the announcement of a merger or acquisition.
  96. That will typically take place
  97. at a certain day,
  98. a certain time,
  99. and regulators will use
  100. the electronic trading records
  101. of the stock exchange,
  102. and will look back
  103. through six months of data
  104. to see if there were
  105. any unusual transactions by insiders,
  106. like corporate directors or their families,
  107. during the period in the run-up
  108. to that announcement.
  109. That could then trigger an investigation
  110. and potentially an enforcement action.
  111. That is RegTech.
  112. Today if we’re looking at areas of
  113. regulators using RegTech,
  114. probably the three biggest that we see are,
  115. first, in the context of
  116. applying big data analytics techniques
  117. to the massive amounts of regulatory filings,
  118. which have exploded
  119. since the 2008 global financial crisis.
  120. Like a large financial institution
  121. is having to make several thousand reports
  122. to regulators around the world every day,
  123. regulators likewise
  124. are receiving thousands of reports,
  125. and are increasingly using that information,
  126. subjecting it to data analytics,
  127. in order to identify potential market risks
  128. or market violations.
  129. A related idea is
  130. what is called macroprudential policy.
  131. Prior to the global financial crisis,
  132. regulators tended to focus on
  133. the safety and soundness
  134. of individual financial institutions.
  135. The idea was that if you prevented
  136. each individual bank from failing
  137. that would prevent a financial crisis.
  138. What the 2008 crisis showed is that
  139. sometimes it is the interlinkages
  140. between participants in a financial system
  141. that causes the crisis.
  142. It is not what one is doing,
  143. but the fact that
  144. everyone is doing the same thing,
  145. and when a certain trigger event happens,
  146. it causes problems
  147. in the entire financial system.
  148. Since the global financial crisis,
  149. regulators have spent
  150. a very significant amount of effort and resources
  151. on coming up with ways
  152. to try to prevent crises from happening
  153. before they take place.
  154. And one of those areas
  155. is using data analytics,
  156. in particular in the context of regulatory filings,
  157. to try to identify risks
  158. prior to them actually happening.
  159. And finally, one that we’ve seen before,
  160. cybersecurity.
  161. As the financial system
  162. has become increasingly digitised,
  163. that has also increased its risks
  164. of hacking and cybersecurity.
  165. Perhaps the best example of this in recent years
  166. has been the hacking of Equifax.
  167. Equifax is a large company in the United States,
  168. which provides credit rating services.
  169. It collects massive amounts of data,
  170. uses that data to provide a credit score,
  171. which banks and other financial institutions
  172. then use as the basis of lending decisions.
  173. In 2017, it announced that all of its data
  174. had been hacked
  175. of over 140 million people.
  176. And that highlights that,
  177. as a financial system becomes more digitised,
  178. the risks of cybersecurity go up.
  179. And many would say today that
  180. cybersecurity is not the biggest financial risk,
  181. it’s not the biggest economic risk.
  182. Many would, in fact, say,
  183. that it’s the biggest national security risk as well.
  184. Because, after all, in addition to
  185. disrupting or stealing money,
  186. hackers can also seek to
  187. bring down large financial institutions
  188. in an effort to cause
  189. the collapse of the financial system
  190. and the economy.
  191. So, like financial institutions and startups,
  192. regulators, too, are a major part
  193. of the RegTech Ecosystem.

Payments & the Regulatory Landscape in Asia Pacific (KorumLegal)

  1. Hello, welcome to this lecture on payment services
  2. and the regulatory landscape for them in Asia Pacific.
  3. I’m Danh Nguyen, the General Manager
  4. and Managing Consultant for EMEA of KorumLegal.
  5. Most people are familiar with
  6. the term “financial services”.
  7. Less is understood about “payments”.
  8. Not many people know or understand what it means.
  9. “Payments” has traditionally been neglected
  10. or underserviced as an area of the law
  11. and as a subset of general banking
  12. and financial services.
  13. This is out of step with how payments
  14. have quickly evolved to become a hotspot of
  15. commercial and FinTech activity in recent years.
  16. In addition to the more traditional players
  17. such as the high street banks
  18. and credit card companies,
  19. like MasterCard and Visa,
  20. and global remittance players like Western Union,
  21. MoneyGram, PayPal,
  22. new players are constantly emerging.
  23. These include Monzo, Airwallex, TransferWise,
  24. WorldRemit, TransferGo, Aussie Forex,
  25. Paybase, Azimo, the list goes on.
  26. The level of innovation
  27. in this space is dizzying
  28. and many hundreds of millions of dollars
  29. are being poured into these companies
  30. by astute investors.
  31. So, what do we mean by payment service?
  32. At its simplest, a payment service
  33. is any service provided by a financial institution
  34. to allow a person or a business to pay
  35. another person or business
  36. for a product or service.
  37. A payment service provider offers merchants
  38. online services for accepting electronic payments
  39. by a variety of payment methods
  40. which includes credit cards,
  41. bank-based payments such as
  42. direct debit, bank transfers,
  43. and real-time bank transfers
  44. based on online banking.
  45. Typically, they use a Software as a Service model
  46. and form a single payment gateway
  47. for their clients to multiple payment methods.
  48. Payments also cover
  49. money transfer and remittances,
  50. cross-border payments
  51. including consumer to consumer,
  52. business to business, consumer to business,
  53. and business to consumer,
  54. and credit cards, debit cards,
  55. stored-value cards, and so on.
  56. Now that you understand better
  57. what is a payment service,
  58. let’s now touch on the regulatory frameworks
  59. for payment services in the Asia Pacific region.
  60. Until about eight years ago,
  61. the regulatory landscape for payments in APAC
  62. was relatively light-touch.
  63. Regulations were playing catch-up
  64. with the new technologies
  65. which allowed for an abundance of
  66. new products and services to emerge,
  67. including FinTechs.
  68. The existing regulatory frameworks in many countries
  69. were simply not equipped to
  70. address the many challenges
  71. and risks posed by these new technologies,
  72. products and services.
  73. Most of these laws were enacted at a time
  74. when these technologies did not exist
  75. or weren’t contemplated.
  76. For example, in Singapore,
  77. the relevant law,
  78. the Money Changing and Remittance Business Act,
  79. dates from 1979 and remained largely intact
  80. with few substantive updates
  81. or modifications for 40 years.
  82. The new law to replace it,
  83. the Payment Services Act 2019,
  84. was only gazetted in February 2019.
  85. It’s expected to come into effect sometime
  86. at the end of this year or early 2020.
  87. In Australia, remittance services
  88. and remittance service providers
  89. were not regulated until 2011
  90. when the Anti-Money Laundering
  91. and Counter-Terrorism Financing Act, 2006
  92. was substantially amended to provide for this.
  93. Lawmakers could not have imagined
  94. the full potential of technology
  95. to fundamentally transform how payment services
  96. would be provided to consumers
  97. and businesses in the digital age.
  98. Few could have predicted that you could send
  99. and receive money via your online messaging app,
  100. or with a few simple clicks on your smartphone,
  101. you can open a bank account
  102. or apply for a debit card.
  103. But lawmakers and
  104. regulators entrusted to enforce them
  105. have been catching up.
  106. They’ve been busy enacting new laws,
  107. or amending existing ones,
  108. to regulate these new activities and products.
  109. Since 2010, we’ve seen new payments
  110. or funds transfer laws being enacted
  111. in Japan, Singapore, Malaysia,
  112. Thailand, Indonesia, Australia,
  113. New Zealand and the Philippines.
  114. While each national law is different,
  115. they all essentially share some common themes.
  116. They all focus on AML/CTF compliance,
  117. good governance,
  118. effective technology risk management,
  119. the need for robust internal controls
  120. and frameworks, and consumer protection.
  121. For example, those against risks of fraud
  122. and unauthorised use
  123. or mishandling of customer funds.
  124. I will now speak a little more in-depth
  125. about one of these new laws,
  126. the Payment Services Act in Singapore.
  127. The stated objectives of
  128. the Monetary Authority of Singapore,
  129. the Central Bank of Singapore,
  130. were to streamline payment services
  131. under a single legislation,
  132. enhance the scope of regulated activities
  133. to address current and future developments
  134. in payment services,
  135. calibrate regulations according to the risks
  136. the activities pose by
  137. adopting a modular regulatory regime.
  138. The Payment Services Act
  139. adopts an activity-based approach
  140. to the licensing and regulation of payment services
  141. and classifies them under seven key activities.
  142. You can see these on the screen right now.
  143. More than one activity can be engaged in,
  144. but only one licence will be
  145. needed to cover all activities.
  146. Retail payment activities will be grouped
  147. into three main licence classes.
  148. Money changing licence,
  149. standard payment institution licence,
  150. and major payment institution licence.
  151. The regulation of licensees
  152. is calibrated according to their activities
  153. based on the risks
  154. and these regulatory concerns.
  155. Money laundering and terrorism financing,
  156. consumer user protection, interoperability,
  157. and technology risk management.
  158. No doubt, the emergence of new technologies
  159. has created tremendous opportunities
  160. for new players to enter
  161. this exciting and dynamic market.
  162. The financial services sector
  163. was ripe for disruption.
  164. Consumers have greater choice
  165. when it comes to the types and
  166. availability of financial services products.
  167. This has also enhanced financial inclusion
  168. and access for everyone.
  169. But with new opportunities,
  170. there are additional legal
  171. and compliance obligations and risk.
  172. Regulatory and compliance burdens for players
  173. seeking to operate in this space
  174. can be quite onerous
  175. and shouldn’t be underestimated.
  176. Scrutiny from regulators in the region
  177. has increased significantly.
  178. Regulators expect payment service providers
  179. to have in place robust compliance,
  180. risk management and governance frameworks
  181. to ensure that they can meet their legal
  182. and regulatory obligations,
  183. and to protect consumer rights and interests.
  184. There needs to be a thoughtful approach
  185. to compliance and risk management
  186. which will help the company to establish
  187. disciplined management of financial crimes,
  188. operational risk and consumer protection.
  189. Regulators also expect providers to
  190. take active steps to enforce the compliance
  191. and risk management framework,
  192. and to monitor compliance
  193. against the requirements.
  194. Navigating through these complex and
  195. onerous legal and regulatory regimes
  196. can be daunting.
  197. The costs of compliance can be high.
  198. In some cases, this may affect
  199. the commercial viability of their business.
  200. So having in place a well-thought out strategy
  201. and an effective and robust compliance
  202. and risk management framework will help
  203. the payment services providers
  204. to take full advantage of the opportunities
  205. that the new technologies offer.
  206. It can also cushion the business
  207. from the downside risks
  208. of running a non-compliant business.
  209. The legal, financial and reputational risks
  210. for the company
  211. and for its senior management team
  212. are far too great
  213. to simply ignore or downplay.
  214. But where the company has a good understanding
  215. of the regulatory requirements
  216. and a culture of compliance is promoted
  217. and embraced by senior management,
  218. the company is positioned well for success
  219. in this burgeoning area.

  1. Well, I think regulators are looking at technologies
  2. and startups such as MindBridge
  3. to be able to introduce new technologies and new ideas
  4. faster into the industry.
  5. In particular, in our case, we’re working with
  6. machine learning, artificial intelligence
  7. with organisations such as the Bank of England.
  8. And using that technology,
  9. they’re able to better review and regulate
  10. say the credit unions in the United Kingdom.
  11. So they use machine learning, artificial intelligence
  12. to be able to look at the liquidity ratios,
  13. make sure that the banks are performing
  14. the way they’re supposed to,
  15. and analysing for things such as trend shock
  16. when the bank introduces a new policy,
  17. how are all the credit unions changing
  18. in relation to each other
  19. and being able to better understand
  20. the different changes in policy that
  21. bank makes relative to the credit unions.

  23. startups are a cost benefit

  32. say the Hong Kong Monetary Authority

  35. to better understand the regulatory market.

  38. has already started to become a widely used term

  42. and I think it’s quickly gaining pace here in Asia.

4.8 Regulatory Sandboxes

  1. We’re now going to talk about Regulatory Sandboxes,
  2. a safe harbour for
  3. supervised innovation in financial market.
  4. The general perception of regulators
  5. is that they are slow and reactive.
  6. The Dodd-Frank Act illustrates this.
  7. It was passed in 2010,
  8. but its full implementation
  9. remains a work in progress.
  10. In almost ten years that have passed
  11. since the great financial crisis,
  12. what has emerged strongly is FinTech.
  13. Whilst the regulators have been
  14. implementing post-crisis reform,
  15. entrepreneurs have been busy.
  16. Today with the regulatory houses in order
  17. regulators are being to also embrace
  18. forward-looking innovation.
  19. The best illustration of this is the speed
  20. at which Regulatory Sandboxes have been announced.
  21. So far, over 12 have been announced in the world,
  22. out of which six have emerged in the last four months
  23. and the majority of them are in Asia.
  24. One might argue that
  25. the boom in Regulatory Sandboxes
  26. is yet another reactionary move driven by
  27. the jurisdiction’s fear of missing out
  28. on the chance of becoming or staying
  29. a meaningful financial centre.
  30. One can also argue that the intent behind establishing
  31. a Sandbox matter as much, or more,
  32. than their narrow regulatory outcome.
  33. Establishing a Sandbox demonstrates
  34. a Regulator’s desire to move forward
  35. towards a more proportionate regulatory framework
  36. that bounces risk with innovation.
  37. And whilst most Regulators seems to have realised
  38. that Sandboxes are not child’s play
  39. many are yet to invest adequate resources
  40. into their operation,
  41. either from a human capital perspective
  42. or technical capacity.
  43. Similarly, as with startups,
  44. Regulators will have to enter into
  45. a reiteration cycle to improve
  46. the quality of and add value to their Sandboxes.
  47. Regulators will need to keep their visions alive
  48. as Sandboxes are unlikely in the first year
  49. to deliver much by ways of result.
  50. Perhaps most importantly,
  51. regulators must ensure
  52. that in the event of another crisis
  53. they do not fully revert from the proactive approach
  54. that they have towards yet another reactive one.
  55. In other words, the innovative spirit
  56. of regulators needs to be nurtured and maintained
  57. as it does with entrepreneurs.
  58. Each needs to learn from the sum of their experience.
  59. Similarly to startups,
  60. regulators should cherish
  61. the journey because innovation
  62. and its effective regulation are ever evolving processes.
  63. This is not to say that regulation
  64. always follows innovation.
  65. India and Europe have demonstrated how reform
  66. can spur innovation and the rise of FinTech.
  67. However, we need to ask the question
  68. of how a proactive
  69. and adaptive regulatory framework can be created.
  70. At the core of this vision lies the numbers of
  71. well explored and established principle for regulators.
  72. First, financial stability at the macro and micro level.
  73. Second, market integrity and
  74. third, consumer protection.
  75. The challenge is using technology to develop
  76. a better way of doing things
  77. from the standpoint of regulators,
  78. the industry and infrastructure.
  79. As an example, whilst all regulators agree
  80. on the importance of AML and KYC,
  81. there has been limited harmonisation
  82. with respect to this most common form of compliance.
  83. Too often, the spirit of the law
  84. is distorted by its implementation.
  85. In the context of banks, this often takes
  86. the form of banks and regulators blaming
  87. the other for each failing to take
  88. the responsibility in addressing
  89. the question of why they cannot innovate.
  90. Lack of innovation is blamed on
  91. a mutual misunderstanding as to what is possible
  92. and how it can practically be achieved.
  93. Regulatory Sandboxes should become the new form
  94. for discussion in which the dominant outcome
  95. is promoting innovation rather
  96. than only trying to reduce risk.
  97. This means that the regulator should not seek
  98. to prevent all risk from occurring,
  99. but instead evaluate whether
  100. an innovation enhances or decreases risk
  101. in comparison to what already exists.
  102. Let’s take some examples.
  103. First, it’s about accepting driverless cars
  104. because they are statistically less likely
  105. to generate an accident as opposed
  106. to failing to support them because
  107. they have caused one or two accidents.
  108. Second, it’s about supporting facial recognition solution
  109. as the instances of fraud associated with them
  110. is likely to be far lower than
  111. with traditional chip and pin mechanism.
  112. For this to happen, regulators
  113. will need data and a lot of it.
  114. This is to ensure the true risk association
  115. with a given innovation are adequately estimated.
  116. Foreign experiences should also be taken
  117. into consideration as their data are used.
  118. The depth and size of the data necessary
  119. has placed immediate pressure
  120. on regulator’s resources.
  121. With limited staff,
  122. regulators will hit a bottleneck
  123. in their review, evaluation,
  124. and approval of innovation.
  125. As a result, regulators need to start
  126. embracing machine learning tools
  127. which will allow them
  128. to better understand large and unstructured data sets.
  129. For now, the largest simulation in the world is in China.
  130. China doesn’t need a regulatory Sandbox
  131. because the whole country is one.
  132. In its technology firm other kids playing in it.
  133. When Alibaba released
  134. its experimental credit scoring tool,
  135. Sesame Credit, it was quickly apparent
  136. that its credit scoring algorithm
  137. was being gained by its users.
  138. Sesame Credit was underpinned by the assumption
  139. that the more a person spends or receives,
  140. the more credit worthy they are.
  141. However people quickly started
  142. to send the same amount
  143. of money back and forth in order
  144. to increase their credit score.
  145. The unusual patent allotted to Alibaba
  146. and lent to the algorithm being readjusted.
  147. However, not every country has the capacity
  148. to run control experiment on the scale of China.
  149. These countries will either have to rely
  150. on machine learning tools or accept the risk
  151. of regulation lagging behind innovation.
  152. This is why Regulatory Sandboxes represent
  153. a critical addition to the toolkit
  154. of innovation available to regulators.

Industry Showcase: Balancing Innovation and Regulation Challenges in Hong Kong (Charles Mok)

  1. Now even in the context of China,
  2. we have seen some of the developments
  3. get to such a stage where they’ve had to come in,
  4. develop regulatory frameworks to balance out risks.
  5. And this is something that I think
  6. we’re seeing around the world
  7. and this is the idea of piloting,
  8. or a test-and-learn approach, regulatory sandboxes.
  9. And this idea of sandboxes was something
  10. that I wanted to ask you particularly about,
  11. coming from the tech side of things, and that is,

  14. It’s a good question.

  41. I have to partner with a bank?
  42. Which means that in some cases

  60. because that’s also important.
  61. Sometimes they say
  62. hey, we didn’t know that Hong Kong,

  64. that we could give it a try.

  66. that they have this regulatory sandbox.
  67. So I think now that we have something
  68. that might be might be good,
  69. we’d better give it the right promotion
  70. to make sure that more people
  71. around the world know about it,
  72. because after all, one of the most important things
  73. about FinTech that I believe, is that the nature of it
  74. is going to be quite global.
  75. And also that’s one of the advantages
  76. that Hong Kong has
  77. is being a traditional financial services centre.
  78. We have the expertise, we have the banking
  79. and the financial service expertise in Hong Kong,
  80. not to mention the technology expertise.
  81. And so we should leverage this particular advantage
  82. to attract more startups or people around the world
  83. to say hey, maybe I should give my idea a good try
  84. in Hong Kong.
  85. Yeah, I very much agree with that.

4.9 Smart Regulation

  1. We suggest that RegTech goes beyond
  2. simple use of technology
  3. for compliance or regulatory purposes
  4. and also extends to cooperative efforts
  5. between policymakers, regulators, startups,
  6. and traditional financial institutions
  7. to build better financial systems.
  8. We call this the idea of smart regulation.
  9. Smart regulation suggests that
  10. in the context of today’s technological transformation
  11. of the financial system,
  12. it is possible for the first time
  13. to redesign underlying infrastructure,
  14. the plumbing of the global financial system
  15. to make the financial system
  16. work better and effectively,
  17. The idea of this is first,
  18. a need for better information and monitoring
  19. that regulators need not only
  20. to follow what is going on
  21. in traditional financial institutions
  22. but they also need to be aware of new entrants,
  23. whether those are FinTech startups,
  24. or TechFins,
  25. and also evolution of technologies.
  26. Whether those technologies
  27. are things like cryptocurrencies,
  28. blockchain, cloud techniques
  29. or anything more that emerges
  30. in coming years.
  31. So the first stage is really
  32. an understanding of what is going on.
  33. Without an understanding,
  34. a regulator cannot do a proper job
  35. of balancing the objectives of
  36. economic growth and financial stability.
  37. From that basis,
  38. one can work together
  39. to design better systems.
  40. And really, the starting point in smart regulation
  41. is systems design.
  42. It is the idea of digitising regulation
  43. so that regulatory requirements
  44. to the greatest extent possible
  45. can be conducted or met by industry participants
  46. in a digital form.
  47. Digitising reporting requirements
  48. and other compliance requirements
  49. allows financial institutions to submit reporting
  50. and other obligations to
  51. regulators in a digitised form.
  52. That process of digitization
  53. immediately makes it more straightforward
  54. for regulators as well as the industry
  55. to apply processes of data analytics,
  56. of datafication
  57. in order not only to
  58. better achieve regulatory objectives,
  59. but also to reduce costs
  60. and increase efficiencies
  61. as well as discover new opportunities.
  62. The idea of smart regulation
  63. is that technology is no longer the limiting factor
  64. in how a financial system or its regulation works.
  65. But to take advantage of these new opportunities,
  66. we must be aware of
  67. what the technology can do
  68. as well as the existing inefficiencies
  69. in many of our systems,
  70. and that is a RegTech process
  71. of designing financial infrastructure.

4.10 Redesigning Financial Intrastructure: India Stack

  1. This idea of designing better financial infrastructure
  2. is core to the idea of FinTech and RegTech’s
  3. re-conceptualization and reconsideration,
  4. recreation of both domestic
  5. and global financial systems.
  6. We can see this happening
  7. in two big examples.
  8. The first is one that
  9. we’ve already looked at in the EU.
  10. The development of a series of
  11. new legal frameworks
  12. reflected in MiFID II, PSD2, and GDPR,
  13. which are transforming the way
  14. that the financial system
  15. in the European Union works.
  16. The second is in the context of India.
  17. And something that we’ve seen
  18. throughout the course so far
  19. is that China is probably the most advanced,
  20. the most exciting digital transformation
  21. that we’ve seen so far in the context
  22. of this FinTech environment,
  23. but the other transformation
  24. which is taking place now
  25. is in many ways just as exciting,
  26. and that is the digital transformation
  27. that is taking place in India.
  28. In India about eight years ago,
  29. a group of tech entrepreneurs
  30. set about designing a strategy
  31. to build a new system of digital infrastructure
  32. to transform not only the Indian financial system
  33. but access to finance
  34. by the majority of the Indian population
  35. and also India’s economy
  36. and economic growth prospects more generally.
  37. In some ways, this was partially a reaction
  38. to the success that we’ve seen
  39. in China’s long-term economic transformation
  40. since the late 1970s.
  41. The idea in India is a series of interlinked pieces
  42. called India Stack.
  43. The foundation of the India Stack
  44. is a new digital biometric ID system.
  45. That digital biometric ID system
  46. includes 10 fingerprints
  47. and two iris scans
  48. for each individual who is issued with an ID.
  49. And since 2010,
  50. over 1.2 billion people in India
  51. have been issued with new digital IDs.
  52. That is more than the iPhone sold
  53. in the same period from its launch in 2007.
  54. The digital IDs in India
  55. are the largest IT rollout
  56. in the history of the world.
  57. Well, that’s a good start.
  58. Digital identity to make it possible
  59. for immediate authentication
  60. of any individual’s identity.
  61. The second level has been a system built on
  62. an electronic payment system that is open,
  63. open API,
  64. which means that it is open
  65. not only to traditional banks,
  66. but also to new entrants.
  67. This payment system allows digital payments
  68. to be made from one ID holder
  69. to another very rapidly.
  70. It also allows the entrance of
  71. new forms of business,
  72. whether those are payment providers
  73. or robo-advisory services
  74. or P2P lending and others.
  75. The third level involves
  76. increasing the use of bank accounts.
  77. And India three years ago,
  78. only about 1/3 of its population
  79. would have had a bank account,
  80. roughly 350 million people.
  81. Now, as a result of a process
  82. whereby government salaries are paid into accounts,
  83. pensions and other benefits,
  84. agricultural supports and the like
  85. are all paid into individual bank accounts,
  86. there has been an explosion
  87. in the number of bank accounts in India.
  88. Over the past five years,
  89. over 300 million new bank accounts
  90. have been opened in India,
  91. more than the population of the United States,
  92. and to the point where now for the first time,
  93. over half of the population of India
  94. has a bank account,
  95. a bank account that is linked to their digital ID
  96. and to an electronic payment system
  97. that allows instantaneous payments
  98. across the financial system.
  99. The final element of this system
  100. brings us back to the ideas of KYC.
  101. An e-KYC system, an e-KYC utility
  102. where individuals have
  103. a sort of Dropbox-like account
  104. where they can place digitised documents
  105. which are the necessary filing requirements
  106. for KYC requirements
  107. across a range of financial institutions.
  108. As a result of this combination of factors,
  109. India has not only exploded the number of people
  110. using bank accounts
  111. and using the financial system,
  112. it has also dramatically reduced the time taken
  113. and the cost for opening an account
  114. and as an added benefit
  115. dramatically reduced the cost of corruption
  116. in a previously cash-based economy.
  117. In November 2016,
  118. the Indian government announced
  119. an incredible experiment
  120. what was called demonetization.
  121. The government said that
  122. 86% of the cash bills in circulation in the country
  123. were no longer valid,
  124. that those paper notes
  125. had to be turned in at a bank
  126. and either left in a bank account
  127. or exchanged for new bills
  128. which would be issued.
  129. Now, the intention of this initiative
  130. was to crackdown on corruption,
  131. to basically catch dark money
  132. that was operating
  133. outside of the formal financial system,
  134. but its big impact was in the context of
  135. driving digital transformation
  136. of the Indian financial system.
  137. And that was possible
  138. because of the infrastructure
  139. which had already been put in place
  140. via the India Stack strategy.
  141. Literally, hundreds of millions of people
  142. for the first time
  143. are now using digital forms of money
  144. for payments, lending and investing
  145. that previously would have been cash based.
  146. We have seen an explosion
  147. in the amount of money in the financial system,
  148. which has reduced interest rates.
  149. It has also increased liquidity available
  150. for startups seeking to raise money
  151. in the economy.
  152. And as with China’s digital transformation,
  153. countries around the world
  154. but particularly in Asia
  155. have been watching India’s process very closely.
  156. And an increasing range of countries,
  157. from Bangladesh to Thailand
  158. to Indonesia and beyond
  159. are seeking to develop similar strategies
  160. to enable them like China has done
  161. and like India is in the process of doing
  162. to leapfrog
  163. to a new digitally based financial system.

References and Suggestions for Further Reading
