Module 5 Data & TechFin

Welcome to Module 5

5.1 Module 5 Introduction

  1. Welcome back to the HKU FinTech course.
  2. You are now in module 5,
  3. and we’ll be talking about data.
  4. As you may know, data is the new oil,
  5. and what this means is that it becomes
  6. an increasingly valuable commodity both for you
  7. as individuals, financial institutions,
  8. or technology companies.
  9. In this module, we will be covering
  10. each of these aspects.
  11. First, we’re going to be talking to you as an individual.
  12. How is GDPR in Europe
  13. covering and impacting businesses
  14. as well as you as an individual?
  15. What about digital identity?
  16. How a piece of password can now be condensed
  17. in a piece of data,
  18. and how that piece of data
  19. can better define who you are,
  20. and therefore customise experiences around you.
  21. We’ll then be also looking
  22. at the financial services industry.
  23. They have been using data since the 1970s,
  24. and have been able to deliver new financial products
  25. on the back of this.
  26. However, there is challenge on doing so at scale.
  27. Startups and the FinTech transformation
  28. is also one which is underlied by the use
  29. of technology and data accessibility
  30. both by technology companies
  31. as well as the financial institutions.
  32. On the technology company side,
  33. we’ll be talking about the challenges
  34. brought by TechFin companies,
  35. as well as the governments of AI.
  36. And finally, regulators.
  37. The increased value of data means
  38. that more and more people want to
  39. access it by legal and illegal means.
  40. And therefore, the regulation of data
  41. from a data privacy perspective,
  42. or the protection of data
  43. from a technological perspective,
  44. will also be addressed in this module.

Module 5 Learning Objectives

Module 5 discusses data, data regulation, data security and the emergence of TechFin. In focuses on both the opportunities as well as the risks and challenges arising from the process of digitization and datafication in finance, as well as policy and regulatory approaches and their implications for business.    

In Module 5, learners will:

  • Understand the role of data in financial services
  • Consider various approaches to data protection and privacy
  • Think about the challenges of digitization and datafication, particularly cybersecurity and technological risk
  • Discuss the emergence of TechFins and the implications for financial services    

5.2 History of Data Regulation

  1. When looking at data
  2. and how it has been regulated over time,
  3. there are different elements to it.
  4. First, we can approach data from a privacy perspective.
  5. A lot of you can relate to this
  6. when we look at data breaches that infect
  7. your personal information being released to the public.
  8. What has been happening with Facebook
  9. and how that data, for example,
  10. has been used in the context of political elections,
  11. both in the US and in Europe.
  12. Data privacy matters
  13. because it’s all about protecting your personal life,
  14. and that life is increasingly now online.
  15. The second element is about
  16. cross-border data management,
  17. and whilst digitization is a global phenomenon,
  18. cross-border data management
  19. is not necessarily something
  20. which is as automatic than one may think,
  21. and there’s some right reason to do so.
  22. Certain jurisdictions are, for example,
  23. much more stringent about data residency rules,
  24. where data can only be held and stored
  25. in a single country and not leave it.
  26. This is one of the big problems for FinTech companies.
  27. Whilst FinTech companies have global ambition,
  28. data regulation, especially on how
  29. cross-border data management is done,
  30. can be a real limiting factor for their ambition of growth.
  31. Why? Because for example, a regulator in a Country A
  32. may not want that the data of its consumer
  33. transferred to Country B where it will be processed
  34. and then the insight brought back to Country A.
  35. This can be for many different reasons,
  36. including national sovereignty and national security.
  37. Since 2007, the financial crisis has shown
  38. that financial services stability
  39. is a national sovereignty issue, and as a result,
  40. data residency and data protection is equally important.
  41. The third element is data management.
  42. This is how people will access and control their data.
  43. One of the topic that, for example,
  44. we’ve covered in Module 4 was GDPR,
  45. and here it’s about this idea that
  46. every single piece of data
  47. should be able to be traced back
  48. to its individual owner, if it identified him.
  49. And if it does, that owner should have the right
  50. of querying the institution who has seen that data,
  51. how many times the data has been used,
  52. and how the data is actually being used,
  53. and in which countries.
  54. This is the idea of data that could be accounted
  55. as a liability or an asset, from a financial perspective.
  56. However, from all perspective,
  57. data and its regulation
  58. should be looked from another angle.
  59. It should be looked from an angle
  60. where data equals money,
  61. and all the rights and obligation attributed to it
  62. should be similar to money.
  63. Data would therefore have a property right,
  64. a commercial right,
  65. and that can be transferred, exchanged, borrowed,
  66. or leased to someone else.
  67. And this matters a lot.
  68. It matters because it will allow digital identity
  69. and data sovereignty to keep on growing as an industry.
  70. It also matters because as the economy
  71. is going from a service economy to a digital economy,
  72. more and more individuals will be looking at ways
  73. of monetising their data to supplement,
  74. if not totally replace their income.
  75. So far, this topic of data regulation hasn’t emerged yet
  76. because the money that can be made
  77. from data monetisation
  78. has not passed the poverty line.
  79. However, when this will happen,
  80. a real question will be raised
  81. at the regulatory and the policy level
  82. on how that data regulation should be approached.
  83. From a trend perspective,
  84. we can imagine that this will mainly
  85. happen in countries like Asia.
  86. This is because, proportionally,
  87. the value of data from an individual in Asia
  88. is higher compared to their salary
  89. to what you would have in Europe.
  90. For example, someone in Vietnam
  91. earning 8,000 US dollar a year,
  92. but having their data worth 50 US dollar a year,
  93. has a higher value as a proportion
  94. of their data to their salary,
  95. compared to a European person
  96. earning 50,000 US dollars a year,
  97. but only having 50 US dollar worth of data.
  98. This is why the future of data regulation
  99. and data being treated as money
  100. is mainly going to come from places like Asia.
  101. However, so far, the regulatory tempo
  102. has clearly been set by Europe,
  103. which has one of the most stringent and complete
  104. regulatory regime around data management,
  105. which is followed across the world and including Asia.

5.3 Data in Financial Services

  1. The importance of data in the economy
  2. has been captured by Professor Klaus Schwab,
  3. executive chairman of the World Economic Forum.
  4. His analysis entails that the world
  5. is entering a fourth industrial revolution
  6. powered by artificial intelligence
  7. and being distinguished
  8. in its characteristic of hyper scalability,
  9. which we will discuss more in module 6.
  10. Importantly, the fourth industrial revolution
  11. has been built on top of the third era,
  12. which started in the 1970s,
  13. thanks to computer and automation.
  14. As we have seen throughout this course,
  15. technological progress
  16. and financial market development
  17. have a close relationship.
  18. Professor Schwab’s third era
  19. of computer and automation coincides
  20. with the digitization of financial services,
  21. which we discussed in module three.
  22. And in this lecture, we are instead
  23. projecting ourself forward,
  24. aiming to answer the following questions.
  25. How can the data created since the 1970s
  26. be used meaningfully in financial services?
  27. And what does the fourth industrial revolution look like
  28. for financial markets?
  29. The importance lies in the fact
  30. that as with any large economic shift,
  31. the relationship of money
  32. and power is being redistributed.
  33. As such, answering if financial institutions need
  34. to become data companies matters.
  35. Should their business model move
  36. towards a data refining instead
  37. of interest or fee-based income?
  38. In order to find answers,
  39. we will present three examples
  40. in exploring in turns, big data,
  41. artificial intelligence, and analytics tools.
  42. Let’s start with the hedge funds industry.
  43. They have historically been distinguishing themselves
  44. by their capacity of using quantitative skills
  45. and hiring a lot of Math scientists.
  46. They have increasingly been leveraging
  47. an external and new alternative data
  48. to make better investment decisions.
  49. For example, the use of geolocation data
  50. was used to estimate the footfall
  51. in shopping malls, and therefore predict
  52. the growth of sales ahead of public quarterly reports,
  53. allowing to make an investment decision
  54. before public information is out.
  55. In the context of the insurance industry,
  56. we have seen usage-based insurance changing
  57. the current flat-fee insurance model
  58. and instead moving towards
  59. a pay-as-you-go service
  60. centred around your lifestyle.
  61. This has been made possible by the increase
  62. of telematic solutions,
  63. such as trackers in your car,
  64. or your mobile phone,
  65. which is then analysed for better pricing.
  66. Here, data is being used
  67. to change the underwriting decision model
  68. whilst becoming the most cost-effective option
  69. for you as a customer.
  70. Finally, in banking,
  71. by using predictive analytics,
  72. a bank is able to monitor the spending patterns
  73. of a consumer, and then
  74. infer if a larger purchase will be made.
  75. The size of that purchase will then define whether
  76. the bank should proactively propose
  77. a consumer loan or mortgage.
  78. Likewise, a very simple example
  79. is taking a travel insurance as soon as
  80. you buy a plane ticket with your credit card.
  81. Now, these examples illustrate that data
  82. has previously been used in financial services,
  83. but this has not been equally done by all.
  84. This is mainly due to the fact
  85. that data today remains a by-product
  86. of activity as opposed to a core asset that is valued.
  87. To make this change across the industry,
  88. new C-level roles are emerging,
  89. such as Chief Data Officer.
  90. Regulators are also concerned by this,
  91. and you saw that in module 4
  92. when we were covering the topic of smart regulation
  93. and what it means from a human capital perspective
  94. for the regulators.
  95. Now, in order to enhance the value of data,
  96. whether internal or external,
  97. going forward, financial institution will need
  98. to consider the three Vs of data.
  99. First, volume.
  100. The combination of behaviour tracking
  101. and Internet of Things is increasing
  102. the amount of data points available.
  103. Second, velocity.
  104. The speed at which data is created requires
  105. the need for real time analytics,
  106. as it challenges the current storage capacity.
  107. Third, variety.
  108. The ability to handle,
  109. understand structured and unstructured data
  110. from internal or external sources.
  111. As this transformation process occurs,
  112. FinTech startups are increasingly providing
  113. the necessary analytical tools to process
  114. and analyse data held by financial institutions.
  115. For now, these remain narrow use cases
  116. and include identification of credit risk
  117. in loan portfolio, transaction of quarterly reports
  118. into investment advice,
  119. or performing complete audit risk review.
  120. Whilst these have always been performed by banks,
  121. today the combination of data availability
  122. and analytical tools make it possible
  123. to do these in seconds instead of hours,
  124. and this is done without a trade-off on accuracy levels.
  125. Hopefully, this will provide banks a capacity
  126. to restore profitability levels,
  127. whilst for consumer,
  128. create a new era of invisible finance,
  129. one that support your lifestyle instead
  130. of creating friction as you’re trying
  131. to achieve your goals.
  132.  

Industry Showcase: Application of Data Analytics in Finance (vPhrase)

  1. Hello everyone, I’m Neerav Parekh.
  2. I’m the founder of a company called vPhrase.
  3. We’re from India.
  4. I’ll tell you about how AI
  5. is being used in the data analytics space
  6. for FinTech companies.
  7. So, we created a product that a lot of institutions use
  8. for improving their report.
  9. Let me give you a few examples.
  10. Motilal Oswal which is one of the largest brokerages,
  11. they use a platform for creating portfolio analysis
  12. for their investors,
  13. so we examine 500,000 portfolios every month
  14. and create statements for the investors.
  15. The whole idea is to give them
  16. the key insights in the portfolio, using language.
  17. So, AI is being used to generate language from data
  18. to explain all the insights to the leader.
  19. So, that is one example.
  20. Another large very large private bank in India
  21. is using our platform to create personalised reports
  22. for the branch managers,
  23. so the whole performance of the branch,
  24. all the data is taken in,
  25. is analysed and language is written
  26. to explain to the branch manager
  27. how exactly is this branch doing.
  28. So, a few bullet points we’ll tell him,
  29. Ok if your branch’s achieving its targets,
  30. which products that are doing well,
  31. which products that are not doing well and so on.
  32. Then there’s another large investment bank
  33. which is using our platform
  34. for risk analysis of stocks and mutual funds.
  35. So, they study the performance of mutual funds,
  36. they study the performance of companies
  37. and we use that data to create risk analysis reports
  38. for them on those stocks and mutual funds.
  39. So, as you’ll see,
  40. we’re using AI for data analysis
  41. and then creating language,
  42. natural language generation,
  43. so that people can understand the data better.
  44. Enterprises can make their reports
  45. easier to understand for their people.
  46. Thank you.

Industry Showcase (NEW): How Startups are Transforming the Asset Management Landscape in Hong Kong? (MioTech)

  1. Hi, my name’s Jason Tu
  2. and I am the CEO and Co-Founder of MioTech.
  3. We are a startup for financial institutions
  4. to better manage and draw insights into their data.
  5. Our company is two years old
  6. and we began this journey in Hong Kong,
  7. the largest financial hub here in Asia.
  8. Just how important is this market?
  9. In 2018, the Hong Kong Stock Exchange
  10. actually snatched the crown jewels of IPO listings,
  11. raising more than US$36.6 billion for 208 companies.
  12. Hong Kong’s Asset and Wealth Management Business
  13. reached more than US$3 trillion Asset
  14. on the management
  15. based on SFC’s annual survey in 2017,
  16. effectively making Hong Kong the No. 1 leader in Asia.
  17. This is just to give you a gist of how important
  18. financial services is to Hong Kong, and vice versa.
  19. Now let’s take a look at the ecosystem
  20. surrounding Asset Management.
  21. For every trade, from the back office perspective,
  22. it will go through custodians, exchange,
  23. clearing and settlement agencies,
  24. and possibly, fund administrators
  25. until accurate information is reflected
  26. in Asset Manager’s hands.
  27. From a front office perspective,
  28. for every investment opportunity,
  29. the asset manager
  30. needs to gather conventional data sets
  31. such as prices, volumes, as well as,
  32. unconventional data sets
  33. such as news, and alternative data.
  34. Asset Managers are at the top of the pyramid,
  35. they digest all these information
  36. in order to deliver return
  37. and avoid risks on their financial assets.
  38. So, where are the opportunities in this industry
  39. for a startup
  40. and particularly in Hong Kong?
  41. This goes back to Hong Kong’s unique role
  42. as the gateway to both China
  43. and broadly speaking, Asia.
  44. From the data and technology perspective,
  45. the market data
  46. and market intelligence industry in Hong Kong
  47. is still dominated by
  48. western market data providers,
  49. with a lack of coverage
  50. and understanding of the local markets.
  51. These large multi-national players
  52. also have decades of company history
  53. and very large complex corporate structure
  54. that don’t allow them to adopt new technologies
  55. as fast as startups can.
  56. The lack of data coverage on Asia
  57. and slow innovation on the technology side
  58. creates opportunities for disrupters to fill the gap.
  59. From a product and user experience perspective,
  60. the entire financial software industry
  61. is lagged behind the consumer app
  62. and Software-As-A-Service industry.
  63. From Google, Facebook to Slack, Zendesk,
  64. user centric design and browser based enterprise
  65. applications have completely changed
  66. both the consumer and enterprise world.
  67. There is a huge opportunity to create a product
  68. that adapts to the modern user
  69. as compared to having clients to adapt
  70. to decades-old financial software
  71. that was created
  72. before most of the younger traders were born.
  73. The question is how?
  74. There are a number of tips
  75. I can offer to entrepreneurs
  76. aiming to change the asset management industry.
  77. First of all,
  78. Hong Kong has unparalleled advantage
  79. in its access to the Greater China Region
  80. and the rest of Asia.
  81. However, entrepreneurs should keep in mind
  82. that understanding Hong Kong isn’t enough,
  83. but rather, you should be eager
  84. to learn the needs in Greater China and Asia,
  85. the places where Hong Kong has access to,
  86. that’s where your users are.
  87. Second of all,
  88. the asset management industry
  89. already has a very complex business structure
  90. compared to other industries.
  91. We have to be very careful
  92. about business model innovations,
  93. and not to introduce more complexity.
  94. Technology innovation, on the other hand,
  95. has much more room to improve efficiency
  96. and reduce complexity.
  97. Last but not the least,
  98. as I’m speaking here right now,
  99. talent is the single most important factor
  100. to innovation.
  101. I’m hopeful that Hong Kong will attract
  102. more technology talents to mix up
  103. with these finance professionals
  104. to disrupt and to make a difference together.

Industry Showcase (NEW): Digitisation of Financial Services – A DBS Approach (Parts 1) (DBS)

  1. Disclaimer: Views expressed are personal and do not reflect the views or thoughts of any organization the speaker may be affiliated/associated with.
  2. Ladies and gentlemen, just to set the scene
  3. and introduce myself.
  4. I’m Mark Li
  5. and I run a digital program office
  6. for transaction banking in DBS.
  7. So I’m really happy to be invited
  8. by the University of Hong Kong
  9. to talk about the digitalisation of financial services,
  10. So my first question to all of you is:
  11. Why does a traditional bank need to go digital?
  12. Why can’t traditional banks just stay
  13. within their comfort zone
  14. and run their business as usual?
  15. The reason is pretty simple.
  16. Just to stay competitive.
  17. In recent years, we have been
  18. experiencing gigantic changes
  19. brought by internet companies
  20. such as Tencent, Alibaba, Amazon, etc.
  21. They are opening up
  22. their own affiliated financial subsidiaries
  23. to engage in businesses that traditionally banks
  24. are doing for a really long time ago.
  25. We also see an increasing number of
  26. startup companies that are focusing on
  27. a single banking function and customer segment.
  28. Within this, you can always find
  29. that they may do a better job
  30. than banks in terms of customer experience
  31. and product offering.
  32. Cost is another reason.
  33. By putting up brick-and-mortar data centre in the clouds,
  34. banks will be able to enjoy a lower cost
  35. while expanding the banking business.
  36. I’m not here to promote any banks
  37. or any digital aspiration
  38. but there are a few things I wish I could share

Industry Showcase (NEW): Digitisation of Financial Services – A DBS Approach (Part 2) (DBS)

  1. Disclaimer: Views expressed are personal and do not reflect the views or thoughts of any organization the speaker may be affiliated/associated with.
  2. Another critical part of digital transformation
  3. is to understand the legacy issues we are facing
  4. we not only for DBS but also as a bank as a whole
  5. and we have to improve with a digital mindset.

  7. who really comes up without physical cash
  8. in Hong Kong and Asia?
  9. Let’s talk about branch needs for banks,
  10. whether you agree or not, there’s still a strong demand
  11. for physical branches in Hong Kong and in Asia
  12. what kind of services
  13. really needs to be done in branches?
  14. Or can we move in digital,
  15. even in terms of transaction and transparency?
  16. Why can our fellow friends in the FinTech market
  17. such as TNG or Tap & Go
  18. offer instant overseas remittance at very low costs,
  19. but many traditional banks cannot?
  20. Account and mortgage opening at least 4 to 8 weeks

5.4: European Big-Bang: PSD2 / GDPR / Mifid2

  1. We’ve seen across this course
  2. how technology has transformed finance,
  3. and in particular, we’ve seen how finance
  4. has transformed finance in China,
  5. perhaps more so than any other place in the world,
  6. how firms such as Ali Baba and Tencent
  7. and their financial subsidiaries like
  8. Ant Financial and WeBank
  9. have led a digital transformation,
  10. driving finance in China
  11. from a cash-based, bank-based system
  12. to one which is based largely on data and technology,
  13. digital forms of payment,
  14. digital forms of lending,
  15. digital forms of investment,
  16. all based upon techniques of automation,
  17. big data, and increasingly, artificial intelligence
  18. to build a framework
  19. for an entirely new 21st-century form of finance.
  20. We’ve also seen in the context of India
  21. how India has been seeking
  22. to build a digital infrastructure
  23. to support the transformation of its financial system.
  24. Likewise, in the European Union,
  25. a series of changes coming into effect in 2018
  26. are fundamentally transforming the relationship
  27. between finance and data, setting the stage
  28. for a new European financial system
  29. not based on monetization of finance,
  30. but rather on monetization of data.
  31. And these three pieces go by
  32. the short names of GDPR, PSD2, and MiFID II,
  33. three rather uninteresting strings of letters,
  34. but fundamentally transforming the way
  35. that finance and data relate in the context of the EU.
  36. First, GDPR.
  37. GDPR is the General Data Protection Regulation,
  38. and this has evolved out of a long process of building
  39. a legal framework for privacy
  40. and data protection in the EU.
  41. GDPR is fundamentally about giving individuals power
  42. and control over their individual personal data.
  43. It allows individuals to direct
  44. holders of their personal data
  45. to delete it, forget it, transfer it,
  46. and in any way that it is the data of the individual,
  47. rather than the data of the company, of the firm.
  48. And this is fundamentally transforming the way
  49. that businesses think about their relationship
  50. to their customer and their data.
  51. This in particular is a big challenge for tech firms
  52. like Google or Facebook or Ali Baba or Tencent.
  53. Another second area is what is called
  54. the Payment Services Directive 2.
  55. PSD2 sets new rules to allow open entry of a wide range
  56. of new entrants to the payments industry.
  57. But beyond allowing
  58. an increasing range of new entrants
  59. and encouraging digitization
  60. and transparency of payments,
  61. PSD2 also creates a series
  62. of requirements for open banking.
  63. In other words, for a customer,
  64. if a bank has your data,
  65. information about your financial history,
  66. your financial transactions, your financial accounts,
  67. PSD2 requires those banks,
  68. if you so direct, to allow
  69. third parties access to your data, to your accounts.
  70. And this fundamentally sets
  71. a framework for banks’ business
  72. being transformed as customers’ data and access
  73. to their accounts is no longer under the control
  74. of banks and other financial institutions,
  75. but it is open to new entrants,
  76. new startups, new tech firms,
  77. all of which are gaining access to massive
  78. amounts of data under the control
  79. of the individual consumer,
  80. rather than the financial institution.
  81. And finally, MiFID II.
  82. MiFID II is
  83. the Markets in Financial Instruments Directive II.
  84. MiFID II is an initiative that has evolved
  85. out of the 2008 global financial crisis.
  86. It is fundamentally about creating greater transparency
  87. in the formal financial markets,
  88. markets for bonds, shares, derivatives, and the like,
  89. whether those are traded on exchange or off exchange.
  90. And amongst many other things,
  91. it requires the reporting of all transactions
  92. relating to EU financial instruments,
  93. like bonds or shares or derivatives, whether or not
  94. those transactions take place on or off exchange.
  95. In terms of total size, it has grown to be
  96. more than 1.7 million paragraphs in length,
  97. 7,000 printed pages of regulations,
  98. which have required financial institutions to spend
  99. literally tens of billions of dollars in
  100. building new systems
  101. to meet its reporting requirements.
  102. Each of these systems,
  103. PSD2, GDPR, and MiFID II,
  104. were all designed for specific reasons.
  105. But no one thought about what would happen
  106. when you combined them together.
  107. And this will be a big bang
  108. transforming over the coming decade
  109. the European financial services industry,
  110. bringing together data and finance in a way
  111. that has never been seen before in the context
  112. of the EU single financial market.

Industry Showcase: PSD2: Open Banking API for Startups (Gini)

  1. For decades, FinTech startups
  2. have been developing useful apps
  3. and products to supplement normal banking products,
  4. and also give consumers more control and insight
  5. into their own finances.
  6. However, banks have never been keen on sharing data,
  7. so FinTech companies have had
  8. to spend considerable resources
  9. to develop workarounds to get the data.
  10. One of the main ways they have done this
  11. is through screen scraping.
  12. Screen scraping is the act of developing a programme
  13. to crawl through a website
  14. and copy the information into another database.
  15. This is commonly found in search engines like Google,
  16. which send crawlers through the internet
  17. to develop their search engine database.
  18. This year the European Union
  19. implemented Revised Directive
  20. on Payment Services aka PSD2
  21. which included regulations to promote the development
  22. and use of online and mobile payments
  23. such as open banking.
  24. Other countries are following suit,
  25. and in Asia, both Hong Kong and Singapore,
  26. as well as other countries
  27. are exploring opening banking APIs.
  28. Opening banking APIs would facilitate
  29. the growth of FinTech companies
  30. and also give consumers greater choice
  31. and opportunities in managing their financial lives.

Industry Showcase: Methods of Data Protection: GDPR Compliance and Personal Privacy (Exate Technology)

  1. Hello, my name is Jonathan Naismith
  2. and I am the Business Manager for Exate Technology,
  3. a RegTech firm specialising in
  4. data protection and data privacy,
  5. enabling regulatory compliance
  6. with the EU General Data Protection Regulation
  7. as well as Cross-Border Data Transfers.
  8. The recent data breaches at firms
  9. such as Equifax, Uber and Facebook’s breach of privacy
  10. have led many to question the ability of firms
  11. to protect sensitive client data.
  12. Sadly, these recent breaches
  13. have become more of a trend than a phenomenon
  14. as they are increasingly common.
  15. So, why is this the case?
  16. To date, data protection has typically been done
  17. on an application by application level
  18. and each application is protected by an IT developer.
  19. The common problems with this
  20. are your IT developers are often contractors,
  21. and thus probably work somewhere else now
  22. and they view the task of protecting those applications
  23. as boring and time consuming.
  24. With new computing viruses being created daily
  25. and the introduction of
  26. quantum computing nearly upon us,
  27. traditional data protection has become outdated
  28. and needs to be addressed now.
  29. At Exate, we encrypt or tokenise the data
  30. on an attribute by attribute level at rest,
  31. in transit, and in memory as required under GDPR.
  32. This then allows an organisation
  33. to separate application security from data security.
  34. The question now becomes,
  35. “How does one view the data if it is always encrypted?”
  36. Exate ensure that data is seen strictly
  37. on a need to know basis for
  38. that specific individual, team, department or firm
  39. to fulfil their role.
  40. We achieve this by providing
  41. your data with a virtual visa.
  42. This is done by wrapping metadata or rules
  43. around each individual data attribute
  44. and allowing those attributes
  45. to flow throughout the organisation with those rules.
  46. Exate then just sit outside a firm’s applications
  47. and acts as an automated border control for your data.
  48. To explain, imagine you have flown into an airport.
  49. If you are a local resident,
  50. you go down the fast track,
  51. that is your public data, it doesn’t need to be protected
  52. it just flows throughout your organisation.
  53. Now your tourists,
  54. they have to go to the man in the booth
  55. who checks their passports and looks at their visa
  56. before deciding whether or not
  57. they can enter the country,
  58. we do the exact same thing for your data.
  59. So just before the data enters an application
  60. we look at the rules around each attribute
  61. and match that back to the individual
  62. who’s trying to access the data.
  63. Now one of two things can happen.
  64. One, the individual passed the rule check
  65. in which the data will decrypt, or two,
  66. if the individual fails even one of the rule checks,
  67. the data will remain encrypted
  68. and their access blocked.
  69. Exate then provides clients
  70. with immutable forensic proof
  71. of who accessed, or attempted to access what data.
  72. In addition, Exate are able to provide customizable
  73. user reports on your data,
  74. in short, data about your data.
  75. Lastly, Exate Technology has
  76. no access to your clients’ data,
  77. their decryption keys or their applications
  78. and requires no code changes to integrate
  79. with any web-based application.
  80. It is important to note
  81. the same technology can be used for
  82. Cross-Border Data Transfers.
  83. I hope this has been of interest to you
  84. and I would like to thank Hong Kong University
  85. for allowing Exate to share this with you.

5.5 Digital Identity

  1. This module will be around digital identity.
  2. The reason why digital identity matters is because
  3. it will be one of the most impactful technology
  4. in the next five years
  5. for you as a viewer.
  6. Identity can be defined
  7. in four different types of categories.
  8. Physical identity will be
  9. elements such as your fingerprint,
  10. your iris, or your DNA.
  11. Legal identity will be, for example, your passport,
  12. your Hong Kong ID, or your driving licence.
  13. Physical and Legal identities
  14. is what we call static identity.
  15. However, you also have something called
  16. dynamic identity.
  17. For example, your electronic identity
  18. is your social media.
  19. Facebook, Twitter, Weibo and WeChat
  20. are example of social media
  21. that are covering your identity.
  22. And finally, behavioral identity.
  23. The way you talk, the way you walk,
  24. the way you type a message
  25. is very unique to you.
  26. And therefore, electronic and behavioral identity
  27. are typically called dynamic identities.
  28. Now let’s bring that in context of finance.
  29. When you’re a financial institution,
  30. you will typically get to know your customer
  31. by the time they going to register,
  32. by giving their passport, or their legal identity,
  33. and electronically when they
  34. will be transacting with you.
  35. What you buy,
  36. the insurance products that you take,
  37. or the mortgages that you actually get from the bank.
  38. Apart from these touch points,
  39. the bank doesn’t necessarily know you.
  40. They don’t really have an access
  41. to your behavioral identity,
  42. they don’t really have access
  43. to your electronic identity.
  44. Therefore, the banks,
  45. unless you often are transacting with the bank,
  46. and if you have a bank account in the first place,
  47. will typically be unable
  48. to have a full picture about yourself.
  49. And this is why for example,
  50. in the West we call thin credit files.
  51. These are people that,
  52. whilst banks would often have financial interaction,
  53. and therefore are not always recorded
  54. as a customer of that bank.
  55. Now in that context, identity actually has few flaws.
  56. In the West, identity is more and more unsafe.
  57. Look at the recent data hack
  58. that has been happening for example in Equifax.
  59. Equifax was holding financial information,
  60. and now one in two Americans
  61. has information exposed to the public.
  62. However, if for example,
  63. in the context of developing markets,
  64. people don’t necessarily have passports.
  65. Because 1.2 billion people in developing countries
  66. do not have a form of formal identity,
  67. they cannot typically enter financial service industry,
  68. and therefore, we need to totally rethink
  69. the way we’re going to be identifying people
  70. to authenticate a transaction.
  71. So let’s take some of these examples.
  72. First of all, we’ll go in India.
  73. India has launched a programme called Aadhaar,
  74. which is now providing 99% of the population over 18,
  75. with a 12-digit number
  76. that is essentially a representation
  77. of your biometric data and your geographical data.
  78. With your Aadhaar number,
  79. not only can you withdraw cash at an ATM,
  80. but you will now also be able to authorise
  81. a large transaction,
  82. or even do an online learning course
  83. by scanning your iris to identify yourself
  84. as a student of that course.
  85. In Nigeria, digital identity is being used
  86. for civil servants.
  87. This is very important for the government
  88. because they have realised
  89. that there is a lot of identity theft coming on.
  90. That identity theft is then leading to people
  91. either receiving two times a salary,
  92. or receiving someone else’s salary.
  93. And since implementing digital identity to civil servant,
  94. they have now saved over 75 million US dollar.
  95. Finally, Europe.
  96. In the context of Europe,
  97. you have PSD2 on one side
  98. and GDPR on the other side,
  99. which are changing the way
  100. data of consumer is being used.
  101. Whilst PSD2 is more about payment competition,
  102. and whilst GDPR is about personal data protection,
  103. the combination of both
  104. is putting the individual
  105. at the centre of the financial services industry,
  106. and empowering them with their own data.
  107. Therefore, now someone would be able to give access
  108. to their bank account to a merchant that actually
  109. directly wants to transact,
  110. bypassing the traditional card network,
  111. or the merchant acquirers.
  112. Therefore, what we see is that
  113. finance is being changed,
  114. and digital identity alongside.
  115. We need to go from a model
  116. where we trade data for convenience,
  117. to a world where we trade data for compensation.
  118. Part of that step is going to be GDPR in Europe.
  119. GDPR, while not allowing individual to
  120. monetize their data,
  121. is providing individual control on their data
  122. by notifying an individual of a breach,
  123. or by allowing an individual of doing data sharing.
  124. Finally, the next step is going to be about reforming
  125. and how ownership is done.
  126. Once we have covered the ownership part,
  127. we’ll have to consolidate our data into a single wallet.
  128. That aggregation will allow us to bring together
  129. the behavioural data, the electronic data,
  130. the legal data, and the physical data
  131. into a single wallet that we will control,
  132. and then we can either grant access to,
  133. or monetize from.
  134. This is how digital identity will impact your life,
  135. and now let’s have a guest lecture
  136. that will share slightly more details.

5.6 Change in mindset: Regulation 1.0 to 2.0 (KYC to KYD)

  1. This RecTech Module has shown you
  2. how regulation is changing.
  3. This change of regulation
  4. can be captured by the notion of KYC to KYD.
  5. Know your customer to know your data.
  6. The reason why this is changing,
  7. is that from a regulator perspective,
  8. we’re going from a world where we’re regulating people
  9. to a world where we’ll be regulating processes,
  10. automation, and algorithm.
  11. And this requires a totally different mindset
  12. but also skillsets for a regulator
  13. to perform that job when that transition has happened.
  14. One of the first things that we’ll see
  15. will be about human capital.
  16. First, regulator from a human capital perspective
  17. will bring brand new stuff in to
  18. better perform their role as regulators.
  19. And in particular I have in mind data scientists.
  20. Just like technology companies or financial institutions
  21. have gone for the last few decades,
  22. regulators will keep on
  23. increasing their team of data scientists.
  24. The second part of that is
  25. that from a mindset perspective
  26. regulators have to accept that technological neutrality
  27. is not anymore a starting point.
  28. What’s technological neutrality has been
  29. a very important notion for the regulators to prevent
  30. them from chasing the latest changes in technology,
  31. today this is not sufficient anymore.
  32. The reason why I say that
  33. is because you have learned in the first module
  34. how financial technology cycles are shorter and shorter.
  35. But that’s exactly the same thing for regulators.
  36. Think about it.
  37. From your personal perspective as a consumer,
  38. a few years ago you would use your pin code to
  39. authorise a transaction.
  40. Last year, you might have used your fingerprint
  41. and tomorrow you might be using your iris.
  42. From a regulator perspective
  43. if every single time that technology are changing
  44. and evolving and you go to them and you say
  45. please can you find me an authorised technology,
  46. the regulators will be bombarded
  47. and clogged with the amount of requests coming in.
  48. And therefore what they said is
  49. I do not care about the technology,
  50. tell me about what you’re trying to achieve
  51. from a process perspective
  52. and I will make sure that process is complied with.
  53. The problem with that approach today
  54. is that more and more of the decision making
  55. is automated as well as the origination of products,
  56. and therefore if regulators don’t understand
  57. how algorithms are operating at the code level
  58. and the type of data that they are using,
  59. it will be very very difficult
  60. for them to perform their role
  61. which is about consumer protection,
  62. financial stability,
  63. and even competition.
  64. Regulation is therefore changing.
  65. We’re going to go from
  66. regulation 1.0 to regulation 2.0.
  67. And to do that transition from
  68. a know your customer world
  69. to a know your data paradigm,
  70. a few changes will have to happen.
  71. We have put together seven key points on
  72. how regulation is done today and
  73. how it will be done tomorrow.
  74. First point, consumer protection.
  75. What is important for regulators
  76. to ensure that the money of people
  77. doesn’t get lost or misappropriated?
  78. I think that this quote has highlighted to you
  79. the increasing value of data
  80. either from a monetization perspective
  81. or for a financial decision perspective.
  82. And therefore, data protection and data privacy will
  83. be equally important as monetary protection of
  84. your individual deposits.
  85. The second point is prudential regulation
  86. which requires firms to put control in place as well as
  87. capital in order to mitigate for risk.
  88. However, going forward,
  89. it’s more going to be about algorithm compliance.
  90. Where a regulator will have to do due diligence on
  91. a system and an algorithm before you’re allowed
  92. to go to market instead of simply asking for capital
  93. to be put against risk.
  94. The third point is financial stability.
  95. While financial stability is very important,
  96. it fails to encapsulate the notion that
  97. financial systems are very much dynamic networks
  98. of many individuals and companies
  99. operating at the same time
  100. that can impact each other.
  101. And therefore the capacity
  102. of regulating a financial network as opposed to
  103. promoting financial stability will be more valuable.
  104. And this is very much aligned with the vision
  105. and the quote of Andy Haldane when he talks about
  106. that Star Trek chair to supervise markets.
  107. The fourth point is about preventing bad behaviour.
  108. Whilst conduct risk has been a major focus point
  109. following the financial crisis,
  110. and this has been a positive development,
  111. firms are still able to pay their way out
  112. of reprehensible actions.
  113. Therefore, regulators would set in place a system where
  114. they can promote good behaviours in order to reinforce
  115. the quality of financial networks.
  116. The fifth point is about reactiveness.
  117. Regulators especially following the crisis,
  118. have been very reactive to the shock that
  119. the great financial crisis has brought to them.
  120. However, what we have seen in this course is that
  121. technology keeps on changing faster and faster.
  122. And therefore the capacity of being reactive to
  123. technological change will lessen in terms of value.
  124. Therefore, regulators need to be forward looking
  125. and think about how finances
  126. will be transformed tomorrow
  127. so that they can start to prepare today.
  128. The sixth point is about reporting.
  129. Push compliance is when financial institutions
  130. are sending reports to the regulators
  131. about specific questions
  132. that has been asked about them.
  133. For example, for the very first time the Bank of England
  134. has been asking financial institution to include
  135. the impact of FinTech in their business.
  136. What that means is that now financial institutions
  137. are thinking twice about how financial technology is
  138. going to be impacting their business
  139. from a risk perspective.
  140. Now, that simple question has therefore
  141. changed the behaviour of the firm that
  142. you are trying to supervise.
  143. Therefore a better way would be
  144. the notion of API compliance
  145. or the capacity of regulators to pull the information
  146. directly from the financial institution
  147. without asking them a question and
  148. therefore directly analyse the impact of external shocks
  149. such as FinTech on their businesses.
  150. Therefore, pull compliance will allow regulators
  151. to supervise firms without
  152. changing the behaviour of these institutions.
  153. The seventh point is about the barriers to entry.
  154. Today, financial markets are controlled
  155. from the supply of institution
  156. by the numbers of the licences issued by regulators.
  157. You can only call yourself a bank
  158. if you receive a banking licence.
  159. However, tomorrow the next barrier to entry will be
  160. about the quality of the algorithm that is held
  161. either by the financial institution or
  162. by the tech company.
  163. The reason why I say that is that finance
  164. on the back of the amount of data currently gathered
  165. is going to be incredibly personalised just to you.
  166. And therefore that level of customization and
  167. individualization will represent
  168. an experience barrier to entry which is
  169. driven by algorithm as opposed to
  170. simply the ownership of a regulatory licence.

5.7 AI and Governance

  1. In a previous module,
  2. I have briefly described artificial intelligence or AI
  3. as part of the ABCD technologies driving FinTech.
  4. As institutions increasingly explore
  5. and implement more AI and machine learning
  6. into their processes and offerings
  7. to customers and clients,
  8. there’s an increasing concern
  9. from policy makers, researchers and regulators
  10. about the responsibility and governance frameworks
  11. that should be in place.
  12. AI researchers have coined a useful acronym
  13. to better understand the core concerns.
  14. They call it AI F.A.T.
  15. Fairness, Accountability and Transparency.
  16. Machine learning involves algorithmic models
  17. being trained using datasets.
  18. However, the saying goes,
  19. garbage in, garbage out.
  20. Unconscious bias can arise,
  21. including egregious examples such as image data sets
  22. not facially recognising African Americans,
  23. or categorising them as gorillas.
  24. Malicious and mischievous actors
  25. can also influence machine learning.
  26. This was painfully demonstrated
  27. when Microsoft’s Tay chatbot
  28. devolved into racist and sexist rants
  29. as a result of learning from
  30. deliberately offensive behaviour of Twitter trolls.
  31. Furthermore, as ever more data sources become
  32. available to track both individual online behaviour
  33. as well as offline activities
  34. through connected devices
  35. such as wearables and smart homes,
  36. machine learning is being used
  37. to convert these behavioural data
  38. into individual profiles for predictive analytics.
  39. Yet, how fair is it if machines increasingly make
  40. financial access and pricing decisions
  41. based on individual predispositions
  42. rather than actions?
  43. Machine learning is being used for important processes
  44. such as credit ratings, search engines, bank loans,
  45. university applications, and health insurance,
  46. and becomes even more crucial
  47. when applied to RegTech
  48. which could lead to civil liability
  49. and loss of one’s liberty.
  50. For example, was there unconscious racial bias
  51. in the COMPAS algorithm for criminal sentencing
  52. risk assessments in the United States?
  53. Do Facebook’s algorithms have a confirmation bias
  54. that allows fake news to be targeted,
  55. and in turn did their postings
  56. impact the outcome of the Presidential elections?
  57. The need to combat negative bias outcomes,
  58. unconscious or otherwise,
  59. remains an important source of concern.

New Challenges of AI and Machine Learning

  1. Artificial intelligence is only so strong
  2. and so good as the data that you serve it.
  3. And actually when you think from
  4. a regulation perspective,
  5. it’s not so much the regulation
  6. of one algorithm which is going to matter.
  7. It’s more going to be about the quality of algorithm,
  8. and the natural tendency of
  9. algorithm to create a oligopoly.
  10. Data is fueling algorithm,
  11. and algorithm only grow by having more data.
  12. And therefore, technology companies
  13. are doing whatever it takes them to be able
  14. to capture all the data they can on their consumer
  15. to have a dominant market position.
  16. I think that data is the new oil as a sentence
  17. is reflecting what you had in the U.S. when you had oil.
  18. The antitrust movement in the U.S. that was trying
  19. to break away the big U.S. cartels around oil
  20. is what’s going to be happening in the data space.
  21. Because data is naturally driving network effects,
  22. and because network effects are themselves
  23. very naturally oligopolistic,
  24. the regulators will need to find a way of
  25. breaking away the dominance
  26. of large tech firms that are controlling most of the data.
  27. Your social data is most likely controlled by Facebook.
  28. Your e-commerce data is more
  29. likely controlled by Amazon.
  30. Your professional data
  31. is more likely controlled by LinkedIn.
  32. These people have all the information about you.
  33. And the only way to break away that stranglehold
  34. on your personal data is by allowing you as an individual
  35. to share your data to other people.
  36. So that you can then fuel the algorithm
  37. of a competitor with the data
  38. that you have been building
  39. about yourself for the last 10 years.
  40. This is where data regulation will go in the future.
  41. Data regulation will focus on individual data
  42. as opposed to the algorithm adjustment
  43. that will be required to be made
  44. by technology companies
  45. as requested by the regulators.

5.9 Data, Metadata and Differential Privacy

  1. I’m now going to be talking about the difference
  2. between data and metadata
  3. and how this is being expressed
  4. with the concept of differential privacy.
  5. If you think about what financial technology is,
  6. financial technology is about the digitization of money
  7. but if you introduce the notion of TechFin,
  8. TechFin is about the monetization of data.
  9. Therefore the point is going to be
  10. how are we are going to differentiate our data?
  11. Currently, the way this has been done
  12. is that technology companies
  13. have tried as much as possible not to have
  14. personal identifiable information about you.
  15. The reason why they try to avoid this
  16. is because as soon as they have PII data,
  17. they start to be subject
  18. to more stringent regulatory supervision
  19. and therefore increase costs for their business models.
  20. And so far this has worked.
  21. The reason is because personal information
  22. such as your age but also the content
  23. of a discussion is something that computer
  24. had a very hard time to understand and make sense of.
  25. Only very recently do computer have
  26. the sufficient computing power
  27. as well as the understanding of the human language
  28. to find out content in a conversation.
  29. Instead, what computers have done
  30. is to look at metadata.
  31. Metadata is contextual data
  32. which is around a content.
  33. For example, the metadata
  34. around my discussion with you
  35. would be how long it took me
  36. to actually record that video,
  37. the content and the numbers of people
  38. which are seeing that video, etc.
  39. The contextual data when it’s
  40. sufficiently cross referenced
  41. with a lot of different dataset
  42. which typically tech companies have
  43. will allow you to infer content.
  44. Let me take another example.
  45. If I am on LinkedIn and suddenly I start
  46. changing my behaviour, contacting more people
  47. and asking for meeting requests,
  48. this is going to flag LinkedIn
  49. that my behaviour is changing
  50. and therefore I may be looking for another job.
  51. And yet at no point did LinkedIn
  52. actually start looking into my messages
  53. saying I am looking for another job opportunity.
  54. They were able to infer that content using metadata.
  55. Data versus metadata is
  56. a very important thing
  57. because financial institution
  58. have very good at protecting your data
  59. but technology companies
  60. have been very good at monetizing your metadata.
  61. Therefore we need to start to see
  62. how we are going to create a framework
  63. to regulate that.
  64. And here you have an example of
  65. what technology companies such as Apple are doing.
  66. There’s a notion called differential privacy.
  67. Differential privacy is about giving
  68. the information to the person
  69. without revealing the whole information set.
  70. There’re many use cases that
  71. you can relate to.
  72. In the context of finance,
  73. typically for financial institution to on board
  74. a financial citizen will mean
  75. more stringent regulatory and compliance requirements
  76. because of that American citizen
  77. now being on boarded.
  78. And therefore sometimes it’s important
  79. for a financial institution
  80. to know that a customer is not an American citizen.
  81. Differential privacy allows for that.
  82. It’s about telling someone who you’re not
  83. without revealing the whole information.
  84. Therefore, as long as a financial institution knows
  85. I am not an American,
  86. I may be any other nationalities
  87. and that’s good enough.
  88. The other way of looking at it is for example,
  89. accessing certain website.
  90. Certain website will require you to confirm
  91. that you’re not under 18
  92. but they do not care whether you are 21 or 38.
  93. The fact that you are 21 or 38
  94. actually starts to be a hint
  95. towards personal identifiable information
  96. but that can be linked back to you
  97. and therefore differential privacy
  98. gets away with that notion by not sharing
  99. the full picture by telling who you are not.
  100. And as long as the rule set is strong in place
  101. with the financial institution,
  102. they’ll be able to leverage on that information.
  103. This is what differential privacy is bringing
  104. and differential privacy
  105. is typically being led by companies
  106. which are not here to make money from your data.
  107. Apple revenue is at 80% driven by hardware sale.
  108. Therefore, for them to not release
  109. and monetize your data is not actually a compromise.
  110. It’s something that makes economic sense.
  111. But a company like Google
  112. and Amazon or Facebook
  113. which have much more difficulty
  114. of actually enforcing differential privacy
  115. because their business is about sharing
  116. the whole complete set of information.
  117. Therefore going forward
  118. you will have a split.
  119. The companies which are not monetizing your data
  120. will support differential privacy
  121. versus the companies which are monetizing your data
  122. will typically look at data sovereignty
  123. as a governance model for their customer.

5.10 Data is the New Oil: Risk of Breach

  1. Welcome back to the HKU FinTech course.
  2. This module is going to be about data.
  3. Recently you may have heard about the sentence
  4. data is the new oil.
  5. These simple words have two deep impacts.
  6. On the first side, it means that data
  7. is going to be one of the most valuable commodity
  8. of the 21st century.
  9. On the second side, it also means that regulators
  10. are going to have to focus on how data
  11. is being consumed, extracted and monetized
  12. by individuals and companies.
  13. Now, data is not something new
  14. and data has been around for the last few decades
  15. and whilst two thousand represented
  16. rising massive tech companies
  17. which we still see today
  18. such as the GAFAs,
  19. Google, Amazon, Facebook and Apple,
  20. those tech companies have approached data
  21. in a total different way.
  22. For example, you may have heard about the expression,
  23. if it’s free, you’re the product.
  24. And that sentence really means one thing.
  25. Is that the personal data that you generate
  26. is more valuable to the company
  27. and this is why they’re giving you a free product.
  28. Tech companies have understood that
  29. and because data is core to their business,
  30. they typically have structured data,
  31. data that is searchable, indexable
  32. and that can create value more easily.
  33. But that’s not the case for everyone.
  34. Financial institution, for them
  35. data was a by-product of their business.
  36. Financial institution make money
  37. by putting a lender and a borrower together
  38. by investing your money into the stock market
  39. but they don’t necessarily make money by your data
  40. and therefore the data you provide them,
  41. whether it’s your passport
  42. or whether it’s transactional data
  43. is very much a by-product of their own business
  44. and therefore that data is so far unstructured.
  45. The difference between
  46. unstructured data and structured data
  47. is that structured data is searchable, indexable
  48. and can be more easily monetized
  49. for financial institution or tech companies.
  50. Now, data is being used in different ways in finance.
  51. For example, it can be used for decision making,
  52. new business discovery,
  53. enhancement of productivity
  54. and regulatory compliance.
  55. Let’s take an example for each of these.
  56. Startups are typically improving their product
  57. at a rapid rate because they use the data
  58. on their product and their consumer
  59. to keep on improving the quality of services
  60. they keep delivering to you.
  61. The second one is banks.
  62. Banks are using data
  63. to do better decision making.
  64. For example credit scoring.
  65. Credit score will impact
  66. whether a bank will originate a loan to you
  67. and if so, at what interest rate.
  68. Regulators and specifically securities regulators
  69. have been using data for a long time
  70. to monitor market
  71. and check if there’s no insider trading happening.
  72. For example, Alibaba has understood
  73. that there’s a correlation
  74. between people wearing skinny jeans
  75. and breaking phones.
  76. The reason is because of the lack of pockets.
  77. And therefore Alibaba is gradually starting
  78. to sell insurance product for phone coverage
  79. to the people buying skinny jeans
  80. on their e-commerce platform.
  81. In other words, what you have is that data
  82. has many opportunities
  83. and data will be allowing the rise of invisible banking.
  84. For the last 100 years,
  85. banks have changed the way they were operating.
  86. 70 years ago the notion of community bank
  87. where you used to know your banker
  88. and have customised service just for you happened
  89. but it was inefficient.
  90. And then the bank consolidation happened
  91. and now we have universal banks
  92. where you’re only one of a million of a customer
  93. across the globe
  94. but tomorrow you will have invisible banks.
  95. Invisible bank will create individualization
  96. of financial product just for you
  97. even though you’re part of
  98. a large financial conglomerate.
  99. On the back of those opportunities
  100. you also have risks.
  101. Those risk are very much the factor
  102. that data is so valuable
  103. and the people controlling data
  104. will now control markets.
  105. And therefore regulators have to understand
  106. how this is going to be changing in the future.
  107. The regulation of data
  108. might be one of the most important part
  109. for regulators to focus on
  110. as opposed to the regulation of algorithm
  111. because we control as individual or data,
  112. the firms control the algorithm
  113. and it might be easier to control data access
  114. than data output.
  115. Finally, market reforms.
  116. The rest of this course will show you
  117. how regulators from around the world,
  118. technology companies as well as financial institution
  119. are approaching the question of data.
  120. Whilst data ownership is very much looked
  121. for the individual perspective,
  122. we’ll discover that in India,
  123. data is regarded as a public good
  124. and therefore regulated as such.
  125. The rest of the course will show you
  126. all you need to know about data,
  127. how it’s impacting finance,
  128. infrastructure and regulators around the world.

Industry Showcase: Cybersecurity Industry Update (Microsoft)

  1. Hi, my name is Jason Lau
  2. and I’m the Cybersecurity Advisor at Microsoft.
  3. Today I would like to talk to you briefly
  4. and give you an update
  5. on cybersecurity industry as a whole
  6. and also some trends in the marketplace
  7. which certainly also impact companies in FinTech.
  8. Many say 2017 was the year of ransomware
  9. and 2018-2019 are the years of
  10. compliance, regulation, and privacy.
  11. This is surely the case with new industry regulations
  12. and laws coming in to effect this year
  13. like GDPR, Singapore Cybersecurity Law,
  14. PCI DSS 3.2 and many more.
  15. “Businesses and users are going to embrace technology
  16. only if they can trust it.”
  17. This message is from Microsoft CEO,
  18. emphasises the importance of trust
  19. whenever we deal with cybersecurity matters.
  20. The world is changing.
  21. And in the past it used to be
  22. it is not a matter of IF you will get hacked,
  23. it’s a matter of WHEN.
  24. Now hackers get more advanced
  25. and organisations are rushing to keep up to date.
  26. And the new phrase in industry
  27. is that you need to assume
  28. you already have been breached.
  29. Former FBI Director James Comey
  30. best summarised this as
  31. “there are two kinds of companies,
  32. the ones who have been hacked
  33. and those who don’t know that they have been hacked.”
  34. McKenzie also acknowledges this
  35. stating that cybersecurity is a CEO issue.
  36. And the loss of productivity and growth
  37. is over $3 trillion.
  38. The average cost of data breaches is $3.5 million.
  39. And as you can see, cyber threats
  40. are a material risk to your organisation.
  41. Here, I’ll briefly go through what we call
  42. the cybersecurity known attack playbook.
  43. On average, a hacker typically sits your network
  44. for over 500 days before they are detected.
  45. The hacker will spend a significant amount of time
  46. achieving their objectives,
  47. which includes reconnaissance,
  48. and involves social engineering
  49. to learn more about your organisation
  50. and users’ behaviour.
  51. Then they decide on a strategy
  52. for their initial compromise.
  53. Once they are in, they will protect themselves
  54. from any basic and typical actions companies will do,
  55. such as rebooting machines.
  56. From there, they will start to look
  57. for high-privileged accounts.
  58. And if they cannot,
  59. they will do their best to perform credential theft
  60. with the goal of elevation of privileges.
  61. From here, they will persist in your network
  62. and move laterally
  63. and then access sensitive data.
  64. As you can see, from a typical defender perspective,
  65. if you don’t have measures,
  66. security and real-time monitoring in place throughout,
  67. you are just playing catch up with incidence response
  68. at the very end of the lifecycle.
  69. This is really often too late.
  70. Thus, on top of the minds of
  71. chief information security officers,
  72. companies have identified
  73. their threat detection and response,
  74. data protection and identity
  75. and access management
  76. are key focus areas for 2017 and 2018.
  77. Here I’d like to talk to you
  78. about the evolution of security perimeters.
  79. In the past, we would build on our on-premise network.
  80. We would build it like a castle.
  81. And it’d be very hard for hackers to breach
  82. your physical infrastructure.
  83. Then it evolved to be more of a network infrastructure.
  84. And we got smarter.
  85. We put in things like intrusion detection systems,
  86. intrusion prevention systems,
  87. smart firewalls, honeypots, etc.
  88. But now this is not enough.
  89. And we’re moving to what’s called
  90. an identify driven security perimeter.
  91. In the past, all of your files and assets
  92. would be within your physical network perimeter.
  93. Each day, hundreds of thousands of attempts
  94. will be made on your network.
  95. And it would only take one user
  96. on the link to click through.
  97. Often it is through a phishing email
  98. in someone in your company,
  99. then they will get tricked
  100. into clicking that particular link.
  101. The link happens to be malicious
  102. and they could perform one of many actions.
  103. One action could be to download and install
  104. malicious payload
  105. to gain access to your company resources.
  106. As you can see in the diagram,
  107. in the modern workplace,
  108. employees access data
  109. from work, home, cafes, hotels, airports,
  110. and the attack perimeter now extends farther
  111. and going to users wherever they go,
  112. thus, giving rise to the title
  113. identify driven security perimeter.
  114. One way to help strengthen security
  115. is through multi-factor authentication.
  116. Unfortunately, all three factors
  117. of something you have,
  118. something that you know,
  119. something that you are,
  120. have all been hacked.
  121. The trend now is to look at multi-factor authentication
  122. and add additional layer of security
  123. called risk-based conditional access,
  124. where real-time monitoring
  125. and the users’ behaviours and location
  126. can help us to assign a user and session risk score,
  127. which then allows us to take immediate
  128. real-time actions to either allow access,
  129. deny access, or request additional levels
  130. of authentication before they continue.
  131. Microsoft has been leading the way with cybersecurity
  132. and trust and fighting for users’ privacy.
  133. Microsoft has stated that cybersecurity
  134. is a No. 1 priority for the company
  135. and investing over one billion each year
  136. into cybersecurity,
  137. including most recently,
  138. the purchase of an Israeli company called Hexadite,
  139. which focuses into the auto-detection
  140. and auto-remediation of security incidents.
  141. Thank you for your time
  142. and hope the short briefing on cybersecurity
  143. was useful for you.
  144. Thank you.
