Module 6 The Future of Data-Driven Finance

Welcome to Module 6

6.1 Module 6 Introduction

  1. In the previous lessons,
  2. you have had a good overview
  3. of the history of FinTech
  4. and different aspects of it,
  5. from payments to data and identity.
  6. In this chapter,
  7. we will look at specific case studies
  8. that will illustrate the concepts you have learned before
  9. and show how FinTech
  10. is being implemented around the world.
  11. I have chosen five very different examples:
  12. an early stage FinTech startup in Europe,
  13. a late stage startup in the U.S,
  14. a bank in South East Asia,
  15. an e-commerce company in China,
  16. and an infrastructure system in India.
  17. These five examples are respectively
  18. Revolut, Credit Karma, DBS, Alibaba and Aadhaar.
  19. For each of these examples,
  20. you could easily spend days looking at
  21. their business models,
  22. technology, value proposition or growth story.
  23. I will share with you the lessons
  24. I find the most interesting,
  25. but do not hesitate to read about these companies,
  26. research similar models
  27. and further your education.
  28. Let’s now start
  29. and I hope you will enjoy the lessons.
  30. Thank you very much.

Module 6 Learning Objectives

Module 6 will bring together the various pieces of the course through a series of case studies. From this basis, it will seek to set the stage to consider future directions for FinTech.

In Module 6, learners will:

  • Integrate know-how from previous modules in case studies of specific firms and initiatives.
  • Analyse the impact of major trends in the context of traditional financial institutions, startups, TechFins, and developed and emerging markets.
  • Consider future directions for FinTech and its implications for your own future.    

6.2 Case Study 1: Revolut

  1. Welcome to this module where we’ll be discussing
  2. the case study of Revolut.
  3. Revolut is a very interesting company
  4. for many reasons.
  5. First, it is a great illustration of the process
  6. of unbundling and re-bundling of finance.
  7. Secondly, it is a great example of
  8. faster product development in finance.
  9. And last but not least,
  10. it is a FinTech startup
  11. that has real consumer adoption.
  12. Revolut links into many of the chapters
  13. that you studied previously,
  14. from the rise of the startups
  15. to mobile money,
  16. but also the evolution of payments
  17. as well as cryptocurrencies and exchanges.
  18. Revolut is a company
  19. that was created in the UK in 2015,
  20. and was founded by Nikolai Storonski,
  21. who’s a former trader at Credit Suisse,
  22. and Vlad Yatsenko,
  23. the CTO who was previously at Deutsche Bank.
  24. When Revolut started a few years ago,
  25. their value proposition was
  26. to offer a free debit card
  27. to those who were travelling,
  28. and didn’t want to pay the high fees
  29. they were charged when they were going abroad.
  30. In practical terms, what does it mean?
  31. Consumers would download an app
  32. to their mobile phones,
  33. they would create an account,
  34. and get onboarded through the phone.
  35. And a few days later,
  36. they would receive a debit card at home.
  37. This was a prepaid debit card,
  38. meaning that consumers would have to
  39. transfer money into that card,
  40. either from their bank account,
  41. or from another card.
  42. Once the money was deposited in the card,
  43. consumers could use it
  44. like a normal debit card,
  45. with the difference that
  46. any foreign transactions were free.
  47. That was their initial value proposition.
  48. Very quickly,
  49. as the number of consumers grew,
  50. Revolut also grew its product offering.
  51. In addition to the free account,
  52. they offered a Premium Account
  53. for example, that offered benefits
  54. like medical insurance.
  55. Then they offered lending,
  56. where consumers could apply for loans
  57. directly from their phones.
  58. And now also insurance,
  59. such as phone insurance
  60. or travel insurance,
  61. where the they can detect when you’re abroad
  62. through your phone
  63. and only charge for these days.
  64. Very recently,
  65. Revolut also started to offer cryptocurrencies
  66. where I as a consumer can
  67. directly buy cryptocurrencies from my app.
  68. Today, Revolut has passed 1 million users.
  69. They have raised more than $80 million,
  70. which is a very good number considering
  71. that they are quite lean,
  72. and do not need to spend that much money
  73. for customer acquisition.
  74. From starting in the UK,
  75. they are now available
  76. in the rest of Europe,
  77. and will certainly be looking at expansion
  78. perhaps in the US or in Asia.
  79. What are the takeaways
  80. that we can learn from a company such as Revolut?
  81. Revolut started on a very narrow product,
  82. from debit card for those who didn’t want to pay
  83. foreign exchange fees,
  84. and now they offer
  85. insurance, cryptocurrencies, business accounts
  86. for small businesses for example.
  87. And finally, they will also apply for a bank licence,
  88. in other words,
  89. they are also becoming a challenger bank.
  90. There are different types of
  91. innovation models in finance,
  92. and Revolut is a very good illustration
  93. of what we call the unbundling
  94. and re-bundling of finance.
  95. In other words,
  96. startups start on a niche product,
  97. the unbundling part,
  98. and end up offering
  99. a very wide range of financial products,
  100. the re-bundling part.
  101. The case study also shows
  102. the pace of consumer adoption in FinTech.
  103. If Revolut were a bank, for example,
  104. they would be the fastest growing bank in the UK.
  105. And they would also be the only bank
  106. to offer cryptocurrencies, for example.
  107. And finally,
  108. watch companies like Revolut very carefully,
  109. because of their agility
  110. and product development process.
  111. Their ability to launch new products very quickly
  112. is something which is quite different
  113. actually from traditional finance,
  114. but very similar to what we see from
  115. internet companies or tech companies.
  116. Now that you have learnt more about Revolut,
  117. why not do a small exercise?
  118. You could for example try to find similar examples
  119. of FinTech companies that are unbundling
  120. and re-bundling finance,
  121. and compare them to Revolut.
  122. Thanks a lot for following this module,
  123. and I hope you found it insightful.
  124. Thank you very much.

6.3 Case Study 2: Alibaba

  1. Welcome.
  2. In this session,
  3. we will look at the case study of Alibaba.
  4. Alibaba has already been
  5. mentioned several times in the course, for example,
  6. in the modules about mobile money,
  7. evolution of payments,
  8. and also alternative finance.
  9. I wanted to spend some time with you
  10. on Alibaba
  11. because it is a very important illustration
  12. of the changing landscape in finance,
  13. and how in certain markets,
  14. new entrants are quickly
  15. taking market share
  16. from traditional financial institutions.
  17. The financial arm of Alibaba
  18. started in 2004 with Alipay,
  19. to facilitate payments for users of Taobao.
  20. Alibaba’s Business-to-Consumers platform.
  21. If we go back in history,
  22. in 2002 eBay acquired PayPal
  23. with the objective to integrate payment
  24. into its auction platform,
  25. and facilitate transactions for its users.
  26. The creation of Alipay by Alibaba
  27. followed a very similar objective,
  28. and one of its first features
  29. were an escrow service,
  30. where the money was not paid to the seller
  31. until the goods were received.
  32. Alipay started as a payment mechanism for Taobao,
  33. but very quickly
  34. grew to offer more and more services,
  35. from payment of utilities to money transfer.
  36. In 2009, Alipay launched mobile payments,
  37. and this has become
  38. one of the most well-known successes of Alibaba.
  39. In practise,
  40. why is Alipay so different from other services?
  41. If you think of PayPal
  42. as a payment mechanism,
  43. Alipay in its first version
  44. was very similar to PayPal.
  45. If you think of Apple Pay or Samsung Pay,
  46. we could say that
  47. Alipay in 2010 was very similar
  48. to those mobile payment services
  49. where you can pay from your smart phone.
  50. But the biggest difference
  51. is that from the Alipay app,
  52. a consumer has access to all the services
  53. that a financial institution can offer,
  54. from payment to investment,
  55. from insurance to money transfer.
  56. In other words,
  57. Alibaba built a digital bank from scratch
  58. and put it directly in the smartphone
  59. of its clients.
  60. Today, the financial arm of Alibaba
  61. is Ant Financial,
  62. and although we think of Alibaba
  63. as an e-commerce company,
  64. Ant Financial is really
  65. a diversified financial services institution.
  66. It includes Alipay,
  67. which Alibaba calls a lifestyle enabler,
  68. and which I would call
  69. the ultimate bank in a phone,
  70. where consumers can make payments,
  71. buy movie tickets,
  72. invest money.
  73. And not just online,
  74. but also in physical shops.
  75. Today Alipay has 500 million clients.
  76. A second pillar of Ant Financial
  77. is Ant Fortune,
  78. the asset management arm
  79. that includes Yu’e Bao,
  80. which is today, the largest money market fund,
  81. or one of the largest money market funds in the world
  82. with more than $100 billion under management.
  83. Another activity of Ant Financial
  84. is Sesame Credit,
  85. which is a credit rating agency,
  86. and that takes a very large amount of data
  87. including social data to score individuals.
  88. In terms of numbers,
  89. Ant Financial has become
  90. one of the largest financial institutions in the world,
  91. with half a billion clients in China,
  92. but is also very significantly expanding
  93. in other countries.
  94. For example,
  95. they are an investor in Paytm,
  96. the largest mobile payment platform in India,
  97. or Ascend in Thailand
  98. and Kakao Pay in Korea.
  99. What can we learn from Alibaba?
  100. Alibaba is so massive
  101. that we could take it as an exception,
  102. but I think that it would be a mistake.
  103. There is much to learn from Alibaba
  104. in terms of innovation model,
  105. and that could be replicated
  106. in a lot of other situations.
  107. For example,
  108. what started as a mobile payment feature
  109. of an e-commerce platform has become
  110. a massive financial institution.
  111. In other words, we are seeing
  112. new entrants getting into finance,
  113. and reaching scale very quickly,
  114. thanks to their existing customer base,
  115. and a big leverage on technology.
  116. The example of Alibaba might be exceptional,
  117. but it is clearly not unique,
  118. and there will be more and more
  119. e-commerce companies and technology companies
  120. trying to offer financial products.
  121. The other takeaway
  122. is the rise of emerging markets in financial services.
  123. In some ways,
  124. the basic financial infrastructure in emerging markets
  125. is an opportunity
  126. for entrepreneurs and companies
  127. to build financial services from scratch,
  128. and to use the latest technologies,
  129. what we call leapfrogging.
  130. We are therefore likely to see
  131. very different types of FinTech development
  132. in the West
  133. and developing countries.
  134. Although Alibaba is exceptional in its scale,
  135. there are more tech companies
  136. getting into finance.
  137. Have a look,
  138. and you will see that there are more and more
  139. in a lot of different countries in the world.
  140. Thank you very much for following,
  141. and I hope you won’t forget
  142. that new entrants are coming into finance.
  143. Thank you.

6.4 Case Study 3: Aadhaar

  1. Hello and welcome to this module
  2. where we will be discussing about Aadhaar.
  3. Aadhaar is a very interesting illustration
  4. of the role of infrastructure
  5. in the development of FinTech.
  6. In other case studies,
  7. we talked about private companies
  8. such as Alibaba or Revolut
  9. but this one is really about infrastructure
  10. at the national level.
  11. Aadhaar was already mentioned in the previous chapters,
  12. in particular in the section about Digital Identity.
  13. Aadhaar was launched in India in 2009,
  14. with the objective to assign a unique ID number
  15. to all residents of India,
  16. and link it to biometric and demographic data.
  17. The reasons behind Aadhaar
  18. was both about inclusion
  19. because the birth registry system
  20. wasn’t robust enough and efficiency
  21. especially for the administration
  22. to handle requests from the public.
  23. Since the unique ID system
  24. could not start from an original document,
  25. such as a birth certificate, for example,
  26. biometrics were used to assess
  27. the identity of the person.
  28. It is done by taking the scans of the 10 fingerprints,
  29. as well as an iris scan
  30. and comparing it to the whole database,
  31. to make sure that you as a person for example
  32. is not included twice.
  33. Aadhaar is now a huge database of individuals,
  34. which includes demographic data
  35. as well as biometric data.
  36. In 2012, the Aadhaar system
  37. added a verification feature,
  38. that allowed organisations
  39. such as banks for example
  40. to enter an Aadhaar number
  41. and verify if the person was a resident.
  42. And this is where the identity infrastructure
  43. links into finance,
  44. because in the onboarding process of new clients,
  45. the step of Know Your Customer,
  46. what we call KYC,
  47. normally takes time and money.
  48. In 2013, Aadhaar offered the eKYC,
  49. Electronic Know Your Customer Service
  50. that allowed residents to instantaneously
  51. send their proof of identity and address
  52. to their providers, like a bank
  53. and making the KYC process much simpler.
  54. The Aadhaar initiative was quite incredible
  55. in terms of numbers.
  56. Three years after launch,
  57. 200 million people were enrolled in the system.
  58. Less than a decade after launch,
  59. pretty much the whole adult population,
  60. 1.2 billion people in India
  61. is registered on Aadhaar.
  62. In terms of application in finance,
  63. 400 million people have now
  64. linked their bank accounts to Aadhaar.
  65. Paytm, the largest mobile payments company in India
  66. with almost 300 million clients,
  67. can scale very quickly
  68. thanks to Aadhaar and eKYC.
  69. And we’ll see in the case study of Digibank,
  70. how Digibank uses Aadhaar in the case
  71. of a digital bank for scalability
  72. What can we learn from
  73. a case study such as Aadhaar?
  74. Aadhaar is an example which is really different
  75. from the other case studies,
  76. such as Revolut or Credit Karma.
  77. However, initiatives like Aadhaar
  78. are critical in the development of FinTech,
  79. because they can greatly accelerate
  80. the development of new financial services.
  81. The first lesson from Aadhaar
  82. is certainly the scale.
  83. It is quite incredible to see
  84. more than a billion people
  85. acquiring a digital identity in less than 10 years.
  86. The roll out of this initiative was very efficient,
  87. and also the role of technology in that process
  88. cannot be underestimated.
  89. And that’s why I don’t really see any reasons
  90. why similar identity infrastructure
  91. cannot be implemented in other countries.
  92. In terms of impact on finance,
  93. it was of course big in the context of India,
  94. where many people
  95. didn’t have any identification at all.
  96. But more generally,
  97. Aadhaar was the first example of eKYC at scale,
  98. which helped to decrease the time and cost
  99. to onboard clients.
  100. Aadhaar might not be perfect as a KYC system,
  101. but it is a very novel way of doing KYC.
  102. In the traditional finance system,
  103. each company does its own checks,
  104. whereas Aadhaar is a centralised checking system.
  105. As finance continues on
  106. becoming more and more digitized,
  107. these discussions about how to do eKYC
  108. will certainly continue.
  109. And last but not least
  110. is of course the social impact
  111. of infrastructure like Aadhaar.
  112. Similarly to Alibaba
  113. that helped millions access finance,
  114. Aadhaar helped many to acquire an identity.
  115. And in itself, it is an amazing feat.
  116. If you were thinking of being an entrepreneur,
  117. the presence, or absence, of infrastructure like Aadhaar
  118. can greatly impact your business model,
  119. so I’d certainly recommend spending some time
  120. looking more into these topics.
  121. Thank you very much for following,
  122. and I hope you enjoyed this part.

6.5 Case Study 4: Credit Karma

  1. Hello and welcome to this module
  2. about Credit Karma.
  3. The reason why I chose Credit Karma
  4. is because it is a very interesting example
  5. of a business model linked to
  6. the monetization of data in finance.
  7. In other words,
  8. this is a financial company
  9. that makes money from data
  10. in the same way
  11. as Facebook or Google for example,
  12. but really in the field of finance.
  13. You have heard about
  14. the changing role of data in finance
  15. in previous modules,
  16. and Credit Karma will help you understand
  17. how it works in practise.
  18. Credit Karma is a US company
  19. that was created in 2007
  20. by Kenneth Lin, Nichole Mustard and Ryan Graciano.
  21. They started with the vision
  22. that credit and financial data should be free.
  23. What does it mean in practise?
  24. In the US, any lending decision
  25. is linked to the credit score of the consumers.
  26. For example, if I wanted to borrow for a house
  27. or for a car,
  28. my bank would first check my credit score.
  29. Until Credit Karma started,
  30. consumers could access their credit score,
  31. but they had to pay for it.
  32. Credit Karma’s model
  33. was to offer credit scores
  34. to their users but for free.
  35. In exchange of this free service,
  36. Credit Karma sends targeted advertising.
  37. For example, Credit Karma,
  38. having the information about its users,
  39. could suggest a credit card with a lower rate,
  40. and Credit Karma would be paid a fee
  41. if the users take that credit card.
  42. As they grew,
  43. they started to offer more and more products.
  44. They started to offer free credit scores,
  45. but then credit monitoring,
  46. recommendations for credit card,
  47. free tax filings,
  48. recommendation for loans,
  49. and identity monitoring for example.
  50. In other words,
  51. as they got an increasing amount
  52. of data about their users,
  53. they could offer
  54. more and more free financial advice,
  55. and make money
  56. when there’s a transaction at the end.
  57. As a company, the growth of Credit Karma
  58. has been quite amazing
  59. during the last few years.
  60. Today they have 75 million users,
  61. including a third of all millennials
  62. and a third of all Americans
  63. with a credit profile.
  64. In 2016, they made more than $500 million in revenue
  65. and they were rumoured to be profitable.
  66. They have also raised around $400 million
  67. and at their last round of financing,
  68. they were valued at $3.5 billion.
  69. In terms of international expansion,
  70. they have now also expanded into Canada.
  71. So, what can we learn from Credit Karma?
  72. We hear a lot about the role of data in finance,
  73. but what does it really mean?
  74. Credit Karma is an interesting example,
  75. because from a conceptual standpoint,
  76. it is very similar to
  77. what Google for example would do
  78. if they wanted to monetize data in finance:
  79. offer a product for free,
  80. and in exchange push targeted advertising.
  81. And so perhaps it’s not a coincidence
  82. that Google is actually
  83. an investor in Credit Karma.
  84. Credit Karma is also an interesting illustration
  85. of scalability in finance.
  86. Normally in finance
  87. it used to take quite a long time
  88. for financial companies to grow,
  89. but here we have an example of a company
  90. which managed to get 25% of the US adult population
  91. to use its products in less than 10 years.
  92. Again, that’s a blueprint that we normally see
  93. in tech companies rather than in finance.
  94. The importance of data in finance
  95. I think cannot be underestimated,
  96. and I suspect that most business models in finance
  97. will rely very heavily on data in the next decade.
  98. We are in a transition phase at the moment,
  99. where new models are emerging,
  100. and at the same time
  101. old models are trying to adapt.
  102. If you have the time,
  103. try to find companies
  104. that are successful in using data in finance.
  105. Thank you very much for following this module
  106. and I hope that you won’t forget
  107. that data will be big in finance.

6.6 Case Study 5: Digibank

  1. Welcome back.
  2. In this module,
  3. we will be talking about Digibank,
  4. the digital bank of DBS.
  5. Digibank is an interesting case study
  6. for many reasons.
  7. First, when we talk about FinTech,
  8. we tend to think of startups
  9. like Revolut or Credit Karma,
  10. or e-Commerce companies like Alibaba,
  11. but traditional banks and insurers
  12. are also very active in FinTech.
  13. Secondly, DBS, the Singapore bank,
  14. is considered as
  15. one of the most innovative banks,
  16. and it’s therefore good to understand
  17. how they are implementing innovation
  18. here in this stage
  19. by launching a separate digital bank.
  20. As you learn about Digibank,
  21. link it back to some of the modules
  22. that you have already watched,
  23. for example, Mobile Money,
  24. Opportunities in Emerging Markets
  25. as well as, Payments.
  26. So Digibank was launched
  27. by DBS in India in April 2016.
  28. The value proposition of Digibank was to be
  29. a paperless, signature-less and branchless bank.
  30. Digibank was launched as a mobile only bank,
  31. and with the objective of
  32. offering a different user experience
  33. by using Artificial Intelligence
  34. and Natural Language Processing, for example.
  35. So instead of calling a call centre,
  36. customers could have access to
  37. an AI virtual assistant
  38. to help them find transactions
  39. or do their budgeting, for example.
  40. DBS even acquired Kassisto,
  41. a US company specialised in AI for banking,
  42. to drive all these capabilities for the platform.
  43. One of the big focus of Digibank
  44. was the mobile experience,
  45. and they actively sought the customers’ feedback
  46. in their product development process
  47. to make the User Interface intuitive.
  48. In practise, customers for example,
  49. could open a Digibank account
  50. with no need for a paper or signature.
  51. They just need their Aadhaar card
  52. and their fingerprints,
  53. and in 90 seconds,
  54. can open a Digibank e-wallet.
  55. This leverage is very heavily on
  56. the Aadhaar digital ID infrastructure,
  57. which we have discussed in another module.
  58. Digibank offers
  59. a very standard suite of financial products,
  60. for example, consumers can open an e-wallet,
  61. a savings account,
  62. use a debit card to pay merchants
  63. and withdraw money.
  64. From the Digibank app,
  65. they can also make mobile payments,
  66. or invest in mutual funds.
  67. When was the results?
  68. 15 months after its launch in India,
  69. Digibank started also in Indonesia,
  70. with a very similar product offering.
  71. Digibank used the same strategy for onboarding,
  72. and leveraged on e-KTP,
  73. the Indonesian biometric ID system.
  74. What can we learn
  75. from a company such as Digibank?
  76. We tend to think of Challenger Banks
  77. as new bank startups
  78. competing with incumbent banks.
  79. Digibank is in practise a Challenger Bank,
  80. but launched by a very large bank.
  81. There are different types of innovation models
  82. for large organisations,
  83. and one of them is to launch projects
  84. that are totally independent
  85. both in terms of technology and management.
  86. Digibank is a very good example of such models,
  87. and has been quite successful,
  88. for example,
  89. today they have more than 1.5 million plans,
  90. and so it’s likely to be replicated by others.
  91. The case study also shows
  92. the importance of leveraging
  93. on existing infrastructure for FinTech projects,
  94. and in this case on the Aadhaar ID system,
  95. is a big driver
  96. for the user experience of Digibank.
  97. And finally,
  98. Digibank is an interesting example
  99. of strategic decisions in large organisations.
  100. Many projects in large organisations fail,
  101. not because of technology
  102. or product issues,
  103. but because of internal politics
  104. typically when new projects
  105. cannibalise existing products,
  106. and there is a resistance
  107. from internal departments.
  108. From a strategic standpoint,
  109. Digibank was launched in India,
  110. where DBS didn’t have a significant presence,
  111. and therefore
  112. there were no issues of cannibalization.
  113. Now that you have learnt about Digibank,
  114. have a look at similar models
  115. of Challenger Banks around the world,
  116. and see if some of them
  117. have a strategy which is similar to Digibank.
  118. Thanks for following this module,
  119. and I hope you liked it.

6.7 Conclusion to Case Studies

  1. Let me now conclude on what we learnt
  2. from the case studies of
  3. Aadhaar, Alibaba, Credit Karma,
  4. Digibank and Revolut.
  5. First, I think we learned
  6. that FinTech has a much broader definition
  7. than FinTech startups,
  8. and it includes of course startups,
  9. but also large organisations,
  10. from bank to technology companies.
  11. Secondly, it is truly global today,
  12. and our case studies brought us
  13. from the US to Europe and Asia
  14. but with very different development models
  15. in each region.
  16. For example, we looked at
  17. unbundling and re-bundling with Revolut,
  18. we discussed about data monetization
  19. with Credit Karma.
  20. Alibaba is about
  21. building a diversified financial institution from scratch,
  22. while Aadhaar is about
  23. creating an infrastructure for a billion people.
  24. And Digibank shows that
  25. we shouldn’t forget the role of traditional banks.
  26. Overall, we see fascinating examples of
  27. innovation around the world,
  28. new business model being created,
  29. and very fast growth
  30. from those who understand finance and technology.
  31. Whether you are a student
  32. who will enter the workforce,
  33. an experienced professional
  34. who wants to learn new skills,
  35. or an entrepreneur
  36. who wants to build the next Alibaba,
  37. the opportunities in FinTech are limitless.
  38. I would therefore like to congratulate you
  39. for having taken this first step,
  40. and encourage you to continue your journey.
  41. It was a real pleasure to be with you.
  42. Thank you very much
  43. for following these cases studies,
  44. and I wish you good luck.

6.8 FinTech Big Trends – Looking Forward

  1. Hope the last couple sessions have been quite exciting
  2. and learning about some of the big trends going on
  3. in the broader FinTech space.
  4. But actually, what is coming ahead?
  5. What are some of the big trends
  6. that we must be watching?
  7. Like I always say,
  8. whoever tells they’re a FinTech expert,
  9. you have to run away.
  10. It’s actually very difficult now to predict
  11. what’s going to happen in the broader FinTech space.
  12. But there are some big trends
  13. that we can actually look at and analyse.
  14. For example, one big trend is the rise of TechFin.
  15. As you know, startups have been really changing a lot
  16. of the financial landscape
  17. of some of the big, big, big game changers
  18. actually could come from the large technology firms.
  19. Think about it.
  20. WeChat, the messaging app produced by Tencent
  21. has more than one billion users globally.
  22. WeChat Pay, which is the payment tool
  23. in there has more than 800 million users
  24. over and across 25 different countries.
  25. So the reach of these platforms
  26. is actually quite incredible.
  27. And some of these technology platforms
  28. may really change financial services as we know it.
  29. A second big trend is the rise of voice
  30. as a user interface.
  31. Over the last couple of years,
  32. every financial institution
  33. was focused on delivering, being mobile first
  34. and delivering financial services to your smartphone.
  35. But what we are seeing right now
  36. is the rise of voice as a user interface,
  37. especially with new tools
  38. like Google Home or Amazon’s Echo.
  39. They are basically voice-enabled intelligent systems.
  40. And actually it’s going to be very interesting
  41. to see over the next coming months and years
  42. how financial institutions are going to start delivering
  43. financial services using voice as a medium.
  44. A third big trend we are seeing
  45. is how data is being used
  46. and how our perception of data is changing as well.
  47. Many are calling data the new oil or the new gold.
  48. And actually there’s a number of initiatives
  49. of how individuals can actually monetize their data
  50. and also have better control over it.
  51. Today when you’re using tools like Facebook or Google,
  52. actually, the services are free
  53. because they’re actually giving away a lot of your data.
  54. And there’s a number of people now exploring how,
  55. can actually individuals start monetizing where I can,
  56. for example, let an organisation access my data,
  57. but obviously, being compensated for that access.
  58. A big trend, the fourth one,
  59. is actually artificial intelligence and the rise of AI.
  60. As we discussed in this course,
  61. AI is actually here to stay
  62. and it’s really changing many facets
  63. of the financial services ecosystem.
  64. But it’s actually more than that.
  65. It’s also changing the nature of the forces,
  66. of the workforce, and how we live our lives.
  67. And it’s going to be very interesting over coming years
  68. to see some of the legal questions that will arise.
  69. For example, if, right now, I’m supervising 10 individuals,
  70. but I can replace those 10 individuals with 10 Chatbots,
  71. what if there’s a mistake that’s being committed?
  72. What if one of the Chatbots does something wrong?
  73. Who’s responsible?
  74. It might be me, as a supervisor.
  75. Is it the Chatbot manufacturer?
  76. Is it the user?
  77. Or is it the organisation that employs the Chatbots?
  78. So there’s many many actual issues, legal issues,
  79. that will come up that have not been addressed yet,
  80. and that will be quite challenging.
  81. And maybe a fifth and big trend,
  82. is really the rise of cryptocurrencies.
  83. We discussed in this course how ICOs, cryptocurrencies
  84. and crypto-assets have become part of, will become
  85. even more part of our everyday lives moving forward.
  86. And it’s going to be interesting,
  87. not only the impact these new innovations
  88. will have on the financial services ecosystem.
  89. But potentially, how it can actually enable us to solve
  90. some of the longstanding problems
  91. we’ve had for many years.
  92. For example, financial inclusion,
  93. where we can actually try
  94. to finally bring more people around the world
  95. inside the financial services ecosystem
  96. and being able to bank the unbanked.
  97. Thank you very much,
  98. and it was a real honour sharing with you my passion
  99. and actually our interest when it comes to FinTech,
  100. and we look forward to seeing you again,
  101. either in person or virtually somewhere in the world.

Industry Showcase: the Next Big Opportunities in FinTech (Hon Charles Mok)

  1. I think coming on that, the tech side,

Research and Development (R&D) and Interactions with Industries Part 1 (Charles Mok)

  1. From the standpoint of universities,
  2. we perhaps haven’t been doing
  3. as much as we should be
  4. on research and interactions with industry.

  12. I think what you said summarised some of the

  15. transformed from a manufacturing economy
  16. to a service economy, particularly,

  19. up to today obviously makes Hong Kong
  20. a financial services centre,
  21. one of the top ones in Hong Kong,

Research and Development (R&D) and Interactions with Industries Part 2 (Charles Mok)

  1. From the standpoint of bigger picture,
  2. also it’s about thinking about
  3. the sorts of transformations
  4. that it’s going to have on our lives, on our professions,
  5. on the ways that we do things.
  6. This is one where I think that
  7. there is much greater scope
  8. for thinking about potential implications
  9. in terms of the legal profession, the medical profession,
  10. every profession and how we can use technology
  11. to achieve better outcomes.
  12. This idea of how can we use technology better
  13. for regulatory purposes, the idea of RegTech.
  14. We see it in autonomous vehicles,
  15. it’s a necessity in financial services,
  16. where are other areas can we actually think
  17. about using technology
  18. to better achieve social objectives?
  19. Well there are many.
  20. The whole idea is very simple, in a way.
  21. Data being available,
  22. we can learn from this data, but today,
  23. because, number one, machines can do all these
  24. number crunching much faster
  25. and better than human beings,
  26. and we have the ability to collect and store
  27. all this data.
  28. Then all of a sudden, we can use this data to
  29. not just prove or even predict many of the new trends
  30. of things that we didn’t know before.
  31. There are many of these areas of applications
  32. that would be actually very straightforward –
  33. healthcare is a good example.
  34. There are already quite a lot of evidence research
  35. showing that actually, particularly for,
  36. let’s say, radiology, and many of the different areas of,
  37. in the traditional sense,
  38. you have very good doctors and professors,
  39. medical professors, looking at a lot of data
  40. or images and try to decide what the
  41. diagnosis is going to be
  42. and actually the computer can totally outperform them
  43. already in many documented, evidenced research.
  44. So the thing is, in the future,
  45. how do we work that into the traditional, the existing
  46. regime, or the existing ways of doing things
  47. in those industries,
  48. including this data healthcare industry.
  49. How do we allow those decisions to be made?
  50. Will the professionals in those industries,
  51. let’s say in this particular case, the doctors,
  52. be very resistant and defensive
  53. because they think, “Oh I’m going to lose my job.”
  54. Or actually, they might figure out a way
  55. to coexist with the technology –
  56. use the technology for better serving the customers
  57. or the patients, right?
  58. So these are all very interesting future development,
  59. but actually, I still see that in the current setting,
  60. at this stage, we’re still struggling with
  61. a lot of the regulatory issues, that may be hindering
  62. some of these developments.
  63. Actually it could apply in some cases
  64. in financial services as well.
  65. How do we collect this data and analyse them
  66. in a let’s say anonymous way,
  67. that will satisfy public concerns
  68. about privacy and security,
  69. as well as regulatory requirements
  70. on privacy and security and so on.
  71. How do we balance that?
  72. Sometimes, some of these regulations
  73. are not entirely clear
  74. or drafted for the purpose of accommodating
  75. the use of machine learning or artificial intelligence.
  76. Some organisations might tend to be
  77. overly conservative,
  78. but how do we balance that?
  79. I don’t believe, on the other hand,
  80. that we should just do it without any regulation,
  81. or do it totally unregulated and lead to potential risk.
  82. But the big question is,
  83. I think we’re still figuring out how to balance.
  84. All I can say is that I hope that there will be more
  85. open-minded consideration
  86. and discussion of these areas.
  87. And try to move some of these regulatory
  88. and industry adoption initiatives faster,
  89. but at the same time,
  90. putting out these concerns and issues out to the public,
  91. to get to public understanding and support.
  92. That process is, of course, important,
  93. but I think it’s also only just being developed.
  94. I think it’s going to be very important.

  17. I think when we look forward,
  18. we can say that over the next year,
  19. a number of issues are going to dominate.
  20. This process of digitization and datafication
  21. has meant that today cybersecurity and tech risk
  22. are amongst the biggest not only financial risks,
  23. but also national security risks.
  24. Likewise, we are seeing the evolution of large TechFins.
  25. The entry of firms from the technology sector
  26. transforming the world of finance.
  27. Likewise as a result of this,
  28. we are seeing increasing issues about data security,
  29. data protection and certainly as we see the rollout,
  30. the impact of GDPR, of MiFID II, of PSD2 in Europe
  31. and also related initiatives elsewhere,
  32. these are said to further set the stage
  33. for more FinTech transformation.
  34. Now looking forward,
  35. we’ve reached the end of this
  36. first 6-week instructor-run course.
  37. And I know that many of you all along the way
  38. have been wondering why can’t I do it all once?
  39. I know that a lot of you like to see
  40. how quickly you can get through these courses.
  41. And I’d like to say that starting from 15th of July
  42. the course will be open.
  43. You will be able to do it as quickly as you want.
  44. And this is also important for those of you
  45. who are enrolled in the current course.
  46. The course ends but you’re rolled over
  47. from the 14th of July to the 15th of July,
  48. so that you actually have until the middle of May,
  49. the 14th of May 2019 to actually get through the course.
  50. And so, those of you
  51. who thought you were running out of time,
  52. you’re not out of time yet,
  53. you have until next May.
  54. And for those of you who finished
  55. and for those of you who are still progressing,
  56. over the next year, we’re going to be doing
  57. monthly updates on current issues.
  58. Likewise we are going to be rolling out
  59. periodic new industry insights
  60. from startups, tech companies, financial institutions,
  61. policymakers, regulators around the world.
  62. So we’ve very much enjoyed having you with us
  63. for the past six weeks,
  64. but we are very much looking forward to
  65. seeing you with us again over the coming year.

Looking Back, Looking Forward

Looking Back, Looking Forward

“Looking Back, Looking Forward” is a monthly segment launched in Jan 2020.

Each month, Professor Douglas Arner will discuss issues across the FinTech world – big and small themes, research topics and other topics of interest. Stay tuned for more.

Looking Back, Looking Forward Episode 1 (Jan 2020)

  1. Looking Back, Looking Forward.
  2. January 1st, 2020 marks the start of a new year
  3. and a new decade,
  4. both for us, but also for FinTech and RegTech
  5. and the launch of a new segment
  6. in our online course which we’re going to call
  7. “Looking Back, Looking Forward”.
  8. And in that segment each month,
  9. we’ll be looking at issues across the FinTech world.
  10. Big themes, small themes, research topics,
  11. whatever that month comes to us that is of interest.
  12. And if I look at the past decade,
  13. if I look at the 2010s, what were the themes
  14. that characterised finance?
  15. First, it was the global financial crisis of 2008.
  16. In many ways, the 2010s were an in-between decade.
  17. In between the 2000s and the global financial crisis
  18. and this decade, the 2020s
  19. and much of what happened in finance
  20. was characterised by the regulatory reforms coming
  21. after the global financial crisis.
  22. reactions and impacts of the global financial crisis.
  23. That’s one of the big themes of the 2010s for finance.
  24. The other big theme
  25. of the 2010s for finance was technology.
  26. The impact of a range of new technologies.
  27. Digitization, datafication, blockchain,
  28. AI and a range of others
  29. which over the past decade
  30. has transformed finance around the world.
  31. And it was really the combination of these two,
  32. the global financial crisis,
  33. particularly as it impacted regulation
  34. and technology that underlied the advent
  35. of FinTech and RegTech.
  36. Now, if we look forward,
  37. if we look froward to the 2020s,
  38. what are the big themes that we see lying ahead?
  39. Well, the first is sustainability.
  40. The fact that as the world develops,
  41. there’s going to be ever more focus on equality,
  42. on climate change, on biodiversity,
  43. on development and how can we build a world
  44. that is better and sustainable for not only all of us
  45. but future generations as well?
  46. The second theme that we see very much
  47. in the context of finance is technology once again.
  48. Technology continues to move at a very rapid phase
  49. and in particular when we combine technology
  50. with sustainability, this is where we get
  51. some of the most exciting possibilities
  52. for finance going forward
  53. and particularly for FinTech.
  54. It’s about new risks,
  55. it’s about deploying new resources
  56. through financial inclusion
  57. and it’s about building
  58. better financial systems through technology.
  59. Risks and opportunities from both sustainability
  60. as well as technology and also
  61. we think that the 2020s are going to be
  62. heavily characterised by challenges,
  63. conflicts between globalisation, fragmentation,
  64. traditional powers, rising powers
  65. and this will have a big impact particularly
  66. in the context of data flows and competition.
  67. The end result is that we expect the 2020s
  68. to be if anything, an even more transformational decade
  69. for finance and financial technology than the 2010s
  70. and we look very much forward to
  71. going through it with you.

Looking Back, Looking Forward Episode 2 (Feb 2020)

  1. Artificial intelligence is likely to be one
  2. of the major issues of the 2020s.
  3. Certainly when we think about some
  4. of the big themes that are being written
  5. around the world, the potential opportunities
  6. of AI and transforming almost everything generate
  7. huge amounts of excitement
  8. but at the same time, lots of considerations
  9. about the potential risks of AI,
  10. from simple automation and job losses,
  11. to replacement or even superseding of human beings
  12. by independent higher intelligences
  13. and an increasing amount of attention,
  14. not surprisingly is being focused around the world
  15. on how do we manage AI?
  16. How do we govern AI so that we secure the benefits
  17. but we minimise the chances of the risks
  18. and we use AI going forward to build a better world.
  19. But where relatively less attention has been placed is
  20. in the context of AI in finance,
  21. I think when we look at AI today,
  22. artificial intelligence has been through
  23. a number of periods over the past 50, 60 years
  24. where it has received attention,
  25. but today a unique confluence of factors
  26. mean that we are seeing more rapid development
  27. than we have ever seen before,
  28. and these are really the result of a twin process
  29. of digitization and datafication.
  30. What the world economic forum Klaus Schwab
  31. calls the fourth industrial revolution
  32. characterised by the digitization of everything.
  33. AI rests on data and in particular, digitised data,
  34. and today the world is creating more data every day
  35. than it ever has before,
  36. and this is a trend that looks set to continue,
  37. but as this data are digitised there is a need for storage
  38. and storage has become ever more available
  39. and ever cheaper.
  40. Combine these with computing power.
  41. We’re all familiar with Moore’s Law
  42. and the increasing availability of computing power,
  43. but combined with digital data storage
  44. and algorithmic theory,
  45. these together lay the groundwork
  46. for transformational development in AI.
  47. AI in finance is actually one of the world’s leaders.
  48. The finance industry is one of the largest spenders
  49. on artificial intelligence.
  50. Why?
  51. Because in finance, there’s a constant arms race,
  52. particularly amongst financial traders and investors
  53. who are looking for an edge in global markets
  54. to the extent that today,
  55. the vast majority of trading in global securities markets
  56. takes place not between human beings,
  57. but between computers,
  58. computers that interact increasingly in a context of
  59. more and more advanced algorithmic systems
  60. including those using an increasing variety of AI tools.
  61. So, AI in finance is developing very rapidly,
  62. particularly, in the context of global markets
  63. and global trading.
  64. Why?
  65. Well, the financial incentives, the financial resources,
  66. the human resources are all there
  67. to support its development,
  68. but this brings with it many, many challenges.
  69. How is the AI going to be monitored?
  70. What sorts of decisions is it going to make?
  71. Is it going to be discriminative?
  72. Is it going to be a black box
  73. that results in decisions, systems approaches
  74. that cause new systemic risks that no one can explain,
  75. no one can understand.
  76. When we look at finance, not only is it
  77. one of the most digitised industries,
  78. the most globalised industries,
  79. but it is also one of the most regulated industries,
  80. and a real trend that we’ve had
  81. since the 2008 global financial crisis,
  82. as well as the related Libor and forex scandals,
  83. or more recently scandals in finance in Australia,
  84. has been to push for more and more cultural change,
  85. ethical behaviour in the financial sector.
  86. How do we replace the greed is good
  87. of the 1980s and the 2000s
  88. with a much more ethical approach to finance,
  89. and one aspect of that is
  90. a regulatory tool focusing on personal responsibility,
  91. and what we’re seeing is an increasing trend
  92. of regulation to personal responsibility
  93. of individual managers, individual directors
  94. for areas within their supervision and control
  95. in regulated financial institutions.
  96. The reality is that AI falls into these
  97. in the same way as other activities,
  98. in the same way as a third-party contractor,
  99. in the same way as an employee, a computer system,
  100. a third-party, whether independent or not,
  101. should fall under the responsibility
  102. of a single human, a manager in charge,
  103. a supervisory responsibility system,
  104. and we describe this as human in the loop
  105. in the context of AI in the financial sector,
  106. and from the standpoint of the financial sector,
  107. this means that finance is in a good position,
  108. relatively speaking so long as those humans realise
  109. that they are responsible for the computer systems
  110. in their areas of responsibility.
  111. This drives systems of due diligence.
  112. It also drives systems of explainability,
  113. and we think that not only does this have huge potential
  114. in the context of AI in finance,
  115. but similar sorts of systems could very well be used
  116. in the context of AI in any regulated industry.
  117.  

Unit

  

Looking Back, Looking Forward (FEB 2020)

Academic Paper 

Artificial Intelligence in Finance: Putting the Human in the Loop

Written by Dirk A. Zetzsche (University of Luxembourg), Douglas Arner (University of Hong Kong),

Ross Buckley (University of New South Wales), and Brian W. Tang (University of Hong Kong) 

This paper puts forth a regulatory roadmap for understanding and addressing the increasing role of AI in finance, focusing on human responsibility and suggesting that an effective path forward involves regulatory approaches in “putting the human in the loop” in enhancing governance and addressing “blackbox” issues.

The new paper can be found here on SSRN: 

https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3531711
